Al-Ash'ari's Clarification of the Vision of God
The poem criticizes denial of vision and praises al-Ash'ari's clarification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ibn al-Khall's poem defends the possibility of seeing God in the afterlife and praises al-Ash'ari's role in clarifying this truth.

Core Idea
Some denied seeing God, but Allah sent al-Ash'ari to clarify the truth.

In the second part of the poem, Ibn al-Khall directly addresses a theological controversy: the denial of the vision of God (ru'ya) by the Mu'tazila. He states that these people affirmed a Lord but claimed He cannot be seen. Then he declares that Allah willed to clarify guidance through a young man from the Ash'ari tribe—Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari—who came from the noble Ansar. Al-Ash'ari expounded the proofs and made the truth victorious.

Guided Path
1
Read the second passage
Read the Arabic lines about those who deny vision and the appearance of al-Ash'ari. Compare with the translation.
Notice the contrast between the people's claim and Allah's will.
2
Identify the theological claim
The passage says: 'أثبتوا رباً ولكن زعموا أنه ممتنع أن يرى' – they affirmed a Lord but claimed He is impossible to see. This is the Mu'tazili position that God cannot be seen in the afterlife.
The vision of God (ru'ya) is a key attribute affirmed by Ahl al-Sunna.
3
Understand al-Ash'ari's role
The poem says Allah wanted to clarify guidance through 'a young man from Ash'ari' who is from the core of the noble Ansar. This refers to Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari, who defended the Sunni position on ru'ya and other attributes.
Al-Ash'ari's lineage is traced to the Ansar, the helpers of Medina.
4
Analyze the outcome
The poem concludes that he clarified the proof until it appeared and made the truth mighty until it became victorious. This signifies the triumph of the Ash'ari school over the Mu'tazili position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
انشدنا الشيخ أبو الحسين بن المبارك بن محمد البغدادي المعروف بابن الخل ببغداد في المدرسة النظامية قصيدة لنفسه مدح بها الشيخ ابا الفتوح محمد بن الفضل بن محمد الاسفرائنی رحمة الله عليه وذكر منها قوله: أزري [؟] منهم من المعتضد الناس فلم يك للمظلوم إلا وزرا وتلاه المكتفى بالله عن كل شيء يقدم المقتدرا واستشاط الناس في عصريهما بخلاف عم حتى اشتهرا هو الله ومن لم يقل ذاك أحال القدرا أثبتوا رباً ولكن زعموا أنه ممتنع أن يرى وأراد الله إيضاح الهدى حين زاغوا بفتى من أشعرا في صميم النجب الأنصار من خير من يوم حنين نصرا أوضح الحجة حتى ظهرت وأعز الحق حتى استظهرا
The shaykh Abu al-Husayn ibn al-Mubarak ibn Muhammad al-Baghdadi, known as Ibn al-Khall, recited for us in Baghdad in the Nizamiyya school a poem of his in which he praised shaykh Abu al-Futuh Muhammad ibn al-Fadl ibn Muhammad al-Isfarayini, may God have mercy on him, and mentioned from it his saying: 'Disgrace [?] from them of al-Mu'tadid the people, so he was not for the oppressed but a supporter. And he was followed by al-Muktafi bi'llah, who over everything presents al-Muqtadir. And the people became enraged in their two eras due to differing in what they were known for [?] is God, and whoever does not say that attributes fate. They affirmed a Lord but claimed that He is impossible to see. And God wanted to clarify guidance when they deviated, through a young man from Ash'ari, from the core of the noble Ansar, from the best of those who gave victory on the day of Hunayn. He clarified the proof until it appeared and made the truth mighty until it became victorious.'
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This passage records a poem by Ibn al-Khall praising al-Ash'ari, though the poem is nominally in praise of al-Isfarayini. The poem first mentions two Abbasid caliphs, al-Mu'tadid and al-Muktafi, and their support of justice. It then criticizes those who make a claim about God that attributes fate (perhaps denial of divine decree) and notes that they affirmed a Lord but denied that He can be seen. This is a reference to the Mu'tazila who deny the vision of God in the afterlife. The poem says God wanted to clarify guidance through 'a young man from Ash'ari', meaning Abul Hasan al-Ash'ari, who is described as from the Ansar (the helpers, referring to his lineage as a descendant of the Ansar from the tribe of Ash'ari). The poem says he clarified the proof and made truth victorious.

Key Terms
الأشعري
الرؤية
الأنصار
أشعرا

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God cannot be seen even in the afterlife.
Correction: Ahl al-Sunna affirm that believers will see God in Paradise, based on Quran and hadith.

Checkpoint
What claim did some people make about God according to the poem?
A) That God has a son
B) That God is impossible to see
C) That God is unjust
D) That God does not decree

Key Takeaway
Ibn al-Khall's poem defends the Sunni belief in the vision of God and credits al-Ash'ari with clarifying this truth.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly long to see Allah in the afterlife as the ultimate reward, or has this belief become a mere theological statement that does not stir your heart? If it is real, how should it change the way you live today—starting with your next prayer?
?
Are there any attributes of Allah that you struggle to accept emotionally or intellectually, even while you profess belief in them? Will you commit to seeking out the clear proofs that scholars like al-Ash'ari have provided, rather than relying on inherited opinions or your own limited reasoning?
?
The poem criticizes those who affirmed a Lord but denied His vision. Is there a gap between your verbal affirmation of faith and your practical surrender to Allah's attributes? What specific step will you take today to close that gap?

The Poem Praising al-Ash'ari and Warning his Detractors
A short panegyric poem defining al-Ash'ari's school and defending his followers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how this poem defends al-Ash'ari's theology and warns against hostility to his followers.

Core Idea
The poem summarizes Ash'ari as the middle path between negation and anthropomorphism.

This short poem encapsulates the Ash'ari self-image: the school is the way of pure monotheism and transcendence, distinct from both extreme negation and anthropomorphism. The poet also fiercely defends al-Ash'ari's companions, labeling their opponents as foolish or insane. Let's examine the verses.

Guided Path
1
Read the poem and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
2
Identify the two theological contrasts
Notice how the poem contrasts tawhid/tanzih with nafy/tashbih. This defines Ash'ari as the balanced middle path.
The contrast is between correct Islamic theology and two extremes.
3
Understand the praise of al-Ash'ari
The poet describes al-Ash'ari as having no peer, being a scholar, imam, knowledgeable jurist, and his speech free from deception.
4
Examine the warning against opponents
Note the strong language: those who hate his companions are foolish, and those who consider them misguided are insane. This is a polemical defense.
This shows the high regard for al-Ash'ari's followers.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وانشدت لبعض أهل التحقيق في مديحه رحمه الله: الأشعري ماله شبیه حبر إمام عالم فقيه مذهبه التوحيد والتنزيه وما عداه النفي والتشبيه وليس فيما قاله تمويه وصحبه كلهم نبيه في قوله على الهدى تنبيه ما فيهم إلا امرؤ وجيه فمن قلى أصحابه سفيه ومن رأى تضليلهم معتوه
And some of the people of verification recited in praise of him, may God have mercy on him: Al-Ash'ari has no likeness; he is a scholar, an imam, a knowledgeable jurist. His school is tawhid and tanzih, and what is other than it is negation and anthropomorphism. There is no deception in what he said. All his companions are noble. In his statement there is an indication of guidance. There is none among them except a dignified man. So whoever hates his companions is foolish, and whoever considers them misguided is insane.
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This passage is a short anonymous poem in praise of al-Ash'ari. It begins by stating that al-Ash'ari has no peer, describing him as a scholar (hibr), imam, knowledgeable, and jurist. It defines his school as tawhid (monotheism) and tanzih (transcendence), contrasting it with other positions which are either nafy (negation, i.e., ta'til) or tashbih (anthropomorphism). This succinctly places Ash'ari theology as the middle path. The poem then declares his statement free from deception and his companions all noble, each one dignified. Finally, it makes a strong warning: whoever hates his companions is foolish (safih), and whoever considers them misguided is insane (ma'tuh). This serves as a polemical defense of the Ash'ari school against its critics.

Key Terms
التوحيد
التنزيه
النفي
التشبيه
الأشعري
السفيه
المعتوه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The poem attacks all Muslims who disagree with al-Ash'ari.
Correction: The poem specifically targets those who hate or declare misguided al-Ash'ari's companions, not all dissenters.

Checkpoint
According to the poem, what is the consequence for someone who considers al-Ash'ari's companions misguided?
A) They are a sinner
B) They are a disbeliever
C) They are insane
D) They are ignorant

Key Takeaway
The poem affirms al-Ash'ari's status as the paragon of Sunni theology and warns that enmity towards his followers is a sign of foolishness or insanity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Who are the scholars in your life whom you are tempted to dismiss or belittle? In light of this poem's warning that hating the followers of truth is foolishness and insanity, where do you need to repent from arrogance toward the scholars of Ahl al-Sunnah?
?
The poem describes al-Ash'ari's companions as noble and dignified. Do you view the scholars of Sunni Islam with respect and honor, or do you allow sectarian bias to cloud your heart? What would it take for you to truly honor them?
?
The poem says there is no deception (tamwih) in al-Ash'ari's words. Are there areas of your faith where you have accepted deception—whether from yourself or others—instead of the clarity of tawhid and tanzih? What step will you take today to confront that deception with truth?

Poem Praising al-Ash'ari's Clarification of Truth
Ibn al-Khall recites a poem praising al-Ash'ari for clarifying tawhid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ibn al-Khall's poem praises al-Ash'ari's role in clarifying the truth of tawhid and the doctrine of seeing Allah.

Core Idea
A poem defends Ash'ari theology by praising al-Ash'ari as the clarifier of truth.

This lesson presents a poem by Ibn al-Khall, recited in praise of al-Ash'ari and his method. The poem contrasts theological confusion with the clarity brought by al-Ash'ari, particularly on the issue of seeing Allah. Through its verses, we see how Ash'ari scholars used praise to reinforce their doctrinal positions.

Guided Path
1
Read the poem and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation, to get an overall sense of its content and structure.
The poem contains some historical references and a clear theological point.
2
Identify the theological claim
Find the line that addresses the vision of Allah. The poem says 'they claimed that He is impossible to see' and warns that denying vision attributes incapability to divine decree.
3
Notice the praise of al-Ash'ari
The poem praises 'a young man from Ash'ara' (Imam al-Ash'ari) who clarified the proof and strengthened the truth.
This connects the poem's praise to al-Ash'ari's method.
4
Reflect on poetry as theological tool
Consider why the author chose a poem rather than a prose treatise. Poetry can be memorable and persuasive, serving as a form of theological endorsement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
انشدنا الشيخ أبو الحسين بن المبارك بن محمد البغدادي المعروف بابن الخل ببغداد في المدرسة النظامية قصيدة لنفسه مدح بها الشيخ ابا الفتوح محمد بن الفضل بن محمد الاسفراینی رحمة الله عليه وذكر منها قوله : ازرعی؟ منهم من المعتضد الناس فلم يك لــلــمـظـلــوم إلا وزرا وتلاه المكتفى بالله عن كل شيء يقدم المقتدرا واستشاط الناس في عصريهما بخلاف عم حتى اشــتــهـرا ه الله ومن لم يقل ذاك احال القدرا أثبتوا رباً ولكن زعموا أنه ممتنع ان يــــــــــــــــــــرا وأراد الله ايضاح الهدى حين زاغوا بفتى من أشعرا في صميم النجب الأنصار من خير من يوم حنين نصرا اوضح الحجة حتى ظهرت وأعز الحق حتى استظهرا
The shaykh Abu al-Husayn ibn al-Mubarak ibn Muhammad al-Baghdadi, known as Ibn al-Khall, recited to us in Baghdad in the Nizamiyya school a poem of his own composition in which he praised the shaykh Abu al-Futuh Muhammad ibn al-Fadl ibn Muhammad al-Isfara'ini, may God have mercy on him, and he mentioned from it his saying: 'Azra'i? Among them, al-Mu'tadid [the people?] — for he was not for the oppressed except a refuge. And al-Muktafi bi-Allah followed him, who precedes al-Muqtadir in everything. And the people became inflamed in their two eras due to widespread disagreement until it became famous. ... God, and whoever does not say that has attributed incapability to divine decree. They affirmed a Lord, but they claimed that He is impossible to see. And God wanted to clarify guidance when they deviated, through a young man from Ash'ara. In the core of the noble Ansar, from the best of those who gave victory on the day of Hunayn. He clarified the proof until it appeared, and he strengthened the truth until it was manifest.'
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This passage is a poetic eulogy recited by Ibn al-Khall in praise of both the addressee (al-Isfara'ini) and indirectly Imam al-Ash'ari. The poem describes historical figures (al-Mu'tadid, al-Muktafi, al-Muqtadir) and contrasts them with those who deviate regarding divine vision ('they claimed that He is impossible to see'), affirming the Ash'ari stance that God will be seen by the believers in the afterlife. It then praises a 'young man from Ash'ara' (Imam al-Ash'ari) who clarified the proof and strengthened the truth. The poem follows a narrative of confusion and disagreement, then resolution through al-Ash'ari's efforts. The language is panegyric and apologetic, defending Ash'ari theology.

Key Terms
أشعرا
ايضاح الهدى
الحجة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The poem is only a historical praise with no theological content.
Correction: The poem explicitly defends the Ash'ari position on seeing Allah and praises al-Ash'ari's clarification of truth.

Checkpoint
What does the poem claim about those who deny the vision of God?
A) They are believers
B) They have attributed incapability to divine decree
C) They are from the Ansar
D) They are from Ash'ara

Key Takeaway
Ibn al-Khall's poem celebrates al-Ash'ari as the one who clarified the truth, especially the doctrine that Allah will be seen by believers.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly yearn to see Allah in the afterlife, or is this just a theological belief you affirm without emotional weight? What concrete step will you take today to increase your longing for His vision?
?
The poem warns that denying the vision of Allah attributes incapability to Him. In what areas of your life do your actions implicitly doubt that Allah is capable of guiding or helping you? How will you repent from that hidden doubt this week?
?
Al-Ash'ari clarified the truth when people deviated. What truth about Allah do you already know but fail to live by? What one action will you take today to align your life with that truth?

Ash'ari's Method: Certainty and Inference, Beyond Tashbih and I'tizal
Poem praising al-Ash'ari's method and refuting tashbih and i'tizal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the poem defends al-Ash'ari's methodology by identifying it as a middle path between tashbih and i'tizal, and based on certainty and inference.

Core Idea
Al-Ash'ari's proof is our proof: reasoned certainty without tashbih or i'tizal.

This poem by Ibn al-Khall is more than praise—it is a doctrinal manifesto. It aligns the Ash'ari method with true tawhid, defining it as 'nazar bil-yaqin wa-istidlal' (consideration by certainty and inference). The poet explicitly distances al-Ash'ari from two extremes: anthropomorphism (tashbih) and rationalist deviation (i'tizal). As you read, notice how the poem uses these contrasts to assert orthodoxy.

Guided Path
1
Read the poem carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the poet's praise and contrasts come through.
Some words are unclear in the original, but the main doctrinal points are clear.
2
Identify the core claim
The poem begins by stating 'The proof of al-Ash'ari is our proof'. This establishes allegiance and sets the tone for what follows.
Notice the possessive 'our'—the speaker aligns himself with al-Ash'ari's school.
3
Understand the method described
The poem describes al-Ash'ari's method as 'nazar bil-yaqin wa-istidlal' (consideration by certainty and inference). This is the rational methodology of Ilm al-Kalam.
Contrast this with blind imitation or pure speculation.
4
See the rejection of two deviations
The poem explicitly rejects tashbih (anthropomorphism) and i'tizal (Mu'tazilism). Al-Ash'ari's path is free from both.
This positions Ash'arism as the orthodox middle way.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وانشدنا أيضا الشيخ الأديب أبو الحسين بن الخل من قصيدة لنفسه مدح بها الشيخ الامام ابا المظفر أحمد بن الامام أبي بكر محمد بن أحمد ابن الحسين الشاشي رحمه الله : حجة الأشـعـرى حـجـتـنـا العـلـ يا كما قدره الرفيع العالي البعيد المدي ابي الحسن المح سن في النصح للورى غير آل والذي اصل الأصول بوصفى نظر باليسقين واستدلال لم تشب صفو عقده شبه التش بيه في معزل عن الاعتزال وحد الله مصلنا صارم الحق مطيحا به دم الضلال سد الله أمة قصدته بالشناعات بالوبا والوبال جهلوا قدره فکل سفیه منهم جاهل لما قال قالى
And the literary shaykh Abu al-Husayn ibn al-Khall also recited to us from a poem of his own composition in which he praised the shaykh al-Imam Abu al-Muzaffar Ahmad ibn al-Imam Abi Bakr Muhammad ibn Ahmad ibn al-Husayn al-Shashi, may God have mercy on him: 'The proof of al-Ash'ari is our proof, O ... as his high, lofty, distant extent? Abu al-Hasan ... in sincerity for creation, not [آل?], and he who established the fundamentals by description: consideration by certainty and inference. The purity of his contract was not tainted by the likeness of tashbih, he is isolated from i'tizal. The oneness of God is our guide, the sharp sword of truth, through it the blood of misguidance is shed. May God block the community that attacked him with calumnies with epidemics and destruction. They were ignorant of his worth, so every fool among them is ignorant of what he said.'
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This passage is a second poem by Ibn al-Khall praising Imam al-Ash'ari and also honoring al-Shashi. The poem explicitly states that al-Ash'ari's proof is the speaker's proof. It describes al-Ash'ari's method as 'consideration by certainty and inference' (nazar bil-yaqin wa-istidlal), which is a direct reference to the rational methodology of Ilm al-Kalam. The poem contrasts this with two deviant groups: 'tashbih' (anthropomorphism) and 'i'tizal' (Mu'tazilite rationalism). Al-Ash'ari's doctrine is 'isolated from i'tizal' and free from tashbih. The poem then launches a strong condemnation of those who attack al-Ash'ari, calling them fools. This reflects the sectarian tensions of the time and the defense of Ash'ari orthodoxy.

Key Terms
حجة الأشعري
نظر
يقين
استدلال
تشبيه
اعتزال
سفيه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The poem is just praise and has no doctrinal content.
Correction: The poem explicitly defines Ash'ari methodology and rejects tashbih and i'tizal, making it a doctrinal statement.

Checkpoint
According to the poem, what two deviations is al-Ash'ari's method free from?
A) Tashbih and Ta'til
B) Tashbih and I'tizal
C) I'tizal and Qadar
D) Tashbih and Jabr

Key Takeaway
Al-Ash'ari's methodology is defined by reasoned certainty (yaqin) and inference (istidlal), and it rejects both anthropomorphism and Mu'tazilite rationalism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever unknowingly fallen into describing Allah with human-like attributes, assuming this makes Him more relatable? How will you purify your conception of Allah using the method of nazar bil-yaqin wa-istidlal?
?
The poem condemns ignorance of al-Ash'ari's worth. Are you quick to dismiss or criticize a scholar or a school of thought without first understanding their actual methodology? What step will you take this week to learn the true position of a group you disagree with?
?
Tawhid is called the 'sharp sword of truth' that sheds the blood of misguidance. Does your commitment to tawhid wield such force in your life, or is it just a comfortable belief? What one false belief or practice will you actively oppose today because of what you learned?

The Ash'ari Middle Path in a Poem
A scholar praises al-Ash'ari's doctrine and condemns opponents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how this poem positions the Ash'ari school as the balanced middle path between anthropomorphism and stripping God of attributes.

Core Idea
Ash'ari doctrine: the middle way between tashbih and ta'til.

This brief but powerful poem distills the core of Ash'ari theology into a few lines. It praises Imam al-Ash'ari as a unique scholar and defines his school as the path of tawhid and tanzih. The poem also sharply distinguishes this path from two extremes — negation (ta'til) and anthropomorphism (tashbih) — and defends the Ash'ari community against detractors. Let's read it with a careful eye for its theological claims.

Guided Path
1
Read the poem in Arabic and English
First, read the Arabic lines of the poem along with the literal translation. Notice the poetic rhythm and how each line builds on the previous one.
The poem uses rhyme and meter typical of Arabic praise poetry.
2
Identify the theological positioning
Pay attention to line three: 'madhhabuhu al-tawhid wa al-tanzih wa ma 'adahu al-nafy wa al-tashbih'. What does this tell you about how Ash'ari doctrine is defined?
Key: tawhid and tanzih are affirmed; nafy and tashbih are rejected as extremes.
3
Analyze the condemnation of opponents
The last two lines are strong: 'faman qala ashabahu safih / wa man ra'a tadlilhum ma'tuh'. How should we understand this strong language? What does it reveal about the context?
This reflects the sectarian atmosphere and the need to defend the Ash'ari community.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وانشدت لبعض أهل التحقيق فى مديحه رحمه الله : الأشعرى ماله شبیه حبــر امــام عـالـم فــقــيــه مذهبه التوحيد والتنزيه وما عداه النفى والـتـشـبـيـة وليس فــيــمــا قـالـه تمويه وصحبه كلهم نبيه في قوله على الهدى تنبيه مـافـيـهـم إلا امرؤ وجـيــه فمن قلا أصحابه سـفـيـه ومن رأى تضليلهم معتوه
And it was recited to me by some people of verification in praise of him, may God have mercy on him: 'Al-Ash'ari has no likeness — he is a scholar, an imam, a learned jurist. His school is tawhid and tanzih, and what is other than it is negation and tashbih. There is no obfuscation in what he said, and his companions are all noble. In his speech there is an indication of guidance. There is none among them except a dignified man. So whoever hates his companions is a fool, and whoever considers them misguided is insane.'
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This passage is a short but dense poem praising Imam al-Ash'ari and his followers. It begins by stating that al-Ash'ari is unique, describing him as a scholar (hibr), imam, learned (alim), and jurist (faqih). Then it defines his school (madhhab) as tawhid (affirming divine oneness) and tanzih (declaring God transcendent above anthropomorphic attributes). It contrasts this with two alternatives: 'negation' (al-nafy, i.e., ta'til or stripping God of attributes) and 'tashbih' (anthropomorphism). Thus Ash'ari doctrine is presented as the middle path. The poem then defends al-Ash'ari's speech as clear (no obfuscation), and his companions as noble. It ends with a sharp condemnation: anyone who hates his companions is a fool, and anyone who considers them misguided is insane. This reflects the strong sectarian defense common in Ash'ari circles.

Key Terms
التوحيد
التنزيه
النفى
التشبيه
تمويه
سفيه
معتوه

Ash'aris as the Saved Sect
Two poetic lines asserting Ash'ari orthodoxy and warning against dissent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ash'ari school is presented as the divinely guided group that strictly follows the Quran and Sunna, and the warning against deviating from them.

Core Idea
Ash'aris claim exclusive correctness by adhering to the Quran and Sunna.

This short passage from Ibn 'Asakir's work captures a key Ash'ari self-understanding: that they alone have been granted success in following scripture properly. It also includes a stern warning against those who deviate from their path. Let's examine the text closely.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once without analyzing. Notice the narrative structure: a chain of transmission followed by two poetic statements.
The first line praises the Ash'aris, the second warns against deviation.
2
Identify the two claims
The first claim is that the Ash'aris have been granted success (wuffiqu) in reaching correctness (sawab) and never depart from Quran and Sunna. The second claim is a threat that anyone who deviates from them will face punishment.
Consider the exclusivity: 'deviate from them' implies they are the standard.
3
Contextualize the passage
Remember that Ibn 'Asakir wrote this work to defend the Ash'ari school against accusations. This poetic testimony is meant to show that Ash'aris are the true followers of scripture.
The warning reinforces the idea that opposing Ash'ari belief is a serious sin.
4
Reflect on the tone
Consider whether such an exclusivist claim is appropriate. How does it compare to other Islamic theological claims?
This passage is polemical; it assumes Ash'ari preeminence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أنشدني الشيخ الفقيه الشهيد ابو الحجاج يوسف بن دوناس الفندلاوي رحمه الله فيما أرى لبعضهم بدمشق: الأشـعرية قوم قد وفقوا للصواب لم يخرجوا في اعتقاد عن سنة أو كتاب قال شيخنا ابو محمد القسم انشدنيهما عبدالوهاب بن عيسى اليشكري وزادنی بعدهما: وكل من زاغ عنهم مصيره لعذاب

Our shaykh Abu Muhammad al-Qasim said: 'Abd al-Wahhab ibn Isa al-Yashkuri recited them to me and added after them: 'And whoever deviates from them, his ultimate end is punishment.'
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This passage consists of two short poetic statements. The first, attributed to someone in Damascus, asserts that the Ash'aris (al-Ash'ariyya) are divinely guided to correctness (sawab) and that their beliefs ('itiqad) never depart from the Sunna of the Prophet or the Quran (kitab). This is a standard Ash'ari claim to orthodoxy. The second line, added by al-Yashkuri, threatens anyone who deviates from the Ash'aris with punishment ('adhab). Together, they present the Ash'ari school as the saved sect and warn against dissent. The tone is authoritative and exclusivist, reflecting the polemical context of Ibn 'Asakir's work defending Ash'arism.

Ash'ari Position on Divine Attributes in Poem
A longer poem details Ash'ari rejection of ta'til, tashbih, and affirmation of God's attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari position on divine attributes: they negate any attribute not validated by revelation and affirm only those attested by authentic transmission, while maintaining tanzih (transcendence).

Core Idea
Ash'aris affirm attributes from revelation while rejecting ta'til and tashbih.

This poem from the Ash'ari tradition celebrates the school's balanced approach to divine attributes. It emphasizes that Ash'aris have been guided to rectitude and that they guide others. The poem highlights two key principles: negating from God whatever is not valid in belief (such as anthropomorphism or having rivals), and affirming every attribute that is established by authentic transmission. This passage defends the Ash'ari method as the middle path between stripping God of attributes (ta'til) and attributing to Him human-like qualities (tashbih).

Guided Path
1
Read the poem and translation
Read the Arabic poem once, then the literal translation. Notice the poetic structure and the key doctrinal points.
Some words are uncertain due to OCR, but the overall meaning is clear.
2
Identify the two actions: negation and affirmation
Look for phrases where the poem says 'they negated from Him what is not valid' and 'they affirmed every attribute valid by authentic transmission'. Understand that this is the core of Ash'ari methodology.
3
Note the contrast with ta'til and ilhad
The poem ends by stating that Ash'aris have not left Quran and Sunna except for the people of ta'til (negation of attributes) and ilhad (heresy). This confirms that Ash'aris are the true followers of revelation.
This reinforces the idea that Ash'ari theology is scripturally grounded.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولبعضهم في هذا المعنى على هذا الوزن : الأسد وبي عرية قوم قد وفقوا للسداد نوا للبرايا طرا طريق الرمـ ــــــــاد ونزهـوا الله عما يقول أهل العناد وقدســـــــوه عن المش ــل جـ ل والأنداد ونزهـــــــــــــــوه عن الزو ز والأولاد وهم نفوا عنه مالا يصح في الاعـ ـتقاد وأثبتوا كل وصف يصح بالأس سناد هم بدور الدياجي وهم . داة العباد وهم بـحـــــ ار علوم وهم صدور البلاد وهم كرام السجايا وهم وجـ وه النوادي لم يخرجوا عن كتاب أو سنة في اعتقاد سوا اولی تعطیل ولا ذوى الحـ ـاد
And for some of them on this meaning and this meter: 'Al-Asad wa-bi 'Ariyya [possibly al-Ash'ariyya] are a people who have been guided to rectitude. They intended for all creatures the path of guidance [?]. They declared God transcendent above what the people of obstinacy say, and they sanctified Him from ... [unclear] and rivals. And they declared Him transcendent from falsehood [?] and children. And they negated from Him what is not valid in belief, and they affirmed every attribute that is valid by authentic transmission. They are the moons of the dark night, and they are the guides of the servants. They are seas of knowledge, and they are the leaders of the lands. They are noble in character, and they are the faces of assemblies. They have not departed from a book or a sunna in belief, except the people of ta'til and the people of heresy [?].'
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This passage is a longer poem praising the Ash'ari school. It begins by stating that the Ash'aris have been guided to rectitude (sadad) and that they guide others to the path of guidance. The poem then emphasizes their commitment to tanzih (declaring God transcendent): they transcend God above what 'people of obstinacy' ('aha al-'inad) say, and they sanctify Him from anthropomorphic concepts and from having rivals (andad) or children (awlad). They negate from God whatever is not valid in belief (i.e., anthropomorphic attributes) and affirm every attribute that is valid by authentic transmission (asnad). This is a clear statement of the Ash'ari position on divine attributes: affirmation of the attributes mentioned in revelation without tashbih or ta'til. The poem then praises Ash'ari scholars as 'moons of the dark night', 'guides', 'seas of knowledge', etc. It concludes by stating that they have not departed from Quran and Sunna in belief, except for the people of ta'til (stripping God of attributes) and the people of heresy (ilhad). This reinforces the poem's theme that Ash'aris are the true followers of scripture.

Defending Tawhid: The Hashwiyya Controversy
Ibn Dawud is attacked by Hashwiyya, writes to al-Baqillani, who sends al-Adhari to debate and clarify tawhid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ash'ari school defended divine transcendence against anthropomorphist groups through scholarly correspondence and public debate.

Core Idea
The Ash'ari response to Hashwiyya shows that tawhid requires active defense through argument and authoritative scholarship.

This passage narrates a historical incident where the esteemed Ash'ari scholar Ibn Dawud faced opposition from anthropomorphist groups in Damascus. He sought help from the leading theologian al-Baqillani, who dispatched al-Adhari to publicly clarify the doctrine of tawhid. The story illustrates the practical measures taken to uphold the correct understanding of Allah's transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Pay attention to the names and the flow of events.
The Arabic contains a chain of narration (isnad) at the beginning.
2
Identify the key figures and their roles
Note the roles of Ibn Dawud, al-Baqillani, and al-Adhari. Why did Ibn Dawud write to Baghdad?
Al-Baqillani was a leading Ash'ari theologian.
3
Analyze the theological terms
Find the terms tawhid, tanzih, tashbih, and tahdid. Understand what they mean in this context.
Tanzih means transcendence; tashbih is anthropomorphism; tahdid is delimitation.
4
Interpret the audience reaction
The people of Damascus said 'One, One'. What does this indicate about their acceptance?
This shows they affirmed pure monotheism and rejected anthropomorphism.
5
Reflect on al-Adhari's later travels
Consider why al-Adhari moved to the Maghrib and what that means for the spread of Ash'ari thought.
His move helped disseminate correct aqidah beyond the Levant.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وسمعت الشيخ الفقيه الإمام أبا الحسن على بن المسلم بن محمد بن على بن الفتح السلمي يحكي عن بعض شيوخه أن أبا الحسن ابن داود لما كان يصلى في جامع دمشق تكلم فيه بعض الحشوية فكتب إلى القاضي أبي بكر بن الطيب بن الباقلاني إلى بغداد يعرفه ذلك ويسأله أن يرسل إلى دمشق من أصحابه من يوضح لهم الحق بالحجة فبعث القاضي تلميذه أبا الله الحسين بن حاتم الأذرى فعقد مجلس التذكير في جامع دمشق في حلقه أبي الحسن بن داود وذكر التوحيد ونزه المعبود ونفى عنه التشبيه والتحديد فخرج أهل دمشق من مجلسه وهم يقولون أحد أحد هذا معنى ما ذكره لى رحمه الله وأقام أبو عبد الله الأذرى بدمشق مدة ثم توجه إلى المغرب فنشر العلم بتلك الناحية واستوطن القيروان إلى أن مات بها
And I heard the faqih shaykh al-Imam Abu al-Hasan Ali ibn al-Muslim ibn Muhammad ibn Ali ibn al-Fath al-Sulami narrating from some of his shaykhs that when Abu al-Hasan ibn Dawud was leading prayer in the Mosque of Damascus, some of the Hashwiyya spoke against him. So he wrote to the judge Abu Bakr ibn al-Tayyib ibn al-Baqillani in Baghdad, informing him of that and asking him to send to Damascus some of his companions to clarify the truth for them with proof. So the judge sent his student Abu Allah al-Husayn ibn Hatim al-Adhari. He held a session of admonition in the Mosque of Damascus, in the circle of Abu al-Hasan ibn Dawud, and he mentioned tawhid, declared the Worshipped One transcendent, and negated from Him anthropomorphism and delimitation. So the people of Damascus left his session saying, 'One, One, this is what he mentioned for us, may God have mercy on him.' And Abu Abd Allah al-Adhari stayed in Damascus for a time, then went to the Maghrib, spread knowledge in that region, and settled in Qayrawan until he died there.
Source pages p. 169

Commentary
This passage is an account of a theological conflict between the Ash'ari scholar Ibn Dawud and the Hashwiyya (a pejorative term for anthropomorphist groups) in Damascus. When Ibn Dawud was leading prayer, some Hashwiyya spoke against him, likely accusing him of heterodoxy. He wrote to the renowned Ash'ari theologian Abu Bakr al-Baqillani (d. 403/1013) in Baghdad, requesting that he send a qualified scholar to rebut their arguments convincingly. Al-Baqillani sent his student Abu Abd Allah al-Husayn ibn Hatim al-Adhari. Al-Adhari then conducted a public teaching session (majlis al-tadhkir) in the mosque, within Ibn Dawud's circle. In this session, he discussed tawhid (the oneness of God), negated anthropomorphism (tashbih) and delimitation (tahdid) from God, emphasizing His transcendence (tanzih). The Damascene audience responded by affirming 'One, One' (ahad, ahad), indicating their acceptance of pure monotheism. This was a triumph for the Ash'ari position. The passage then notes that al-Adhari remained in Damascus for a time before moving to the Maghrib (North Africa), where he continued to spread knowledge, settling in Qayrawan (in present-day Tunisia) until his death. This story illustrates the role of scholarly correspondence and the institutional support of the Ash'ari school in defending theological orthodoxy against anthropomorphic tendencies.

التوحيد
التنزيه
التشبيه

أبو بكر الباقلاني

Key Takeaway

Commit to one practice this week that deepens your conviction in Allah's transcendence.

A Heretic Kisses a Tablet at the Kaaba
An eyewitness reports a heretic kissing a tablet at the Kaaba in 545 AH.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand an eyewitness account of heretical practice at the Kaaba and its significance in Islamic history.

Core Idea
Even the Kaaba is not immune to heretical acts.

This lesson presents a striking anecdote from the year 545 AH, where a known heretic performs an unusual ritual at the Kaaba. The story, narrated by a reliable eyewitness, shows how heretical practices could infiltrate even the most sacred spaces. Let us read the account and reflect on its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice the setting: the Sacred Mosque, a room in a ribat, and the narrator's condition.
The narrator is ill and lies down facing the Kaaba.
2
Identify the heretic's action
Focus on what the man from ahl al-bid'a does: he spreads his prayer mat, takes out a tablet, kisses it, and places it before him. Why is this significant?
The tablet is likely venerated, showing innovation in worship.
3
Understand the narrator's perspective
The narrator explicitly identifies the man as 'a known heretic'. This labels the act as deviant and not part of normal practice.
The narrator is a respected scholar, adding credibility.
4
Reflect on the lesson's message
Consider why this story is recorded: to show that heretical practices can occur even at the holiest sites, and to warn against innovation.
This reinforces the importance of adhering to authentic teachings.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

I was looking for a place to rest on my side for an hour. Then I saw the door of the congregational room of the Ramashti ribat near the Bab al-'Azura open, so I headed towards it and entered. I fell on my right side facing the honorable Kaaba, spreading my hand under my cheek so that sleep would not overtake me and break my ablution. Then suddenly a man from the people of innovation (ahl al-bid'a), known for it, came and spread his prayer mat at the door of that room, and took out a tablet (lawh) from his pocket – I think it was of stone – with writing on it. He kissed it and placed it before him. (1) We were told this story by the shaykh, the imam, Abu Ja'far Ahmad ibn Abi Bakr al-Qurtubi, who said: I heard Shaykh Abu al-Fath 'Amir ibn Niham in the presence of our shaykh Abu Muhammad al-Qasim at the time of our hearing of this book from him, and whoever inscribed his name in the audition heard it from the words of Shaykh Abu Ja'far. Thus in the margin of the original.

Commentary
This passage is an anecdotal report about an incident at the Kaaba in 545 AH. The narrator, Abu al-Fath 'Amir, describes entering the Sacred Mosque feeling ill. He lies down in a room of the Ramashti ribat facing the Kaaba, careful not to fall asleep and break ablution. Then he sees a known heretic (min ahl al-bid'a) come, spread his prayer mat, take out a stone tablet with writing, kiss it, and place it before him. The anecdote illustrates the presence of heretical practices even at the holiest site, and the narrator's surprise. The marginal note provides the chain of transmission for this story from Abu Ja'far al-Qurtubi.

Dream Vision: Prophet Approves Sunni Creed
Narrative of a dream where Prophet rejects Rafidah and accepts Sunni creed of Ghazali.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a dream vision was used as a spiritual testimony to affirm Sunni orthodoxy and reject Rafidi innovations.

Core Idea
A dream validates that the Prophet accepts Sunni creed and rejects the Rafidah.

This passage recounts a vivid dream in which the Prophet Muhammad ﷺ personally approves the creeds of Sunni imams and rejects the Rafidah. The narrator, a Sunni scholar, witnesses the Prophet accepting al-Shafi'i, Abu Hanifa, and finally al-Ghazali's Qawa'id al-'Aqa'id as the true creed. This story serves as a spiritual testimony to the correctness of Sunni beliefs.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context
Read the opening lines to see the chain of transmission and the narrator's situation. Note that he is in a state of weakness and seeks rest in the mosque.
The narrator is a Sunni scholar who feels ill and enters a room to rest.
2
Observe the Rafidi's actions
Notice how the man of innovation prays with a stone tablet, prostrating on it and kissing it. This contrasts with Sunni practice and sets up the conflict.
The narrator is disturbed by this and wishes the Prophet were alive to condemn them.
3
Enter the dream
The narrator falls asleep and dreams of a courtyard where the Prophet sits surrounded by scholars. See how each Sunni imam presents his book and is welcomed.
Al-Shafi'i and Abu Hanifa are explicitly named and warmly received.
4
Witness the rejection of Rafidah
When the Rafidi tries to present his booklets, he is rebuked and expelled by one of the Prophet's companions. This is the key moment of validation.
The Rafidi's beliefs are described as 'false' (bāṭila) in the narrative.
5
Present the Sunni creed
Finally, the narrator presents al-Ghazali's Qawa'id al-'Aqa'id, and the Prophet permits him to read it. The story ends as he begins.
This highlights the full acceptance of the Sunni creed as represented by al-Ghazali.

(۱) حدثنا بهذه الحكاية الشيخ الإمام أبو جعفر أحمد بن أبي بكر القرطبي قال سمعت الشيخ أبا الفتح عامر بن نحام وذلك بحضرة شيخنا أبي محمد القسم في حين سماعنا لهذا الكتاب عليه ومن أثبت أسمه في السماع سمعها من لفظ الشيخ أبي جعفر ) هكذا في هامش الأصل
I heard the faqih and imam Abu al-Qasim Sa'd ibn Ali ibn Abi al-Qasim ibn Abi Hurayra al-Isfarayini al-Sufi al-Shafi'i in Damascus (1) said: I heard the unique imam, the ornament of reciters, the beauty of the sanctuary, Abu al-Fath 'Amir ibn Niham ibn 'Amir al-'Arabi al-Sawi in Mecca, may Allah protect it, say: I entered the Sacred Mosque on Sunday, between noon and afternoon, the fourteenth of Shawwal, year five hundred and forty-five. I had a kind of weakness and dizziness such that I could not stand or sit due to its severity, and I was looking for a place to rest on my side for a moment. I saw the door of the congregational room of the Ramashti ribat near Bab al-'Uzura open, so I headed towards it and entered. I lay on my right side facing the noble Ka'ba, resting my hand under my cheek so that sleep would not take me and break my ablution. Suddenly a man of innovation, known for it, came and spread his prayer mat at the door of that room, and took out a tablet from his pocket—I think it was of stone—with writing on it. He kissed it and placed it before him, and prayed a long prayer dropping his hands in it according to their custom. He would prostrate on that tablet each time, and when he finished his prayer, he prostrated on it and prolonged it, and rubbed his cheek on both sides on it, and supplicated earnestly. Then he raised his head, kissed it, placed it on his eyes, then kissed it a second time and put it back in his pocket as he had done. When I saw that, I disliked it and was horrified by it, and said to myself: 'Would that the Messenger of Allah, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, were alive among us to inform them of the evil of their doing and the innovation they are upon.' While thinking this, I was warding off sleep from myself so that it would not take me and break my ablution. While I was like that, drowsiness came over me and overcame me, and I was as if between wakefulness and sleep. I saw a wide courtyard with many people standing, and in the hand of each was a bound book. They had all gathered around a person. I asked people about their situation and who was in the circle. They said: 'He is the Messenger of Allah, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, and these are the followers of the schools of law; they want to read their schools and creed from their books to the Messenger of Allah and have him correct them.' He said: While I was like that, looking at the people, one from the circle came with a book in his hand. It was said: 'This is al-Shafi'i, may Allah be pleased with him.' He entered the middle of the circle and greeted the Messenger of Allah, may Allah bless him and grant him peace. He said: I saw the Messenger of Allah, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, in his beauty and perfection, wearing white washed clean clothes—turban, shirt, and other garments—in the manner of the people of tasawwuf. He returned the greeting and welcomed him. Al-Shafi'i sat before him and read his school and creed from the book to him. After that, another person came, said to be Abu Hanifa, may Allah be pleased with him, with a book in his hand. He greeted and sat beside al-Shafi'i, and read his school and creed from the book. Then after him every follower of a school came until only a few remained, and each reader sat next to the other. When they finished, suddenly one of the innovators called Rafidah came, with unbound booklets in his hand containing mention of their false beliefs. He wanted to enter the circle and read them to the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him. So one of those who were with the Messenger of Allah went out to him, rebuked him, took the booklets from his hand, threw them outside the circle, drove him away, and humiliated him. He said: When I saw that the people had finished and no one remained to read anything to him, I advanced a little, and in my hand was a bound book. I called out and said: 'O Messenger of Allah, this book is my creed and the creed of the People of the Sunnah; would you permit me to read it to you?' He, peace be upon him, said: 'And what is that?' I said: 'O Messenger of Allah, it is Qawa'id al-'Aqa'id composed by al-Ghazali.' So he permitted me to read, so I sat down and began: (1) This story was narrated to us by the shaykh and imam Abu Ja'far Ahmad ibn Abi Bakr al-Qurtubi, who said: I heard Shaykh Abu al-Fath 'Amir ibn Niham—and that was in the presence of our shaykh Abu Muhammad al-Qasim at the time of our hearing this book from him—and whoever confirmed his name in the hearing certificate heard it from the utterance of Shaykh Abu Ja'far. Thus in the margin of the original.
Source pages p. 227

Commentary
This passage is a first-person narrative of a dream vision (ru'ya) intended to validate Sunni orthodoxy. It begins with the chain of transmission (isnad) through which the story is related. The narrator, Abu al-Fath 'Amir ibn Niham, describes entering the Sacred Mosque in Mecca in 545 AH feeling ill. He sees a man of innovation (bid'a) praying with a stone tablet, prostrating on it and kissing it—this represents the practice of the Rafidah (Shi'a) who venerate such objects. The narrator wishes the Prophet were alive to condemn them. Then he falls asleep and dreams of a courtyard where the Prophet ﷺ sits surrounded by people with books. Al-Shafi'i and Abu Hanifa come forward and read their schools and creeds to the Prophet, who approves. Then a Rafidi tries to present his booklets, but is rebuked and expelled. Finally, the narrator presents al-Ghazali's 'Qawa'id al-'Aqa'id' as his creed, and the Prophet permits him to read it. The story cuts off as he begins. The marginal note adds an alternative chain for this story. The passage functions as a hagiographic proof that the Sunni creed of al-Ghazali is the true creed accepted by the Prophet, while the Rafidah are rejected.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This dream is taken as a binding legal proof (ḥujja) for Sunni doctrine.
Correction: Dreams are not a source of sharī'a; they are spiritual affirmations. The passage itself does not claim legal authority but uses the dream to comfort and encourage Sunni believers.

Checkpoint
What happened when the Rafidi tried to present his booklets to the Prophet in the dream?
A) The Prophet accepted them
B) He was rebuked and thrown out
C) Al-Shafi'i debated him
D) The narrator defended him

Key Takeaway
The dream vision powerfully affirms that the Sunni creed, particularly as expounded by al-Ghazali, is the accepted belief of the Prophet, while Rafidi innovations are rejected.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read about the Rafidi being humiliated and rejected, do you feel a sense of superiority, or does it make you examine your own practices to ensure they are free of innovation? How can you guard against self-righteousness while still upholding the truth?
?
The narrator was physically weak and in pain, yet he remained vigilant in observing the Rafidi's actions and then sought the Prophet's validation. In your moments of weakness or illness, do you still prioritize seeking the truth and aligning your creed with the Prophet's approval? What changes would you need to make to do so?
?
The Prophet approved al-Ghazali's book as the creed of the People of the Sunnah. Do you treat the scholarly works of Sunni Islam with that same reverence? Do you actively study and internalize them, or do you merely claim to follow the Sunni path without deep engagement? What one step can you take today to deepen your understanding of your own creed?

Sects and Disagreements
beginner
8 min
Dream of the Prophet Validating Sunni Creeds
Ibn Asakir narrates a dream where imams present their doctrines to the Prophet.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a dream narrative was used to affirm Sunni creeds and reject innovations, and why al-Ghazali's work is presented as orthodox.

Core Idea
A dream vision of the Prophet validates Sunni orthodoxy and condemns heresy.

This lesson presents a vivid dream account recorded by Ibn Asakir. In it, the Prophet accepts the creeds of the Sunni imams but rejects the Rafidah. The story culminates with the narrator receiving permission to read al-Ghazali's Qawa'id al-'Aqa'id, signaling its orthodoxy.

Guided Path
1
Read the dream narrative
Read the Arabic passage once for the story. Then read the translation. Notice the sequence of events and the emotions of the narrator.
The narrator initially feels disturbed by some practices he observes.
2
Identify the participants
List the characters in the dream: the narrator, the Prophet, al-Shafi'i, Abu Hanifa, other imams, the Rafidah man, and the companion who expels him. Note how each is treated.
3
Contrast the treatment of groups
Observe the stark contrast: the Sunni imams are welcomed and their books accepted; the Rafidah is rebuked and his booklets thrown away. The narrator's book is permitted.
4
Reflect on the purpose
Ask yourself: why would Ibn Asakir include this story? What message does it convey about Sunni orthodoxy and the authority of al-Ghazali's work?
This story serves to legitimize Sunni creeds, especially Ghazali's Qawa'id al-'Aqa'id.

الله صلى كل واحد منهم وصلى صلاة طويلة مرسلاً يديه فيها على عادتهم ، وكان يسجد على ذلك اللويح في كل مرة فإذا فرغ من صلاته سجد عليه وأطال فيه وكان يمعك خده من الجانبين عليه ، ويتضرع في الدعاء ثم رفع رأسه وقبله ووضعه على عينيه ثم قبله ثانيا وأدخله في جيبه ، كما كان قال فلما رأيت ذلك كرهته واستوحشت منه ذلك وقلت في نفسي ليت كان رسول الله عليه وسلم حيا ، فيما بيننا ، ليخبرهم بسوء صنيعهم وما هم عليه من البدعة ، ومع هذا التفكر كنت أطرد النوم عن نفسى كيلا يأخذنى فتفسد طهارتي فبينا أنا كذلك إذ طرأ على النعاس وغلبني فكأني بين اليقظة والمنام فرأيت عرصة واسعة فيها ناس كثيرون واقفين ، وفي يد کتاب مجلد قد تحلقوا كلهم على شخص فسألت الناس عن حالهم وعمن في الحلقة قالوا هو رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم وهؤلاء أصحاب المذاهب يريدون أن يقرؤا مذاهبهم واعتقادهم من كتبهم علي رسول الله ويصححوه عليه ، قال فبينا أنا كذلك انظر إلى القوم إذ جاء واحد من أهل الحلقة وبيده كتاب قيل أن هذا الشافعي رضى الله عنه فدخل في وسط الحلقة وسلم على رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم قال فرأيت رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم في جـمـالـه وكماله متلبسا بالثياب البيض المغسولة النظيفة من من العمامة والقميص وسائر الثياب على زي أهل التصوف فرد عليه الجواب ورحب به وقعد الشافعي بين يديه وقرأ من الكتاب مذهبه واعتقاده عليه ، وبعد ذلك جاء شخص آخر قيل هو أبو حنيفة رضى الله عنه وبيده كتاب فسلم وقـعـد بجنب الشافعى وقرأ من الكتاب مذهبه واعتقاده ثم أتى بعده كل صاحب مذهب إلى أن لم يبق إلا القليل وكل من يقرأ يقعد بجنب الآخـر فـلـمـا فرغوا إذا واحد من المبتدعة الملقبة بالرافضة قد جاء وفي يده كراريس غير مجلدة فيها ذكر عقائدهم الباطلة وهم أن يدخل الحلقة ويقرأها على رسول الله له فخرج واحد ممن كان مع رسول الله إليه وزجره وأخذ الكراريس من يده ورماها إلى خارج الحلقة وطرده وأهانه ، قال فلما رأيت أن القوم قد فرغوا وما بقى أحد يقرأ عليه شيئا تقدمت قليلا وكان في یدی کتاب مجلد فناديت وقلت يا رسول الله هذا الكتاب مـعـتـقـدى ومعتقد أهل السنة لو أذنت لى حتى أقرأه عليك فقال صلى الله عليه وسلم وايش ذاك قلت يا رسول الله هو ( قواعد العقائد ( الذي صنفه الغزالي فأذن لي في القراءة فقعدت وابتدأت :
God bless each one of them. He prayed a long prayer, sending his hands in it according to their custom, and he prostrated on that wooden tablet every time, and when he finished his prayer he prostrated on it and prolonged it, and he rubbed his cheek on both sides on it, and he supplicated humbly in prayer, then he raised his head and kissed it, placed it on his eyes, then kissed it a second time and inserted it into his pocket, as he had said. When I saw that, I disliked it and felt alienated by it, and I said to myself: 'Would that the Messenger of God, peace be upon him, were alive among us, to inform them of the evil of their doing and what they are upon of innovation.' And with this reflection, I kept driving away sleep from myself lest it overcome me and spoil my purity. While I was like that, suddenly drowsiness came upon me and overcame me, and I was as if between wakefulness and sleep, and I saw a wide courtyard in which many people were standing, and in the hand of a bound book, they had all gathered around a person. I asked the people about their situation and about who was in the circle. They said: 'He is the Messenger of God, may God bless him and grant him peace, and these are the followers of the schools of law (as-hab al-madhahib). They want to read their schools of law and their creed from their books to the Messenger of God and have him correct it.' He said: While I was thus looking at the people, suddenly one of the people of the circle came, and in his hand a book. It was said: 'This is al-Shafi'i, may God be pleased with him.' He entered the middle of the circle and greeted the Messenger of God, may God bless him and grant him peace. He said: I saw the Messenger of God, may God bless him and grant him peace, in his beauty and perfection, dressed in white, washed, clean clothes, including the turban, shirt, and the rest of the clothes, in the attire of the people of tasawwuf. He returned the greeting and welcomed him, and al-Shafi'i sat before him and read from the book his school of law and his creed to him. After that, another person came, it was said: 'He is Abu Hanifa, may God be pleased with him,' and in his hand a book. He greeted and sat next to al-Shafi'i, and read from the book his school of law and his creed. Then after him came every follower of a school until only a few remained; and everyone who read sat next to the other. When they finished, suddenly one of the innovators (al-mubtadi'a) known as the Rafidah came, and in his hand were unbound booklets containing mention of their false beliefs, and he wanted to enter the circle and read them to the Messenger of God. So one of those who were with the Messenger of God went out to him, rebuked him, took the booklets from his hand, threw them outside the circle, expelled him, and humiliated him. He said: When I saw that the people had finished and no one remained to read anything to him, I advanced a little, and in my hand was a bound book. I called out and said: 'O Messenger of God, this book is my creed and the creed of Ahl al-Sunna. If you would permit me to read it to you.' He, peace be upon him, said: 'And what is that?' I said: 'O Messenger of God, it is Qawa'id al-'Aqa'id which al-Ghazali composed.' So he permitted me to read, so I sat down and began:
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage is a narrative (story) that recounts a dream vision of a scholar (likely Ibn Asakir himself or his source). The story describes how a person, annoyed by certain innovative practices (like kissing a wooden tablet after prayer), falls asleep and sees a dream. In the dream, he sees the Prophet Muhammad, peace be upon him, surrounded by followers of various schools of law (madhahib). One by one, the imams—beginning with al-Shafi'i, then Abu Hanifa, then others—approach the Prophet and read their books (their doctrines and creeds) to him, and the Prophet accepts them. Then a man from the Rafidah (a heretical group) tries to present his unbound booklets of false beliefs, but a companion of the Prophet expels him and throws his booklets away. Finally, the narrator presents a bound book titled 'Qawa'id al-'Aqa'id' (The Foundations of Creeds) by al-Ghazali, claiming it as his own creed and that of Ahl al-Sunna, and the Prophet permits him to read it. The story thus affirms the orthodoxy of the Sunni schools and the works of al-Ghazali, while condemning the innovations of the Rafidah. The narrative style serves to legitimize Ghazali's creedal work through a prophetic dream.

Key Terms
الرافضة
أهل السنة
البدعة
قواعد العقائد
الغزالي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that dreams can be used as a source of religious law or that any book approved in a dream is automatically true.
Correction: This dream is not a source of legislation but a rhetorical affirmation of Sunni orthodoxy; it does not imply that any book presented to the Prophet in a dream is automatically correct.

Checkpoint
What happened to the man from the Rafidah when he tried to present his booklets to the Prophet?
A) He was allowed to read them and the Prophet corrected them.
B) He was expelled and his booklets were thrown away.
C) He was invited to sit with the other imams.
D) He was given a bound book to replace his unbound ones.

Key Takeaway
This dream narrative affirms the orthodoxy of Sunni imams and al-Ghazali's Qawa'id al-'Aqa'id, while rejecting the Rafidah innovations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you were to present your personal creed to the Prophet in a dream, would you be confident that it aligns with the beliefs he would accept? What steps can you take today to ensure your faith is based on authentic sources?
?
The dream shows the Prophet welcoming those who read bound books of correct doctrine, while expelling the one with unbound booklets of falsehood. Is there any 'unbound booklet' in your own life—an idea or practice you follow without solid evidence from the Quran and Sunnah? Will you set aside your pride and examine it?
?
Al-Ghazali's Qawa'id al-'Aqa'id is presented as a revered text. But it is a human composition. Are you more attached to the labels 'Sunni' or 'Shafi'i' than to the truth itself? If someone questioned your beliefs, would you be able to defend them from revelation, or would you just rely on the authority of a book or scholar?

The Mahdar: Testimony against Hashwiyya Anthropomorphism
A legal testimony documenting Hashwiyya anthropomorphism and affirming Ash'ari creed.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the historical testimony against Hashwiyya anthropomorphism and how it affirms the Ash'ari doctrine of divine transcendence and eternal attributes.

Core Idea
A legal document exposing bodily descriptions of God taught by the Hashwiyya.

This passage is a formal legal testimony (mahdar) containing the signatures of many imams, documenting the heretical anthropomorphic beliefs of the Hashwiyya and affirming the correct Ash'ari creed. It was included by Ibn Asakir to demonstrate the historical consensus of Sunni scholars against such deviations. As you read, notice how the witnesses meticulously describe both the false beliefs and the true doctrine.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context
This document is a legal testimony (mahdar) collected by the companions of Abu Nasr al-Qushayri. Notice the chain of transmission and why it was included.
The document was shown to the author's son, who ordered it to be copied.
2
Identify the Hashwiyya beliefs
Read the testimony and list the specific anthropomorphic beliefs attributed to the Hashwiyya, such as attributing body parts and a physical descent to God.
The text mentions foot, molars, uvula, fingers, riding a donkey, etc.
3
Contrast with affirmed creed
Note the Ash'ari emphasis on eternal attributes (knowledge, power, etc.) and absolute transcendence.
The text asserts that God is exalted above accidents and change.
4
Grasp historical significance
The document appeals to the vizier for support, showing the seriousness of the threat posed by these anthropomorphic beliefs.
This shows that theological disputes had political dimensions.

والأسواق والطرقات والخلوة والجماعات ، ثم غرهم الطمع والأهمال ومدهم في طغيانهم الغى والضلال إلى الطعن فيمن يعتضد به أئمة الهدى وهو للشريعة العروة الوثقى وجعلوا أفعاله الدينية معاصى دنية ، وترقوا من ذلك إلى القدح في الشافعي رحمة الله عليه وأصحابه واتفق عود الشيخ الإمام الأوحد أبى نصر ابن الأستاذ الإمام زين الإسلام أبى القسم القشيري رحمة الله عليه ، من مكة حرسها الله فدعا الناس إلى التوحيد وقدس البارى عن الحوادث والتحديد فاستجاب له أهل التحقيق من الصدور الأفاضل السادة الأماثل وتمادت الحشوية في ضلالتها والاصرار على جهالتها وأبوا إلا التصريح بأن المعبود ذو قدم وأضراس ولهوات وأنامل وأنه ينزل بذاته ويتردد على حمار فى صورة شاب أمرد بشعر قططط ، وعليه تاج يلمع وفى رجليه نعلان من ذهب وحفظ ذلك عنهم وعللوه ودونوه في كتبهم وإلى العوام القوه ، وأن هذه الأخبار لا تأويل لها وأنها تجري على ظواهرها وتعتقد كما ورد لفظها وأنه تعالى يتكلم بصوت كالرعد وكصهيل الخيل وينقمون على أهل الحق لقولهم أن الله تعالى موصوف بصفات الجلال منعوت بالعلم والقدرة والسمع والبصر والحياة والارادة والكلام وهذه الصفات قديمة وأنه يتعالى عن قبول الحوادث ، ولا يجوز تشبيه ذاته بذات المخلوقين ولا تشبيه كلامه بكلام المخلوقين ومن المشهور المعلوم أن الأئمة الفقهاء على اختلاف مذاهبهم في الفروع كانوا يصرحون بهذا الاعتقاد ويدرسونه ظاهراً مكشوفا لأصحابهم ومن هاجر من البلاد إليهم ، ولم يتجاسر أحد على إنكاره ولا تجوز متجوز بالرد عليهم دون القدح والطعن فيهم ، وأن هذه عقيدة أصحاب الشافعي رحمة الله عليه يدينون الله تعالى بها ويلقونه باعتقادها ويبرون إليه من سواها من غير شك ولا انحراف عنها وما لهذه العصابة مستند ولا للحق مغيث يعتمد إلا الله تعالى ورأفة المجلس السامي الأجلى العالمى العادلى القوامي النظامى الرضوى ، أمتعه الله بحياة
And among what came to the imam, the scholar, the hafiz, the trustworthy, the pride of the Sunna, the hadith master of Syria, Abu Muhammad al-Qasim after the death of his father, the imam, the scholar, the hafiz, the shaykh of Islam, Abu al-Qasim Ali ibn al-Hasan ibn Hibbat Allah al-Shafi'i, may God have mercy on him, of the benefits befitting this book: a document (mahdar) in the handwriting of some companions of the imam, the scholar, Abu Nasr Abd al-Rahim ibn al-Ustadh Abu al-Qasim al-Qushayri, containing the signatures of the imams attesting to the correctness of his statement and their agreement with his creed, in the manner that is mentioned in this book. Our shaykh Abu Muhammad al-Qasim showed it to us, made us hear it, and ordered us to write it down. So we copied it as it is and recorded it in this biography befitting him. It is:
In the name of God, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful.
Those whose name and lineage are established, whose path and school are sound, and whose religion and trustworthiness have been tested—among the imams, jurists, eminent scholars, people of the Quran, and notable just witnesses—testify, and they wrote their well-known signatures in their customary expressions, hastening to fulfill the trust and being cautious in that. God (forbid) that religion should be violated out of fear of His saying, 'And who is more unjust than one who conceals a testimony that he has from God?' (Quran 2:140). That a group of the Hashwiyya, the riffraff, the rabble claiming to be Hanbalis, have manifested in Baghdad such terrible innovations and heinous disgrace as no atheist would permit, let alone a monotheist, nor would anyone who disparages the foundation of the Sharia or a denier (of attributes) permit. They accused everyone who exalts the Creator, may He be blessed and exalted, above deficiencies and defects, negates from Him temporal origin and anthropomorphic comparisons, sanctifies Him from incarnation and decay, venerates Him above change from one state to another, and above His indwelling in accidents or the occurrence of accidents in Him, of disbelief and transgression, and of opposing the people of truth and faith. They went to extremes in slandering the past imams, reviling the people of truth and the party of religion, and cursing them in mosques, shrines, assemblies, masjids, markets, roads, in private and in public. Then greed and neglect deluded them, and error and misguidance prolonged their transgression until they attacked the one whom the imams of guidance rely upon, who is the firmest handhold of the Sharia, and they considered his religious deeds as base sins. Then they escalated from that to disparaging al-Shafi'i, may God have mercy on him, and his companions. And it happened that the unique shaykh, the imam Abu Nasr ibn al-Ustadh, the imam Zayn al-Islam Abu al-Qasim al-Qushayri, may God have mercy on him, returned from Mecca, may God protect it, and called people to tawhid (monotheism) and declared the Creator exalted above accidents and limitation. The people of verification among the eminent notables, the nobles, the excellent, responded to him. But the Hashwiyya persisted in their misguidance and adherence to their ignorance, and they insisted on declaring that the worshiped one has a foot, molars, uvulae, and fingers; that He descends in His essence and travels on a donkey in the form of a beardless youth with curly hair, wearing a shining crown, and on his feet sandals of gold. They preserved that from them, recorded it, compiled it in their books, and cast it to the common people. And they claimed that these reports have no interpretation and are to be taken literally and believed as they are worded, and that He, exalted, speaks with a voice like thunder and like the neighing of horses. They resent the people of truth for saying that God, exalted, is described with attributes of majesty, characterized by knowledge, power, hearing, sight, life, will, and speech, and that these attributes are eternal, and that He is exalted above receiving accidents, and that it is not permissible to liken His essence to the essence of created beings nor to liken His speech to the speech of created beings. And it is well-known and manifest that the imams of jurisprudence, despite the differences in their schools in subsidiary matters, used to declare this belief and teach it openly and clearly to their companions and to whoever migrated from other lands to them. No one dared to deny it, nor did anyone permit himself to reject them without slandering and insulting them. And this is the creed of the companions of al-Shafi'i, may God have mercy on him, by which they worship God, meet Him with this belief, and disassociate from anything else, without doubt or deviation. This group has no support, and truth has no helper to rely on except God, and the compassion of the lofty, most glorious, learned, just, Qawwami, Nizami, Radawi council [i.e., the vizier], may God let him enjoy life.
Source pages p. 236

Commentary
This passage is a legal testimony (mahdar) recorded by the companions of Abu Nasr al-Qushayri and signed by many imams, affirming the correct creed and condemning the anthropomorphic beliefs of the Hashwiyya (a group associated with the Hanbalites). The document begins with an introduction explaining how it came to be included in this book: it was shown to the author's son, Abu Muhammad al-Qasim, who ordered it to be copied. Then the testimony itself begins with the basmala. The witnesses state that a group of Hashwiyya, claiming to be Hanbalis, have spread terrible innovations in Baghdad: they accuse those who affirm God's transcendence (tanzih) of kufr, they slander past imams, curse in public places, and even attack al-Shafi'i. When Abu Nasr al-Qushayri returned from Mecca and called to tawhid, the thoughtful people responded, but the Hashwiyya insisted on their false beliefs. They explicitly attribute bodily parts to God: a foot, molars, uvula, fingers. They claim He descends bodily and rides a donkey in the form of a beardless youth with curly hair, wearing a crown and golden sandals. They record these beliefs in their books and teach them to the common people. They reject ta'wil (allegorical interpretation) and insist on literalism, and they say God speaks with a voice like thunder and horse neighing. They resent the Ash'aris for affirming that God's attributes (knowledge, power, hearing, sight, life, will, speech) are eternal and that He is not subject to change or indwelling. The document then asserts that this Ash'ari creed is what all the jurist imams, of various schools, openly teach. No one dared to deny it. And this is the creed of the Shafi'is, by which they worship God and disavow any other belief. The document ends by appealing for support from the ruling council (the vizier).

Key Terms
الحشوية
التشبيه
تنزيه
الصفات القديمة
أبو نصر القشيري
محضر
الشافعي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The document condemns all Hanbalis as anthropomorphists.
Correction: The document specifically criticizes the Hashwiyya, a group that claims to be Hanbalis but holds extreme anthropomorphic views. It does not condemn mainstream Hanbalis.

Checkpoint
According to the mahdar, what did the Hashwiyya claim about God?
A) He has no attributes
B) He has a foot, molars, uvula, and fingers
C) He is purely spiritual
D) He is incarnate in all things

Key Takeaway
The mahdar serves as a historical record of the Ash'ari defense of divine transcendence against anthropomorphic deviations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you imagine God, do you ever picture a form or location in your mind? How can you train your heart to worship a God who is transcendent beyond any image you can conceive?
?
This testimony shows scholars willing to document and condemn public error. Are you willing to speak the truth about God even if it makes you unpopular or brings mockery from those around you?
?
If a group today taught that God has a hand like a human hand, would you have the courage to reject it publicly as these Ash'ari imams did? What holds you back?

Anthropomorphic Claims of the Hashwiyya
List of Hashwiyya anthropomorphic beliefs and rejection of ta'wil

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the specific anthropomorphic claims attributed to the Hashwiyya and understand their rejection of metaphorical interpretation (ta'wil) of scriptural passages.

Core Idea
The Hashwiyya took descriptions of God literally, attributing body parts and physical movement to Him.

This passage exposes the extreme anthropomorphism of the Hashwiyya sect, recounting their explicit claims about God's physical form and actions. It serves as a clear example of the beliefs that the Ash'ari school opposed and refuted.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation to get the overall meaning.
Notice the specific physical attributes mentioned.
2
List the anthropomorphic attributes
Go through the passage and list each body part or action the Hashwiyya attributed to God (e.g., foot, molars, descending, riding a donkey).
There are at least ten distinct items in this list.
3
Understand their stance on interpretation
The Hashwiyya reject ta'wil and insist on literal reading. Find the sentence that states this clearly.
The key phrase is 'وأن هذه الأخبار لا تأويل لها'.
4
Reflect on why this is problematic
Consider why the Ash'aris would view such beliefs as contradicting tawheed and divine transcendence (tanzih).

He permits criticism that harms the foundation of the Sharia, and there is no one who negates [attributes], and they attribute everyone who declares the Creator, exalted and majestic, free from deficiencies and defects, negates from Him temporal origination and anthropomorphisms, sanctifies Him from incarnation and cessation, magnifies Him from change from state to state, and from His incarnation in originated things and the origination of originated things in Him—they attribute [such a person] to unbelief and transgression, and opposition to the people of truth and faith. They went to extremes in slandering the past imams, reviling the people of truth and the party of religion, and cursing them in congregational mosques, shrines, assemblies, masjids, markets, roads, in private and in congregations. Then greed and negligence deceived them, and error and misguidance extended them in their transgression to attacking the one whom the imams of guidance rely upon, who is the firmest handhold of the Sharia, and they considered his religious actions as base sins. They escalated from that to attacking al-Shafi'i, may God have mercy on him and his companions. It coincided with the return of the unique teacher Abu Nasr, son of the teacher Imam Zayn al-Islam Abu al-Qasim al-Qushayri (may God have mercy on him) from Mecca, may God protect it. He called the people to tawheed and declared the Creator transcendent over temporal events and limitation, so the people of verification from the foremost nobles and virtuous leaders responded to him. But the Hashwiyya persisted in their misguidance and insistence on their ignorance, and they refused except to declare that the worshipped being has foot, molars, uvula, fingers, and that He descends in His essence and moves about on a donkey in the form of a beardless youth with curly hair, wearing a shining crown and on his feet two sandals of gold. They preserved that [statement] from them, explained it, and recorded it in their books, and they threw it to the common people. And [they said] that these reports have no interpretation, that they are taken literally and believed as their wording came, and that He, exalted, speaks with a voice like thunder and like the neighing of horses.
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
This passage is an indictment of the Hashwiyya sect. It begins by accusing them of slandering those who uphold tanzih (transcendence) and attribute to them disbelief. Then it lists their anthropomorphic claims: that God has body parts (foot, molars, etc.), descends, rides a donkey, etc. It also mentions that they reject ta'wil (interpretation) of scriptural passages and take them literally. The passage serves to document the extreme anthropomorphism that the Ash'aris opposed.

?

?

What concrete step will you take to ensure your aqeedah is free from tashbih?
?
This passage records a historical deviation. If you were to meet a person who insists on a literal anthropomorphic understanding of Allah, what would you say to them, and how would you feel knowing the gravity of such a belief? Are you ready to defend the principle of tanzih, even if it means challenging familiar interpretations?

Hashwiyya Attacks on the People of Tanzih
Accusations against those who uphold tanzih and al-Qushayri's response

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Hashwiyya persecuted those who affirmed divine transcendence (tanzih) and how Imam al-Qushayri responded to this deviation.

Core Idea
The Hashwiyya aggressively slandered and cursed the Ash'ari scholars who upheld God's transcendence.

This passage documents the Hashwiyya's hostility toward those who negated anthropomorphism from God, including slander, cursing, and attacks on Imam al-Shafi'i. It also describes the return of Imam al-Qushayri to Mecca and his call to tawheed, which was answered by the people of verification.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Focus on the accusations and the role of al-Qushayri.
Notice the strong language: 'كفر والطغيان', 'قذف الأئمة', 'ثلب أهل الحق'.
2
Identify those attacked
The Hashwiyya attack anyone who declares God free from deficiencies, negates anthropomorphism, and sanctifies Him from incarnation. List the categories of people they target.
Also note the specific imams mentioned: al-Shafi'i.
3
Understand the response
Imam al-Qushayri returned from Mecca and called people to tawheed and transcendence. Who responded to him?
The text says 'أهل التحقيق من الصدور الأفاضل السادة الأماثل'.
4
Reflect on the context
Consider why the Hashwiyya would resort to public cursing and slander, and what this reveals about the intellectual climate.
