وليس انقراض أرباب هذه المذاهب التي سمينا يصحح لهذا الجاهل هذه المقالة التي عنه حكينا ، ولسنا نسلم أن أبا الحسن اخترع مذهباً خامسا وإنما أقام من مذاهب أهل السنة ما صار عند المبتدعة دارسا وأوضح من أقوال من تقدمه من الأربعة وغيرهم ما غدا ملتبسا وجدد من معالم الشريعة ما أصبح بتكذيب من اعتدى منطمسا ولسنا ننتسب بمذهبنا في التوحيد إليه على معنى أنا نقلده فيه ونعتمد عليه ولكنا نوافقه على ما صار إليه من التوحيد لقيام الأدلة على صحته لا لمجرد التقليد وإنما ينتسب منا من انتسب إلى مذهبه ليتميز عن المبتدعة الذين لا يقولون به من أصناف المعتزلة والجهمية المعطلة والمجسمة والكرامية والمشبهة السالمية وغيرهم من سائر طوائف المبتدعة وأصحاب المقالات الفاسدة المخترعة لأن الأشعرى هو الذى انتدب للرد عليهم حتى قمعهم وأظهر لمن لم يعرف البدع بدعهم ولسنا نرى الأئمة الأربعة الذين عنيـتـم في أصول الدين مختلفين ، بل نراهم في القول بتوحيد الله وتنزيهه في ذاته مؤتلفين وعلى نفى التشبيه عن القديم سبحانه وتعالى مجتمعين ، والأشعري رحمه الله في الاصول علي منهاجهم أجمعين ، فما علي من انتسب إليه على هذا الوجه جناح ولا يرجى لمن تبرأ من عقيدته الصحيحة فلاح ، فإن عددتم القول بالتنزيه وترك التشبيه تمشعرا فالموحدون بأسرهم أشعرية ولا يضر عصابة انتمت إلى موحد مجرد التشنيع عليها بما هى منه برية
The extinction of the masters of these schools that we have named does not validate for this ignorant person the statement we have reported from him. And we do not concede that Abu al-Hasan invented a fifth school; rather, he revived from the schools of Ahl al-Sunnah that which had become obscure among the innovators, and he clarified from the statements of those who preceded him from the four and others that which had become ambiguous, and he renewed from the landmarks of the Shari'ah that which had become effaced by the denial of the aggressor. And we do not attribute ourselves in our school of tawhid to him in the sense that we blindly follow him and rely on him, but we agree with him on what he adopted of tawhid because of the evidence establishing its correctness, not for mere taqlid. Rather, whoever among us attributes himself to his school does so to be distinguished from the innovators who do not adhere to it, from the categories of the Mu'tazila, the Jahmiyya al-Mu'attila, the Mujassima, the Karramiyya, the Mushabbiha al-Salimiyya, and others from all the sects of innovators and the proponents of fabricated corrupt doctrines, because al-Ash'ari is the one who undertook to refute them until he suppressed them and made their innovations apparent to those who did not know the innovations. And we do not see the four imams whom you mentioned differing in the principles of religion; rather, we see them in the statement of the tawhid of Allah and His transcendence in His essence agreeing, and on the negation of tashbih from the Qadim (Exalted and High) united. And al-Ash'ari (may Allah have mercy on him) in the principles is upon their methodology altogether. So there is no sin upon the one who attributes himself to him in this manner, nor is success hoped for the one who disassociates himself from his correct creed. For if you consider the statement of transcendence and abandoning tashbih as 'tamash'ur', then all the monotheists are Ash'aris, and it does not harm a group that has affiliated itself with a monotheist that accusation is made against them of that which they are innocent.
Source pages p. 269

Commentary
This passage is a defense of the Ash'ari school. Ibn Asakir begins by arguing that the extinction of earlier schools does not validate the ignorant claim that Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari invented a fifth school. Rather, Ash'ari revived the Sunnah that had become obscure and clarified ambiguous statements from earlier scholars. The author clarifies that his own adherence to Ash'ari in tawhid is not based on blind imitation (taqlid) but on evidence. He then explains that the purpose of attributing oneself to Ash'ari is to distinguish oneself from innovators such as the Mu'tazila, Jahmiyya, Mujassima, Karramiyya, and Mushabbiha. Ash'ari is the one who refuted them and exposed their innovations. Next, Ibn Asakir asserts that the four imams (Abu Hanifa, Malik, al-Shafi'i, and Ahmad) are not in disagreement on the principles of religion (usul al-din); they are united on tawhid and tanzih (transcendence of Allah) and on negating tashbih (anthropomorphism) from Allah. Al-Ash'ari followed their methodology. Therefore, there is no blame on one who attributes himself to Ash'ari in this sense. If calling the affirmation of tanzih and rejection of tashbih 'tamash'ur' (being Ash'ari), then all monotheists are Ash'aris, and accusation cannot harm them.

Key Terms
أبو الحسن الأشعري
المبتدعة
المعتزلة
الجهمية
المجسمة
الكرامية
المشبهة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Ash'ari invented a new school and his followers follow him blindly.
Correction: Ibn Asakir clarifies that Ash'ari revived the Sunnah and his followers adhere to him based on evidence, not blind imitation.

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Asakir, why do followers attribute themselves to al-Ash'ari?
A) Because al-Ash'ari invented a revolutionary new doctrine that no one before him held.
B) To distinguish themselves from innovators and because his views are supported by evidence.
C) Because the four imams disagreed on usul al-din and Ash'ari reconciled them.
D) Because blind following (taqlid) is the only way to be saved.

Key Takeaway
Ash'ari revived the Sunnah, and following him is based on evidence, not blind imitation, with the purpose of distinguishing true Sunnis from innovators.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Ibn Asakir says they follow Ash'ari based on evidence, not blind imitation. Have you ever believed something in your aqeedah simply because you were taught it, without verifying the proof? Could you defend your creed from the Quran and Sunnah if asked?
?
The Sunnah had become obscure until Ash'ari revived it. In your own life, are there aspects of the Sunnah you have neglected or let become obscure? What is one specific practice you can revive today?
?
Ash'ari's school served to distinguish truth from the many innovating sects. Do you know how to distinguish correct Islamic belief from the deviations of the sects mentioned? Are you taking steps to learn the evidence so you are not swayed by falsehood?

The Four Imams Agree on Usul al-Din
Ibn Asakir asserts the unity of the four imams on usul and the universal monotheism of Ash'ari's position.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Asakir's assertion that the four imams are united on the principles of faith, especially tawhid and tanzih, and that Ash'ari followed their methodology, making all monotheists effectively 'Ash'ari' in this regard.

Core Idea
The four imams agree on tawhid and tanzih, and Ash'ari follows their path.

In this second part of the passage, Ibn Asakir addresses the claim that the four imams differ in the principles of religion. He insists they are united on tawhid and the negation of anthropomorphism (tashbih). Al-Ash'ari followed their path. Therefore, if affirming tanzih is called 'being Ash'ari,' then all monotheists are Ash'aris. This passage emphasizes the unity of the Sunni tradition on the essential creed.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the transition from the previous argument to this new point about the four imams.
2
Identify the claim of unity
Ibn Asakir explicitly states that the four imams are not differing in usul al-din; they are united on tawhid and tanzih.
This counters the accusation that Ash'ari diverged from them.
3
Understand the key terms
Tawhid (oneness), tanzih (transcendence), and nafy al-tashbih (negation of anthropomorphism) are the core agreed principles.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Ibn Asakir concludes that if tanzih is called 'tamash'ur', then all monotheists are Ash'aris, and no blame can be attached.
This is a powerful rhetorical move to show the orthodoxy of Ash'ari's position.

?

?

?

Imam al-Shafi'i's Poem on Love of Ahl al-Bayt
Ibn Asakir transmits Imam al-Shafi'i's poem affirming love for Ahl al-Bayt.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Shafi'i's poem affirms that love for Ahl al-Bayt is a Sunni virtue, not heretical rafd.

Core Idea
Love for the Prophet's family is not rafd; it is integral to faith.

Imam al-Shafi'i, a towering figure in Islamic law and theology, composed a powerful poem defending his love for the Prophet's family. This lesson presents the poem with its chain of narration, showing how the Ash'ari tradition upholds this love as orthodox. The poem uses a striking rhetorical device to reject any claim that loving Ahl al-Bayt is deviance.

Guided Path
1
Read the poem and its chain
First, read the Arabic poem and its literal translation. Notice the strong chain of narrators tracing back to al-Shafi'i himself. This chain adds authority to the text.
The chain includes al-Khatib al-Baghdadi, a famous hadith scholar.
2
Understand the term rafd
Rafd (رفض) was a term used for those who rejected the first three caliphs. Al-Shafi'i plays on this term to say: if loving the Prophet's family is called rafd, then I am a Rafidi. This is a rhetorical exaggeration to show how absurd the accusation is.
The poem is not endorsing Shia rafd but using irony.
3
Reflect on the rhetorical strategy
Consider why al-Shafi'i uses the conditional statement: 'If love of Ahl al-Bayt is rafd...' He forces the listener to question the very definition of rafd and to recognize that love of the family is mandatory.
4
Connect to Ash'ari orthodoxy
This poem is preserved by Ibn Asakir, a prominent Ash'ari historian. It serves as evidence that loving Ahl al-Bayt is central to Sunni Islam and not a sectarian innovation.
The poem aligns with the Ash'ari position of balanced love for the Prophet's family.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهذا كقول إمامنا الشافعي المطلبي ابن عم المصطفى النبي صلي الله عليه وسلم فيما أخبرنا الشيخ أبو القسم هبة الله بن عبد الله بن أحمد الواسطى ببغداد ، قال أنا أبو بكر أحمد بن على بن ثابت الخطيب قال أنا أبو سعد إسماعيل بن علي بن الحسن بن بندار بن المثنى الاستراباذى ببيت المقدس قال أخبرنا على بن الحسن بن حيويه الدامغانى قال أنا محمد بن محمد بن الأشعث ثنا الربيع هو ابن سليمان قال أنشدنا الشافعي رحمه الله : يا راكباً قف بالمحـصـب من منى واهتف بقاطن خيفها والناهض سحراً إذا فاض الحجيج إلى منى فيضاً كملتطم الفرات الفائض إن كان رفضاً حب آل محمد فليشهد الثقلان أنى رافضي
And this is like the saying of our Imam al-Shafi'i al-Muttalibi, cousin of the Chosen Prophet (may Allah bless him and grant him peace) — as informed to us by Shaykh Abu al-Qasim Hibat Allah ibn Abd Allah ibn Ahmad al-Wasiti in Baghdad, he said: Abu Bakr Ahmad ibn Ali ibn Thabit al-Khatib informed us, he said: Abu Sa'd Isma'il ibn Ali ibn al-Hasan ibn Bundar ibn al-Muthanna al-Ishtrabadhi informed us in Bayt al-Maqdis, he said: Ali ibn al-Hasan ibn Huwayh al-Damghani informed us, he said: Muhammad ibn Muhammad ibn al-Ash'ath informed us, he said: al-Rabi' — who is Ibn Sulayman — reported to us, he said: al-Shafi'i (may Allah have mercy on him) recited to us: 'O rider, stop at the pebbled place of Mina, and call out to those dwelling in its valley and those rising at dawn. When the pilgrims overflow into Mina at night, flowing like the overflowing torrent of the Euphrates. If love for the family of Muhammad is rafd, then let the two weighty ones bear witness that I am a Rafidi.'
Source pages p. 269

Commentary
This passage transmits a chain of narration for a poem by Imam al-Shafi'i. The chain is from Ibn Asakir back to al-Shafi'i through reliable narrators including al-Khatib al-Baghdadi. The poem itself is a defense of loving Ahl al-Bayt (the family of the Prophet). Al-Shafi'i describes a scene in Mina during Hajj and then declares that if loving the family of Muhammad is considered rafd (a term for the Shia rejection of the first three caliphs), then he proudly declares himself a Rafidi. This is a rhetorical device to emphasize that love of Ahl al-Bayt is not rafd but a fundamental part of faith. The poem serves as evidence that the Ash'ari position of loving the Prophet's family is sunni orthodoxy.

Key Terms
الشافعي
آل محمد
رفض
الثقلان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some assume that love for Ahl al-Bayt is exclusive to Shia Islam or implies rejection of the first caliphs.
Correction: Al-Shafi'i's poem shows that such love is central to Sunni Islam and not heretical rafd.

Checkpoint
What is the point of al-Shafi'i's poem?
A) To denounce love for Ahl al-Bayt as innovation.
B) To claim that love for Ahl al-Bayt qualifies one as a Rafidi.
C) To affirm that love for Ahl al-Bayt is not heretical rafd but praiseworthy.
D) To criticize the Ash'ari school.

Key Takeaway
Imam al-Shafi'i's poem demonstrates that love for the Prophet's family is a fundamental Sunni value, not a sign of deviance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If someone today accused you of being a 'Rafidi' simply because you openly express love for the Prophet's family, would your faith be strong enough to accept that label rather than hide your devotion? What holds you back from that level of public love for Ahl al-Bayt?
?
The poem describes pilgrims flowing like a flooding river to Mina. Does your heart ache with that same overwhelming yearning to honor the Prophet and his family, or has it become dull? What practical step can you take this week to manifest that love, even if it costs you reputation?
?
Imam al-Shafi'i was willing to be misunderstood by labeling himself a Rafidi in a hypothetical. Are you willing to be misunderstood by others for your love of Ahl al-Bayt? When you feel social pressure to downplay that love, what specific thought from this lesson can you recall to strengthen your resolve?

Defending the Ash'ari Creed in Verse
An anonymous poem defends the Ash'ari creed and refutes its detractors.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how this poem defends the Ash'ari creed by asserting its truth, universal scholarly acceptance, and the honor of being called Ash'ari.

Core Idea
The Ash'ari creed is well-guided and negating deficiencies from Allah is its truth.

The poem you are about to read was recited anonymously in defense of the Ash'ari school. It powerfully affirms that the Ash'ari creed is well-guided and embraced by all scholars. The poet also responds to the accusation of 'tamash'ur' (being Ash'ari) as an innovation, declaring that if affirming Allah's transcendence is called Ash'ari, then he proudly bears that label. This lesson unpacks the poem's arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the poem
Read the Arabic poem and its translation carefully. Notice the rhythm and rhyme.
Though poetic, its argument is clear.
2
Identify the main claims
The poem makes several claims: the creed is well-guided, scholars affirm it, detractors are ignorant liars, and being Ash'ari is honorable. Find each claim in the text.
List them in order of appearance.
3
Reflect on the final couplet
The poet turns the accusation around. Consider: what does he mean by 'if he who negates all deficiencies... you accuse him of tamash'ur'? How does this connect to tanzih (transcendence)?
This is the climax of the poem.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأنشدت لبعضهم في المعنى المتقدم : إن اعتقاد الأشعرى مسدد لا يمترى في الحق إلا ممتـرى . وبه يقول العالمون بأسرهم من بين ذى قلم وصاحب منبر والمدعون عليه غير مقاله ما فيهم إلا جهول مفترى قذر التعامي واعتصم بمقاله واعلم يقينا أنه القول السرى وارفض ملامة من نهاك بجهله عما يراه لأنه لم يشعر وإذا لحاك العاذلون فقل لهم قول أمرئ في دينه مستبصر إن كان من ينفى النقائص كلها عن ربه ترمونه بتمشعر وترونه ذا بدعة في عقله فليشهد الثقلان أني أشعرى
And I recited for some of them on the aforementioned meaning: 'Indeed the Ash'ari creed is well-guided, none doubts the truth except the doubter. And by it all scholars say, from among the possessor of a pen and the owner of a pulpit. And those who claim other than his article, none among them except an ignorant liar, foul of self, who feigns blindness; so cling to his article, and know for certain that it is the sound saying. And reject the blame of one who forbids you with his ignorance of what he sees because he does not perceive. And when the blamers blame you, say to them the saying of a man with insight in his religion: "If he who negates all deficiencies from his Lord, you accuse him of tamash'ur, and you consider him an innovator in his intellect, then let the two weighty ones bear witness that I am Ash'ari."'
Source pages p. 270

Commentary
This passage presents an anonymous poem in defense of the Ash'ari school. The poem begins by asserting that the Ash'ari creed is well-guided (musaddad) and that no one doubts its truth except the doubter. It claims that all scholars, whether writers or preachers, affirm it. Those who attribute false statements to Ash'ari are ignorant liars. The poet advises the reader to cling to Ash'ari's article and reject the blame of ignorant critics. The final couplet addresses the accusation of tamash'ur: if affirming Allah's transcendence (negating deficiencies) is called 'tamash'ur' and considered innovation, then the poet declares himself an Ash'ari before the two weighty witnesses (the jinn and mankind). This poem reinforces the earlier argument that tanzih is the truth and being called Ash'ari is honorable.

Key Terms
الأشعري
تمشعر
النقائص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some accuse Ash'aris of being innovators because they emphasize tanzih.
Correction: The poem argues that negating deficiencies from Allah is the truth, and being called Ash'ari is honorable.

Checkpoint
According to the poem, what is the status of the Ash'ari creed?
A) It is innovated and doubtful.
B) It is well-guided and accepted by all scholars.
C) It is only for the ignorant.
D) It is a rejected sect.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari creed is defended as the sound truth, and its adherents proudly affirm divine transcendence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Can you bear to be called an innovator or extremist by others if you are simply negating all deficiencies from Allah? Where does your allegiance truly lie—with people's approval or with the truth you know?
?
The poet declares his Ash'arism openly before jinn and mankind. Are you willing to publicly affirm a theological truth that might bring you scorn, or do you shy away from such boldness for fear of criticism?
?
When you hear someone accuse a person of 'tamash'ur' or being 'too strict' about Allah's transcendence, do you immediately distance yourself, or do you stand with the truth that negating deficiencies is essential? Examine your heart: are you more concerned with reputation or with defending tanzih?

Refutation of Abu Ali al-Ahwazi's Criticisms
Ibn Asakir dismisses al-Ahwazi's criticisms as baseless and malicious.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ibn Asakir refutes al-Ahwazi by discrediting his character rather than engaging his arguments.

Core Idea
A critic's moral flaws can discredit their accusations.

Ibn Asakir does not address al-Ahwazi's specific points; instead, he undermines the critic himself. This lesson shows a classical method of refutation by impugning the opponent's integrity and motives.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage slowly, then read the translation. Notice the strong language used by Ibn Asakir.
2
Identify rhetorical strategies
Look for how Ibn Asakir labels al-Ahwazi (e.g., 'possessor of defects') and uses conditional statements to question his character.
3
Consider the approach
Why does Ibn Asakir attack the person rather than the argument? Is this effective? Reflect on the context of defending Ash'ari orthodoxy.
This reveals the importance of adab in scholarly debate.
4
Connect to learning objective
Summarize how this passage achieves its goal without addressing any specific criticism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأما ما ذكره ذو المعايب والمخازى أبو على الحسن بن على ابن إبراهيم الأهوازي ) فمما لا عليه لبيب ولا يرعيه سمعه مصيب لأنه رجل قد يعرج تبينت عداوته لأهل الحق وشنآنه ويكفيك من كتابه ترجمته وعنوانه ، ولو كان من ذوى الديانات لم يتفرغ لذكبر المثالب ولو أنه من أولي المروات لاستحيا من تتبع المعايب ولو لا أنه وجدها كثيرة في نفسه لما اختلقها لمن ليس هو من أبناء جنسه
As for what was mentioned by the possessor of defects and disgraces, Abu Ali al-Hasan ibn Ali ibn Ibrahim al-Ahwazi — it is something that no intelligent person would care about, nor would a perceptive listener pay attention to. For he is a man who limps, his enmity and hatred towards the people of truth have become evident. His book's biography and its title suffice you. If he were among the religious, he would not have occupied himself with enumerating defects. If he were among the chivalrous, he would have been ashamed to pursue faults. And if he had not found many faults in himself, he would not have fabricated them against someone who is not of his kind.
Source pages p. 270

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the criticisms made by Abu Ali al-Ahwazi against the Ash'aris. Ibn Asakir begins by dismissing al-Ahwazi's claims as unworthy of any intelligent or perceptive person's attention. He describes al-Ahwazi as a man who limps (a metaphor for spiritual or moral deficiency) and whose enmity towards the people of truth is clear. Ibn Asakir says that the very title and content of al-Ahwazi's book reveal his bias. He argues that a truly religious person would not waste time collecting faults, and a chivalrous person would be ashamed to pursue them. Ibn Asakir concludes that al-Ahwazi projects his own many faults onto others, fabricating accusations against those who are not like him.

المثالب

Refuting al-Ahwazi's Character
Ibn Asakir dismisses al-Ahwazi's credibility based on his character.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ibn Asakir discredits an opponent by attacking his character and motivations, and why such refutation is considered valid in scholarly discourse.

Core Idea
An opponent's moral flaws can disqualify his criticism of others.

Ibn Asakir begins his refutation of al-Ahwazi by focusing not on his arguments but on his character. He uses strong language to dismiss al-Ahwazi as a man of faults and disgraces, arguing that his enmity to truth is evident. The passage sets the stage for a more substantive refutation by questioning the critic's credibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on al-Ahwazi
Read the first part of the source (from 'As for what was mentioned...' to '...not of his kind'). Notice how Ibn Asakir describes al-Ahwazi and the reasons he gives for dismissing him.
The language is deliberately harsh to undermine the opponent's authority.
2
Identify the three conditional statements
Ibn Asakir uses three 'if-then' statements: If he were religious, he wouldn't mention faults; if he were magnanimous, he'd be ashamed; if he weren't full of faults, he wouldn't fabricate. Understand the logic: he implies al-Ahwazi lacks these virtues.
This is a form of ad hominem argument, but in this context it is meant to expose hypocrisy.
3
Reflect on the purpose
Why does Ibn Asakir attack the person rather than the argument? Consider that questioning the integrity of a critic can be a valid scholarly move if the critic's character is well-known.
This prepares the reader for the anecdote that follows.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأما ما ذكره ذو المعايب والمخازى أبو على الحسن بن على ابن إبراهيم الأهوازي فمما لا عليه لبيب ولا يرعيه سمعه مصيب لأنه رجل قد يعرج تبينت عداوته لأهل الحق وشنآنه ويكفيك من كتابه ترجمته وعنوانه ، ولو كان من ذوى الديانات لم يتفرغ لذكبر المثالب ولو أنه من أولي المروات لاستحيا من تتبع المعايب ولو لا أنه وجدها كثيرة في نفسه لما اختلقها لمن ليس هو من أبناء جنسه . وقد أخبرنا الشيخ أبو القسم هبة الله بن محمد بن عبد الواحد بن الحصين الشيباني ببغداد قال أنا القاضي أبو القسم على ابن المحسن بن على التنوخى قال ثنا أبو الحسن علی بن محمد بن السرى لفظه قال أنا أبو بكر بن دريد قال أنا أبو حاتم يعني السجستاني عن العتبى ، قال سمعت أعرابياً من تنوخ يقول لآخر وسمعه يعيب قوماً قد استدللت على كثرة عيوبك بكثرة ذكرك الناس فإن الطالب لها يطلبها بقدر ما فيه منها ثم أنشده :
واجرأ ما رأيت بظهر غيب على ذكر العيوب ذوو العيوب
، وروى غيره ) على عيب الرجال ذوو العيوب )
As for what was mentioned by the man of faults and disgraces, Abu 'Ali al-Hasan ibn 'Ali ibn Ibrahim al-Ahwazi — it is something no intelligent person would pay attention to, nor would a correct listener heed it, because he is a man who limps. His enmity towards the people of truth and his hatred have become clear to me. Sufficient for you from his book is its translation and title. If he were among the religious, he would not have devoted himself to mentioning faults. If he were among the magnanimous, he would have been ashamed to pursue defects. And if he did not find many faults in himself, he would not have fabricated them for those who are not of his kind.
And Shaykh Abu al-Qasim Hibat Allah ibn Muhammad ibn 'Abd al-Wahid ibn al-Husayn al-Shaybani informed us in Baghdad, saying: The judge Abu al-Qasim 'Ali ibn al-Muhsin ibn 'Ali al-Tanukhi informed us, saying: Abu al-Hasan 'Ali ibn Muhammad ibn al-Sari narrated to us orally, saying: Abu Bakr ibn Duraid narrated to us, saying: Abu Hatim — meaning al-Sijistani — narrated to us from al-'Utbi, who said: I heard an Arab from Tanukh say to another, having heard him criticizing a people: 'I have deduced the abundance of your own faults from your frequent mention of people. For the seeker of faults seeks them according to what he himself possesses.' Then he recited:
'And the most audacious thing I have seen behind the back is the mention of faults by the faulty ones.'
And someone else narrated: 'against the faults of men, the faulty ones.'
Source pages p. 270

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of al-Ahwazi's credibility. Ibn Asakir begins by dismissing him as a man of faults and disgraces, stating that no intelligent person would heed him. He emphasizes that his enmity to the truth is clear, and his book alone suffices to reveal its poor quality. The author argues that if al-Ahwazi were religious or honorable, he would not waste time finding faults in others, and if he were not full of faults himself, he would not fabricate them for others. Then an anecdote is cited through a chain of transmission: an Arab observed that a person frequently criticizing others reveals his own faults, because a fault-finder seeks faults according to what he himself has. The Arab then recites a poetic line stating that the most audacious thing is for the faulty to mention others' faults. This supports the refutation by a universal principle.

Key Terms
الأهوازي
المعايب
المثالب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does Ibn Asakir say would be true if al-Ahwazi were religious?
A) He would not write books.
B) He would not devote himself to mentioning faults.
C) He would be a scholar.
D) He would praise others.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Asakir discredits al-Ahwazi by arguing that his obsession with others' faults reflects his own moral defects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Before you criticize someone's character, have you honestly examined your own faults and asked yourself whether you are projecting your own shortcomings? Ibn Asakir argues that al-Ahwazi's focus on others' defects reveals his own. What would a review of your recent criticisms reveal about you? Commitment: For the next week, before you speak a word of criticism, write down one of your own faults first.
?
Think of a time when you used a person's minor flaws to dismiss their valid point or legitimate criticism. How did that reflect on your character? The passage says: 'If he were among the magnanimous, he would have been ashamed to pursue defects.' Are you ashamed yet? Commitment: Apologize to that person or at least acknowledge your deflection to yourself.
?
If everything you have said about others were compiled into a book, what would its title be? Would it be a book of faults like al-Ahwazi's, or a book of building others up? The passage says: 'Sufficient for you from his book is its translation and title.' Your words are your book. What does the title say about the state of your heart? Commitment: Today, speak only words that you would be proud to have as the title of your book.

The Arab Anecdote on Fault-Finding
An Arab anecdote illustrating that fault-finding reflects the critic's own faults.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the lesson of the Arab anecdote: that a person who frequently criticizes others often does so because of his own faults.

Core Idea
Fault-finding reveals the critic's own defects.

After dismissing al-Ahwazi's character, Ibn Asakir supports his argument with an anecdote from Arab tradition. A Bedouin observes that a person who constantly mentions others' faults is actually revealing the abundance of his own. This universal truth reinforces the refutation and provides a memorable lesson.

Guided Path
1
Read the anecdote
Read the anecdote from 'And Shaykh Abu al-Qasim...' to the end. Notice the chain of transmission and the main point: the Bedouin's deduction and the line of poetry.
The chain shows the story's authenticity in Islamic scholarship.
2
Analyze the Bedouin's statement
The Bedouin says: 'I have deduced the abundance of your own faults from your frequent mention of people.' Understand the logic: the seeker of faults finds them according to what he himself possesses.
This is a psychological insight: projection of one's own flaws onto others.
3
Reflect on the poetry
The line of poetry says: 'And the most audacious thing I have seen behind the back is the mention of faults by the faulty ones.' Consider why it is audacious and how it supports the Bedouin's point.
The word 'audacious' implies hypocrisy and shamelessness.

Refuting the Claim of Reversed Religion
Ibn Asakir refutes the critic and praises al-Ash'ari's merit.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ibn Asakir refutes the accusation that religion is reversed by exposing the opponent's ignorance and contrasting him with the excellence of al-Ash'ari.

Core Idea
A claim reversed: the critic's ignorance reverses truth, not religion.

Ibn Asakir begins by acknowledging the wording of his opponent's accusation but then turns the tables, arguing that the real reversal is in the critic's own understanding. He skillfully contrasts the critic's lowly status with the towering merit of Imam al-Ash'ari, using vivid imagery of the full moon and a dim star.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then read the English translation. Notice the rhetorical structure.
The passage is a single continuous argument.
2
Identify Ibn Asakir's rhetorical strategy
Notice that he first grants the opponent's wording is accurate but then denies the intention and criticizes the opponent's self-knowledge.
This is a classic dialectical move.
3
Examine the conditionals
Ibn Asakir uses a series of 'if not' statements to argue that the opponent's own ignorance and the scarcity of scholars allowed his views to spread.
These conditionals turn the accusation back on the accuser.
4
Appreciate the contrast with al-Ash'ari
The passage concludes with a powerful simile: al-Ash'ari is like the full moon compared to a dim star, emphasizing his superiority.
This elevates the discussion from refutation to praise.
5
Reflect on the lesson's core idea
Consider how Ibn Asakir transforms the critic's claim of reversal into evidence of the critic's own reversed understanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأما قوله قد رأيت الأمر فى الدين منعكسا بضده والتفريط فيه خارجاً عن حده وصارت الرؤوس اعجازا والاكثار من الباطل إيجازا وكثر السفهاء وقل العلماء . فإنه قد أصاب فى اللفظ وإن كان أخطأ في القصد و جهل قدر نفسه حين غمض العلماء حتى خرج في ذلك عن الحد ولولا أن الأمر صار منعكسا والحق عند الجهال عاد مندرسا لما كان أعجمي من أهل الأهواز لا يفرق بين الحقيقة والمجاز ، ولا يعرف ما معنى الإيجاز ، ينزل الرؤوس بمنزلة الأعجاز ويحمل الجهال والسفهاء على أن يذموا العلماء والفقهاء ولولا تفريطه فى طلب العلم والحكمة لترك إفراطه في ذم العلماء والأئمة ولما جهل من اشتهر بالعلم بين الخلق وضلل من عرف بنصرة الحق ولولا كثرة أعوانه السفهاء، كما زعم لكف عن غلوائه في قوله ، وأن رغم ولم يسمع قول خوزى غبى فى حق عالم أحوزى عربي ولولا قلة العلماء في عصره كما ذكر لما أهمل كشف أمره حتى انتشر وإلا فالفرق بينه وبين شيخنا أبي الحسن من الحال الواضح والأمر البين ، وفضل أبي الحسن رحمة الله عليه عند أولى النهى كفضل القمر ليلة البدر على السهمى ومتى وز الأهواز يعيبون عرب البصرة ، وكيف يتهم أولاد المجوس بالالحاد والزندقة أبناء ذوى الهجرة ولا شك أن الأهواز من جملة البلدان التي افتتحها أبو موسى الأشعرى جد هذا الإمام ، وكذلك أصبهان وغيرها مما افتتح على يديه رحمه الله من الفتوح العظام واختلف في كيفية استيلاء أبي موسى علي الأهواز فتحا فقيل افتتحها بالسيف عنوة ، وقيل بل افتتحها صلحا والأصح قول من قال أنها افتتحت على وجه العنوة وذلك السبب عندى هو الموجب لهذه الحفوة والمورث للغلظة على ولده والقسوة والمؤثر في شدة النفور عن معتقده ، والنبوة لأنه ادخل على أسلاف كان
As for his saying: 'I have seen the matter in religion reversed with its opposite, and negligence going beyond its limit, and heads become tails, and abundance of falsehood becomes brevity, and fools become many and scholars few.' Indeed, he hit the mark in wording, but he missed the intention, and he was ignorant of his own worth when he disparaged scholars until he went beyond the limit. If the matter were not reversed and truth were not almost obliterated among the ignorant, a non-Arab from Ahwaz who cannot distinguish between reality and metaphor, and does not know the meaning of brevity, would not descend heads to the position of tails, nor would he incite the ignorant and foolish to disparage scholars and jurists. If he had not neglected seeking knowledge and wisdom, he would have abandoned his excess in disparaging scholars and imams, and he would not have been ignorant of one famous for knowledge among people, nor would he have declared misguided one known for supporting truth. If it were not for the abundance of his foolish supporters, as he claims, he would have refrained from his extremism in his speech—but he insisted and did not listen to the saying of a stupid Khuzi against a learned Ahwazi Arab. If it were not for the scarcity of scholars in his time, as he mentioned, his case would not have been neglected until it spread. Otherwise, the difference between him and our shaykh Abu al-Hasan is clear and evident. The merit of Abu al-Hasan, may Allah have mercy on him, in the eyes of the intelligent is like the merit of the full moon over the star Suhayy. When will the Ahwazis ever disparage the Arabs of Basra? How can the children of Zoroastrians accuse the children of the Emigrants of heresy and disbelief? There is no doubt that Ahwaz is among the countries conquered by Abu Musa al-Ash'ari, the grandfather of this Imam (al-Ash'ari), as well as Isfahan and others, which were conquered at his hands—may Allah have mercy on him—of great conquests. There is disagreement on how Abu Musa subdued Ahwaz: it is said he conquered it by the sword forcefully, and it is said he conquered it by peace treaty. The most correct is the saying of those who said it was conquered forcefully. This reason, in my opinion, is what necessitated this hostility and inherited harshness towards his descendants and severity, and the cause of strong aversion to his creed and prophethood, because he entered upon his ancestors... (incomplete)
Source pages p. 271

Commentary
This passage is a detailed refutation of al-Ahwazi's claim that religion is reversed, with heads becoming tails and fools outnumbering scholars. Ibn Asakir first grants that the wording is accurate but says the intention is wrong, because the opponent himself is ignorant. He argues that if truth were not indeed reversed in the eyes of the ignorant, an ignorant non-Arab from Ahwaz would not disparage scholars and praise fools. He blames the opponent's neglect of knowledge for his excess in blaming scholars. He then contrasts the opponent with his own shaykh, Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari, likening his superiority to the full moon over a dim star. Finally, he uses a historical argument: Ahwaz was conquered by force by Abu Musa al-Ash'ari, the ancestor of Imam al-Ash'ari. This conquest by force, according to Ibn Asakir, is the reason for the animosity of the people of Ahwaz towards al-Ash'ari's descendants and their creed, as they inherited a grudge from that conquest. The passage ends incomplete but clearly aims to explain the origin of the opponent's hostility.

Key Terms
الأهواز
الغلو
السهمى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The critic believes he has identified a genuine reversal in religion where scholars are few and fools are many.
Correction: Ibn Asakir shows that the apparent reversal is due to the critic's own ignorance and the abundance of foolish supporters, not an actual change in religious truth.

Checkpoint
How does Ibn Asakir characterize the difference between al-Ash'ari and the critic?
A) The critic is like a full moon, al-Ash'ari like a dim star.
B) Al-Ash'ari is like the full moon, the critic like the star Suhayy.
C) Both are equally learned.
D) The critic is superior in rhetoric.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Asakir defends al-Ash'ari by turning the critic's own words against him and exposing the true source of the supposed reversal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever been quick to accuse a scholar or teacher of error without first examining your own ignorance in the matter? What would it take for you to admit that the flaw might be in your understanding rather than in the person you criticize?
?
Ibn Asakir shows that the critic's own negligence in seeking knowledge led him to disparage scholars. Where in your life have you let laziness in learning lead you to dismiss those more knowledgeable? Will you recommit to seeking knowledge before judging?
?
The critic saw heads as tails. Is there a person of knowledge or piety whom you have undervalued because of your own assumptions? How can you ensure you see people as they truly are, especially those whom Allah has blessed with understanding?

The Historical Root of Hostility: The Conquest of Ahwaz
Ibn Asakir explains the historical origin of hostility against al-Ash'ari.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Asakir's historical argument that the hostility of the Ahwazis towards al-Ash'ari's school stems from the forcible conquest of their city by al-Ash'ari's ancestor.

Core Idea
Grudges from war outlast theological debates.

Having refuted the opponent's theological accusations, Ibn Asakir now unveils what he considers the true source of hostility: historical resentment from the conquest of Ahwaz by Abu Musa al-Ash'ari, the ancestor of Imam al-Ash'ari. This argument shifts the discussion from doctrine to psychology and history.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Focus on the rhetorical questions and the historical narrative about Abu Musa al-Ash'ari.
The passage begins with two rhetorical questions challenging the Ahwazis.
2
Understand the two rhetorical questions
Ibn Asakir asks: 'When will the Ahwazis disparage the Arabs of Basra?' and 'How can the sons of Zoroastrians accuse the sons of the Emigrants of heresy?' These questions invert the critic's implied superiority.
The critic was from Ahwaz, which was non-Arab and had Zoroastrian roots.
3
Learn the historical fact about Ahwaz
Ibn Asakir states that Ahwaz was conquered by Abu Musa al-Ash'ari, the grandfather of Imam al-Ash'ari, and that there is a dispute whether it was by force or treaty, but the correct view is by force.
This is the key historical claim.
4
Connect conquest to hostility
Ibn Asakir asserts that this forcible conquest is the cause ('al-sabab') of the harshness, aversion, and hatred of the Ahwazis towards al-Ash'ari's descendants and creed.
The argument is that the descendants of the conquered bear a grudge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأما قوله قد رأيت الأمر فى الدين منعكسا بضده والتفريط فيه خارجاً عن حده وصارت الرؤوس اعجازا والاكثار من الباطل إيجازا وكثر السفهاء وقل العلماء . فإنه قد أصاب فى اللفظ وإن كان أخطأ في القصد و جهل قدر نفسه حين غمض العلماء حتى خرج في ذلك عن الحد ولولا أن الأمر صار منعكسا والحق عند الجهال عاد مندرسا لما كان أعجمي من أهل الأهواز لا يفرق بين الحقيقة والمجاز ، ولا يعرف ما معنى الإيجاز ، ينزل الرؤوس بمنزلة الأعجاز ويحمل الجهال والسفهاء على أن يذموا العلماء والفقهاء ولولا تفريطه فى طلب العلم والحكمة لترك إفراطه في ذم العلماء والأئمة ولما جهل من اشتهر بالعلم بين الخلق وضلل من عرف بنصرة الحق ولولا كثرة أعوانه السفهاء، كما زعم لكف عن غلوائه في قوله ، وأن رغم ولم يسمع قول خوزى غبى فى حق عالم أحوزى عربي ولولا قلة العلماء في عصره كما ذكر لما أهمل كشف أمره حتى انتشر وإلا فالفرق بينه وبين شيخنا أبي الحسن من الحال الواضح والأمر البين ، وفضل أبي الحسن رحمة الله عليه عند أولى النهى كفضل القمر ليلة البدر على السهمى ومتى وز الأهواز يعيبون عرب البصرة ، وكيف يتهم أولاد المجوس بالالحاد والزندقة أبناء ذوى الهجرة ولا شك أن الأهواز من جملة البلدان التي افتتحها أبو موسى الأشعرى جد هذا الإمام ، وكذلك أصبهان وغيرها مما افتتح على يديه رحمه الله من الفتوح العظام واختلف في كيفية استيلاء أبي موسى علي الأهواز فتحا فقيل افتتحها بالسيف عنوة ، وقيل بل افتتحها صلحا والأصح قول من قال أنها افتتحت على وجه العنوة وذلك السبب عندى هو الموجب لهذه الحفوة والمورث للغلظة على ولده والقسوة والمؤثر في شدة النفور عن معتقده ، والنبوة لأنه ادخل على أسلاف كان
As for his saying: 'I have seen the matter in religion reversed with its opposite, and negligence going beyond its limit, and heads become tails, and abundance of falsehood becomes brevity, and fools become many and scholars few.' Indeed, he hit the mark in wording, but he missed the intention, and he was ignorant of his own worth when he disparaged scholars until he went beyond the limit. If the matter were not reversed and truth were not almost obliterated among the ignorant, a non-Arab from Ahwaz who cannot distinguish between reality and metaphor, and does not know the meaning of brevity, would not descend heads to the position of tails, nor would he incite the ignorant and foolish to disparage scholars and jurists. If he had not neglected seeking knowledge and wisdom, he would have abandoned his excess in disparaging scholars and imams, and he would not have been ignorant of one famous for knowledge among people, nor would he have declared misguided one known for supporting truth. If it were not for the abundance of his foolish supporters, as he claims, he would have refrained from his extremism in his speech—but he insisted and did not listen to the saying of a stupid Khuzi against a learned Ahwazi Arab. If it were not for the scarcity of scholars in his time, as he mentioned, his case would not have been neglected until it spread. Otherwise, the difference between him and our shaykh Abu al-Hasan is clear and evident. The merit of Abu al-Hasan, may Allah have mercy on him, in the eyes of the intelligent is like the merit of the full moon over the star Suhayy. When will the Ahwazis ever disparage the Arabs of Basra? How can the children of Zoroastrians accuse the children of the Emigrants of heresy and disbelief? There is no doubt that Ahwaz is among the countries conquered by Abu Musa al-Ash'ari, the grandfather of this Imam (al-Ash'ari), as well as Isfahan and others, which were conquered at his hands—may Allah have mercy on him—of great conquests. There is disagreement on how Abu Musa subdued Ahwaz: it is said he conquered it by the sword forcefully, and it is said he conquered it by peace treaty. The most correct is the saying of those who said it was conquered forcefully. This reason, in my opinion, is what necessitated this hostility and inherited harshness towards his descendants and severity, and the cause of strong aversion to his creed and prophethood, because he entered upon his ancestors... (incomplete)
Source pages p. 271

Commentary
This passage is a detailed refutation of al-Ahwazi's claim that religion is reversed, with heads becoming tails and fools outnumbering scholars. Ibn Asakir first grants that the wording is accurate but says the intention is wrong, because the opponent himself is ignorant. He argues that if truth were not indeed reversed in the eyes of the ignorant, an ignorant non-Arab from Ahwaz would not disparage scholars and praise fools. He blames the opponent's neglect of knowledge for his excess in blaming scholars. He then contrasts the opponent with his own shaykh, Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari, likening his superiority to the full moon over a dim star. Finally, he uses a historical argument: Ahwaz was conquered by force by Abu Musa al-Ash'ari, the ancestor of Imam al-Ash'ari. This conquest by force, according to Ibn Asakir, is the reason for the animosity of the people of Ahwaz towards al-Ash'ari's descendants and their creed, as they inherited a grudge from that conquest. The passage ends incomplete but clearly aims to explain the origin of the opponent's hostility.

Misconception: The hostility towards al-Ash'ari is based on sound theological disagreement.

?

Will you investigate the true source of that feeling?
?

Examine your own heart: are there convictions you hold that might be more about loyalty to your group's history than about the truth itself? What would it cost you to separate your identity from those inherited biases?
?
The children of Zoroastrians accused the children of Emigrants of heresy—a charge that reflected more on their own history than on the accused. When you accuse someone of error, are you projecting a wound from your own past? Ask Allah to show you your blind spots and grant you the courage to see them.

The Attacker's Contradiction on the Reversal of Affairs
Ibn Asakir rebukes the attacker's claim that religious matters are reversed.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ibn Asakir refutes an attacker's claim about religion being reversed by turning the accusation back on the attacker himself.

Core Idea
The very accusation of reversal reverses onto the accuser himself.

Ibn Asakir faces an accusation that religious matters are turned upside down. Instead of simply denying it, he accepts the wording but reveals the true reversal lies in the accuser—an ignorant man disparaging true scholars. This lesson unpacks that masterful rhetorical move.

Guided Path
1
Read the attacker's claim
Read the first part of the passage where the attacker states his observation. Notice the wording: 'heads become tails, fools increase, scholars decrease.'
Ibn Asakir acknowledges the accuracy of the wording but not the intent.
2
Identify Ibn Asakir's critique
Find where Ibn Asakir turns the tables: he says the attacker himself is the example of this reversal. An ignorant non-Arab from Ahwaz is criticizing scholars.
This is the core of the refutation.
3
Understand the conditional arguments
Examine the 'were it not for' statements. They show that the attacker's own negligence and lack of knowledge cause his extremism.
These conditionals strengthen the rebuttal.
4
Reflect on the irony
Notice the irony: the attacker's complaint about reversal is true, but only because he himself has reversed the proper order by attacking the learned while being ignorant.
Ibn Asakir uses the attacker's own words against him.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأما قوله قد رأيت الأمر فى الدين منعكسا بضده والتفريط فيه خارجاً عن حده وصارت الرؤوس اعجازا والاكثار من الباطل إيجازا وكثر السفهاء وقل العلماء . فإنه قد أصاب فى اللفظ وإن كان أخطأ في القصد و جهل قدر نفسه حين غمض العلماء حتى خرج في ذلك عن الحد ولولا أن الأمر صار منعكسا والحق عند الجهال عاد مندرسا لما كان أعجمي من أهل الأهواز لا يفرق بين الحقيقة والمجاز ، ولا يعرف ما معنى الإيجاز ، ينزل الرؤوس بمنزلة الأعجاز ويحمل الجهال والسفهاء على أن يذموا العلماء والفقهاء ولولا تفريطه فى طلب العلم والحكمة لترك إفراطه في ذم العلماء والأئمة ولما جهل من اشتهر بالعلم بين الخلق وضلل من عرف بنصرة الحق ولولا كثرة أعوانه السفهاء، كما زعم لكف عن غلوائه في قوله ، وأن رغم ولم يسمع قول خوزى غبى فى حق عالم أحوزى عربي
As for his statement: 'I have seen the matter in religion reversed to its opposite, excess outside its limit, heads become tails, much falsehood considered conciseness, fools increase and scholars decrease' – he has indeed hit the mark in wording although he erred in intent and was ignorant of his own worth when he disparaged scholars until he transgressed the limit. And were it not that the matter has become reversed and truth among the ignorant has become obliterated, a non-Arab from Ahwaz who cannot distinguish reality from metaphor and does not know the meaning of conciseness would not lower heads to the level of tails and incite the ignorant and fools to disparage scholars and jurists. And were it not for his negligence in seeking knowledge and wisdom, he would have abandoned his excess in disparaging scholars and imams, and would not have ignorantly criticized those renowned for knowledge among creation and misguidely condemned those known for supporting truth. And were it not for the abundance of his ignorant supporters, as he claims, he would have refrained from his extremism in his statement, even if it displeases him, and he would not have heard the saying of a stupid Khuzi against a deserving Arab scholar.
Source pages p. 271

Commentary
This passage is a rebuttal of an accusation made by the attacker. The attacker claimed that in religion matters are reversed: scholars are few, fools are many, truth is considered falsehood, etc. Ibn Asakir responds by agreeing with the wording of the statement but pointing out that the attacker misapplies it. The reversal, Ibn Asakir explains, is actually evident in the attacker himself: a non-Arab from Ahwaz who cannot distinguish truth from metaphor dares to criticize scholars. The attacker's own ignorance is proof that the reversal is in him. Ibn Asakir further argues that if the attacker had pursued knowledge himself, he would not have overstepped in condemning scholars. He also notes that the attacker relies on ignorant supporters, which enables his extremism. The overall point is that the attacker's critique ironically applies to himself, not to the scholars he attacks.

انعكاس
تفريط
إفراط
جهال
علماء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think Ibn Asakir agrees with the attacker's claim entirely.
Correction: Ibn Asakir only agrees with the wording but then demonstrates that the reversal applies to the attacker himself, not to the religious situation as a whole.

Checkpoint

A) The attacker himself exemplifies the reversal by being an ignorant critic of scholars.
B) The scholars have actually become ignorant.
C) The religion has truly been reversed and scholars are to blame.
D) The attacker's supporters are the true scholars.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Asakir masterfully turns the attacker's accusation of religious reversal back on the accuser, showing that the true reversal lies in the accuser's own ignorance and audacity to criticize learned scholars.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The attacker relied on ignorant supporters to bolster his extremism. In your own life, do you surround yourself with people who encourage your ignorance or those who push you towards knowledge and truth? Name one person you can turn to for honest correction.

The Inherited Enmity Behind the Attack on al-Ash'ari
Ibn Asakir explains the attacker's enmity through history of Ahwaz conquest.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ibn Asakir uses historical and genealogical reasoning to explain the attacker's hostility as inherited enmity from the conquest of Ahwaz.

Core Idea
Inherited hatred, not theology, drives the attacker's criticism.

Ibn Asakir offers a unique explanation for why someone would attack Imam al-Ash'ari so fiercely. Rather than focusing on theological disagreements, he traces the hostility back to a historical event: the conquest of Ahwaz by Abu Musa al-Ash'ari, which brought calamity upon the attacker's Magian ancestors. This lesson explores that reasoning and its implications for evaluating the attacker's motives.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and English translation to grasp the historical context and Ibn Asakir's argument.
Pay attention to the mention of Ahwaz, Abu Musa al-Ash'ari, and the Magian ancestors.
2
Identify the key claim
Find where Ibn Asakir states that the attacker's ancestors were Magians who suffered from the conquest, and how that led to inherited enmity.
3
Understand the inheritance of hatred
Note Ibn Asakir's statement that 'hatred is inherited and love is inherited'. Reflect on how this explains the attacker's vendetta against al-Ash'ari.
Consider why the attacker cannot attack Abu Musa directly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا شك أن الأهواز من جملة البلدان التي افتتحها أبو موسى الأشعرى جد هذا الإمام ، وكذلك أصبهان وغيرها مما افتتح على يديه رحمه الله من الفتوح العظام واختلف في كيفية استيلاء أبي موسى علي الأهواز فتحا فقيل افتتحها بالسيف عنوة ، وقيل بل افتتحها صلحا والأصح قول من قال أنها افتتحت على وجه العنوة وذلك السبب عندى هو الموجب لهذه الحفوة والمورث للغلظة على ولده والقسوة والمؤثر في شدة النفور عن معتقده ، والنبوة لأنه ادخل على أسلاف الأهوازي من المجوس بلية ومحنة أورثت قلبه لنسله عداوة وأحسنة فلهذا استفرغ جهده في الازراء على أبي الحسن والتشنيع ، ورماه بكل ما أمكنه ذكره من الأمر الشنيع لأن البغض يتوارث والود يتوارث فلذلك تجاوز في عداوته الحد لأنه لما لم يتجاسر على أن يطعن في أبي موسى ويعيب أمره شفى بما ذكره في ولده أبي الحسن رحمه الله صدره
And there is no doubt that Ahwaz is among the countries conquered by Abu Musa al-Ash'ari, the grandfather of this imam (al-Ash'ari), as well as Isfahan and others conquered at his hands, may Allah have mercy on him, among the great conquests. There is disagreement over how Abu Musa took Ahwaz: it is said he conquered it by force with the sword, and it is said he conquered it by peace treaty. The more correct view is that it was conquered by force. And that reason, in my view, is what necessitated this disrespect and inherited harshness towards his offspring and severity, and caused intense aversion to his doctrine, because he brought upon the ancestors of this Ahwazi from the Magians calamity and tribulation that caused his heart to inherit enmity towards his descendants. Therefore he exerted his utmost effort in disparaging Abu al-Hasan and slandering him, and accused him of every shameful thing he could mention, because hatred is inherited and love is inherited. Thus he exceeded in his enmity beyond the limit, because when he dared not attack Abu Musa and criticize his affair, he vented his rage by what he mentioned against his son Abu al-Hasan, may Allah have mercy on him.
Source pages p. 271

Commentary
This passage provides a historical and psychological explanation for the attacker's hostility. Ibn Asakir states that Ahwaz was conquered by force by Abu Musa al-Ash'ari, the grandfather of Imam al-Ash'ari. The conquest brought hardship upon the ancestors of the attacker, who were Magians (Zoroastrians). This enmity is inherited, and since the attacker cannot directly attack Abu Musa, he instead attacks his descendant, Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari. Thus the attacker's criticism is not based on religious merit but on ancestral grudge. Ibn Asakir uses this to discredit the attacker's motives.

Rebuttal on the Scarcity of Unveilers of Resemblance
Ibn Asakir refutes the claim that those rejecting anthropomorphism have become extinct.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ibn Asakir uses a logical inversion to refute the claim that refuters of anthropomorphism are extinct.

Core Idea
The opponent's error proves the scarcity of truth, not its absence.

In this brief but powerful rebuttal, Ibn Asakir turns the tables on his opponent. The opponent laments that those who expose the error of anthropomorphism have died out. Ibn Asakir points out that if they were truly absent, the opponent would not be able to persist in his mistaken belief. The very existence of his error is evidence that the correct scholars are few, not extinct.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Notice the concise structure.
2
Identify the opponent's claim
The opponent says 'the unveilers of resemblance have become extinct.' What does this mean?
3
Analyze Ibn Asakir's rebuttal
He uses a conditional: 'if they were not few, he would not have believed...' How does this refute the opponent?
This is a logical inversion.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why scarcity of scholars does not mean the truth is lost. How does this apply to defending orthodoxy?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قوله ( وأندرس الكاشفون للنشبه ) فلولا قلتهم لم يعتقد ما كان عليه من الاعتقاد المشتبه
As for his statement: 'The unveilers of resemblance have become extinct' – if they were not few, he would not have believed what he believed of ambiguous creed.
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This is a short but sharp refutation. The attacker claimed that those who expose and refute anthropomorphism (tashbih) have disappeared. Ibn Asakir turns the argument around: if those 'unveilers' were not scarce, the attacker himself would not hold the ambiguous creed he holds. In other words, the attacker's very existence holding a mistaken belief is proof that the correct scholars are few, not that they are extinct. The statement is ironic: the attacker bemoans the scarcity of truth, but that scarcity is what allows his error to persist.

Key Terms
اندراس
الكاشفون للنشبه
اعتقاد مشتبه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The opponent thinks that because few refuters remain, the truth is lost.
Correction: Scarcity does not equal extinction; the opponent's own error proves that those who could correct him exist but are few.

Checkpoint
How does Ibn Asakir refute the claim that 'unveilers of resemblance' have become extinct?
A) He lists many such scholars.
B) He says if they were not few, the attacker would not hold his ambiguous creed.
C) He argues that extinction is impossible.
D) He ignores the claim.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Asakir shows that the opponent's error is itself proof that the refuters of anthropomorphism are scarce, not extinct.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear someone claim that true Islamic scholarship has vanished, does that thought ever shake your confidence in the survival of truth? How does Ibn Asakir's argument force you to see that your own doubts might actually prove the opposite of what you fear?
?
Have you ever felt that because you see so few people correcting theological errors around you, the correct path must be lost? This lesson shows that the existence of error itself is evidence that truth's defenders are still present. Can you honestly examine your heart: do you use the scarcity of scholars as an excuse to stop seeking truth or to justify complacency?
?
Imagine standing before Allah knowing that you once believed falsehood was widespread and truth extinct. This passage holds you accountable: if error exists, it is only because truth is scarce—not gone. Will you now take the responsibility to be among the few who unveil the truth, or will you remain passive, letting the scarcity of da'wah continue?

The Contradiction of Claiming Few Followers of Sunnah
Ibn Asakir highlights a contradiction in the attacker's statements.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ibn Asakir exposes logical inconsistency in his opponent's argument.

Core Idea
Inconsistency exposes falsehood.

The attacker tries to belittle the followers of the Sunnah while simultaneously claiming his own doctrine is followed by the majority. Ibn Asakir pounces on this contradiction, showing that the attacker's own words refute each other. This passage demonstrates a classic dialectical tool in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the two claims made by the attacker.
The attacker first says Sunnah followers are few, then says most people follow his doctrine.
2
Identify the two claims
Identify the attacker's claim that 'seekers of Sunnah are few' and his claim that 'the vast multitude are upon his doctrine'.
3
Understand the contradiction
If his doctrine is the Sunnah, then the majority are Sunnah followers, contradicting the claim that they are few. If his doctrine is not Sunnah, then he admits most people are on a false path.
Both possibilities undermine his argument.
4
Reflect on the method
This is a reductio ad absurdum: assuming the attacker's statements are true leads to a logical impossibility.
Ibn Asakir only needs to point out the inconsistency; he does not need to provide further proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قوله ( وعز الطالبون للسنة إلا من أدركه الله بالعصمة وخصه بالتوفيق وقليل ما هم . فكيف يستقيم له هذا القول وهو يزعم أن الجم الغفير على مثل مذهبه واليسير من عداهم
As for his statement: 'The seekers of the Sunnah have become scarce, except those whom Allah has protected and granted success, and they are few in number' – how can this statement be consistent for him while he claims that the vast multitude are upon his like minded and the few are other than them?
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
Ibn Asakir points out a logical contradiction. The attacker claims that the true followers of the Sunnah are very few. But he also claims that the great majority of people follow his own doctrine. If his doctrine is what he claims, then the majority are Sunnah followers, contradicting the claim that they are few. If his doctrine is not Sunnah, then he is admitting his own deficiency. The argument shows the attacker's inconsistency.

Key Terms
الطالبون للسنة
الجم الغفير
مذهبه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One can consistently claim that only a few follow the truth while also claiming that one's own group is the truth and is followed by the majority.
Correction: If one's group is the truth, it cannot be both a small minority and the majority simultaneously. Consistency demands that the numerical claim match the doctrinal claim.

Checkpoint
What logical inconsistency does Ibn Asakir find in the attacker's claims?
A) The attacker says Sunnah followers are few but also claims many follow his view, which would make them many.
B) The attacker says he is a Sunnah follower but also criticizes Sunnah followers.
C) The attacker says only few are protected by Allah.
D) There is no inconsistency.

Key Takeaway
Logical inconsistency is a powerful tool for refuting false claims; Ibn Asakir shows that the attacker's own words contradict each other.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever found yourself making contradictory claims when defending your beliefs to others? When you say one thing but another action or statement contradicts it, how do you reconcile that? What would it take for you to admit inconsistency and correct yourself?
?
Imagine you are the attacker in this passage. Read your own words as quoted by Ibn Asakir. Would you be able to defend yourself? If not, what does that reveal about the importance of self-consistency in your own faith and arguments?
?
This passage shows that even a small logical inconsistency can dismantle an entire position. Are there any areas in your own understanding of Islam where you hold two beliefs that might be contradictory? Take a moment to identify one and resolve it today.

The Attacker's Wrong Self-Claim
Ibn Asakir denies that the attacker fits the description of a learned speaker.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ibn Asakir affirms a sound theological principle while denying that his opponent qualifies as the learned speaker it describes.

Core Idea
True scholars are those who defend truth, not those who insult them.

Ibn Asakir engages with an attacker who invokes the principle that Allah never leaves the earth without a knowledgeable speaker. Instead of rejecting the principle, Ibn Asakir turns it back on the attacker, showing that the very scholars he attacks are the true learned speakers. This is a classic defensive move in theological debates.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Note that Ibn Asakir first concedes the general point ('he has spoken truly') before shifting the focus to the attacker's personal disqualification.
Pay attention to the pivot word 'but'.
2
Identify the agreement and the denial
Ibn Asakir agrees with the statement about Allah preserving a learned speaker, but denies that the attacker is that person. Find the exact words for both points.
3
Understand who the true scholars are
According to Ibn Asakir, the true scholars are precisely those whom the attacker has 'excessively disparaged' and 'insulted.' This is the core of the rebuttal.
Note the contrast between 'he is not' and 'they are.'
4
Reflect on the rhetorical strategy
Ibn Asakir does not argue against the principle itself; he argues that the opponent does not match its description. This is a clever way to defend the scholars without rejecting a widely accepted hadith-like idea.
This strategy avoids conflict over the principle and focuses on the opponent's credibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قوله ( أن الله عز وجل لا يخلى الأرض من قائل عليم وعالم حكيم يقول الحق ويدفع الباطل ، ولا يدع لذى بدعة قولا يعلو ولا أمراً . فقد صدق ولكن ليس هو ممن وصفه بهذه الصفة إذ لم يتحقق يسمو كونه من أهل العلم ولا من ذوى المعرفة ، ولكن هم العلماء الذين بالغ في ذمهم وأغرق لفرط جهله وسوء عقده في شتمهم
As for his statement: 'Allah Almighty does not leave the earth without a knowledgeable speaker and a wise scholar who speaks the truth and repels falsehood, and does not leave any innovator with a word that prevails nor any matter' – he has spoken truly, but he is not among those he described with this attribute, since he has not attained being among the people of knowledge nor among those of insight. Rather, they are the scholars whom he has excessively disparaged and gone to extremes in insulting due to his extreme ignorance and bad understanding.
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
Ibn Asakir agrees with the general principle that Allah always preserves a true scholar on earth to defend truth and refute falsehood. However, he denies that the attacker is that person. Instead, the true scholars are the very ones the attacker has been vilifying. The attacker, due to his ignorance and bias, does not have the qualifications to be that 'knowledgeable speaker'. So the statement is true in itself but misapplied by the attacker.

Key Terms
لا يخلي الأرض
قائل عليم
عالم حكيم
أهل العلم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think Ibn Asakir rejects the hadith-like principle that Allah always preserves a learned speaker on earth.
Correction: Ibn Asakir affirms the principle but denies that the attacker is that speaker; the attacked scholars are the true learned speakers.

Checkpoint

A) He denies the principle entirely.
B) He agrees but says the attacker is not that speaker; the attacked scholars are.
C) He says the attacker is that speaker.
D) He ignores it.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Asakir uses a sound principle to defend the true scholars, showing that the attacker has misapplied it to himself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever criticized a scholar while feeling you are defending the truth? How does Ibn Asakir's response force you to examine whether you truly belong among the 'knowledgeable speakers' or are instead exposing your ignorance through slander? What will you do the next time you are tempted to speak ill of a scholar?
?
You may claim noble descriptions for yourself—'I speak the truth, I defend the Sunnah'—but does your treatment of the very people who carry that legacy match your words? Ibn Asakir shows that the same person can utter a true principle yet be disqualified from it. What description do you claim that your actions contradict, and what are you willing to change today to align your life with your claims?
?
Ibn Asakir affirmed the truth in his opponent’s statement but still held him accountable. Can you admit the good in those you disagree with while still recognizing their faults? Or does your dislike blind you to their virtues? This lesson demands that you be just, even to an opponent. When have you failed at this, and how will you practice this justice tomorrow?

Rebuttal on the Virtue of Sunnah and Severity of Innovation
Ibn Asakir challenges an attacker's praise of Sunnah by questioning his credentials and defending al-Ash'ari.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ibn Asakir defends the practice of refuting innovations as integral to the Sunnah and challenges his opponent's credibility through a direct comparison with Imam al-Ash'ari.

Core Idea
Refuting innovation is a sign of following the Sunnah, not a departure from it.

The attacker attempts to exalt the Sunnah and condemn innovation, but Ibn Asakir turns the tables: he challenges the attacker to prove his own commitment to Sunnah by examining his track record. He cites Imam al-Ash'ari's extensive refutations of deviant sects as a model of true adherence. This passage is a powerful example of defending orthodoxy through direct challenge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the forceful tone and the list of sects. Imagine the courtroom-like challenge.
2
Identify the attacker's claim
The attacker said 'There is no virtue better than the Sunnah and no evil more severe than innovation.' Ibn Asakir uses this as a starting point to question the attacker's sincerity.
3
Analyze the list of sects
Ibn Asakir lists many groups that al-Ash'ari refuted: Kharijites, Shia, Mu'tazila, Jabriyya, Jahmiyya, Mushabbiha, Karramiyya, Salimiyya. This shows al-Ash'ari's comprehensive defense of orthodoxy.
Note the terms: each sect represents a deviation in theology.
4
Understand the logical alternatives
Ibn Asakir presents two conditional arguments: if the attacker thinks refuting innovation is innovation, he is foolish; if he thinks tanzih is innovation and tashbih is Sunnah, he is wrong.
This exposes the attacker's inconsistency.
5
Reflect on the final challenge
Ibn Asakir dares the attacker to specify al-Ash'ari's supposed innovations. This forces the opponent to provide concrete evidence, which he likely cannot.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قوله ( لا معروف أفضل من السنة ولا منكر أشد من البدعة. فانظروا بعين التحقيق إلى مقالة هذا القرعة لتعلموا أهو أشد تسننا وأقوى في العلم تمكنا أم من أشتهرت ردوده على جميع المبتدعة من أصناف الخوارج وطوائف المتشيعة وانتشرت تصانيفه فى الابطال لمذاهب المعتزلة القدرية والإنكار على من يقول بأقوال المفوضة الجبرية والاصطلام لحجج المعطلة الجهمية والمحق لتعلقات المشبهة الجسمية من الكرامية والسالمية بالحجج السمعية والبراهين العقلية فإن اعتقد أن الرد على أصحاب البدع بدعة فقد تحقق كل ذي لب تسميتي إياه قرعة وإن اعتقد أن البدعة اعتقاد التنزيه والتوحيد والسنة القول بالتشبيه والميل إلى التقليد فبئس ما اعتقد وويل له مما تقلد ، وإن كان يبدع الأشعري رحمه الله في بعض المسائل الأدلة الاقلة فليذكر ما ابتدع فيه حتى نسمع ما عنده عليه من
As for his statement: 'There is no virtue better than the Sunnah and no evil more severe than innovation' – look with the eye of investigation at the statement of this scab-head (qur'a) to know whether he is more firmly adherent to the Sunnah and stronger in knowledge, or the one whose refutations against all innovators from the various Kharijites and sects of the Shia are well known, whose books refuting the doctrines of the Qadari Mu'tazila, and condemning those who say the sayings of the Mufawwida Jabriyya, and demolishing the arguments of the Jahmiyyah Mu'attila, and verifying the attachments of the Mushabbiha Jasmiyya from the Karramiyya and Salimiyya with scriptural proofs and rational evidences. If he thinks that refuting the people of innovation is innovation, then every person of intellect will certainly call him a scab-head. If he thinks that innovation is the belief in transcendence and oneness, and the Sunnah is to speak of anthropomorphism and incline to blind following, then evil is what he believes and woe to him for what he follows. And if he considers al-Ash'ari, may Allah have mercy on him, an innovator in some issues due to weak evidences, let him mention what innovations he is said to have introduced so that we might hear what he has to present against him.
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This is a lengthy and passionate rebuttal. Ibn Asakir responds to the attacker's praise of Sunnah and condemnation of innovation. He challenges the reader to compare the attacker with Imam al-Ash'ari, who is known for his extensive refutations of many innovating sects: Kharijites, Shia, Mu'tazila, Jabriyya, Jahmiyya, Mushabbiha, Karramiyya, Salimiyya. If the attacker thinks refuting innovation is itself innovation, then he is a 'scab-head' (a term of contempt). If he thinks that affirming God's transcendence (tanzih) and tawhid is innovation, and that anthropomorphism (tashbih) and blind following is the Sunnah, then he is gravely mistaken. Finally, Ibn Asakir dares the attacker to specify which innovations al-Ash'ari supposedly introduced, so that his claims can be examined. The passage is a direct challenge: the attacker's vague criticism is worthless without concrete evidence.

Misconception: Refuting innovators is itself an innovation and should be avoided.
Correction: Ibn Asakir argues that refuting innovation is a duty, and al-Ash'ari's work exemplifies this.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
True adherence to the Sunnah requires actively refuting innovations, and al-Ash'ari's thorough refutations stand as a model.

?

Exposing Contradiction in Claims About Sunni Scarcity
Ibn Asakir refutes two claims: the disappearance of revealers and the rarity of Sunni seekers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Asakir's logical refutation of two claims about the scarcity of truth-embracers and Sunni seekers, and see how he exposes a contradiction in the opponent's position.

Core Idea
The opponent's own boast reveals the inconsistency in his claim about Sunni rarity.

Ibn Asakir here addresses two related claims made by an opponent: that those who clarify ambiguous beliefs have dwindled, and that genuine seekers of the Sunnah are exceedingly rare. He does not directly rebut the first claim but instead uses the second to expose a glaring contradiction. Let's examine how he does this.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation together. Note the two quoted claims.
The first quotation may appear incomplete due to OCR.
2
Identify the two claims
Find the opponent's statements about 'revealers of ambiguity' and 'seekers of Sunnah'.
3
Understand Ibn Asakir's refutation
Ask yourself: how does the opponent's own claim about the majority following his school contradict his claim about rarity of Sunnis?
4
Reflect on the lesson
Consider why Ibn Asakir chooses to expose inconsistency rather than simply deny the claims.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قوله ( وأندرس الكاشفون للنشبه ، فلولا قلتهم لم يعتقد ما الاعتقاد المشتبه كان عليه من وأما قوله ( وعز الطالبون للسنة إلا من أدركه الله بالعصمة وخصه بالتوفيق وقليل ما هم . فكيف يستقيم له هذا القول وهو يزعم أن الجم الغفير على مثل مذهبه واليسير من عداهم
As for his saying: 'The revealers of ambiguity have disappeared, and were it not for their small number, the ambiguous belief would not have been held' — and as for his saying: 'The seekers of the Sunnah are rare except whom Allah protects and grants success, and few they are.' So how can this statement be valid for him while he claims that the great multitude is upon his like school and the few are those other than them?
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage presents two refutations. First, the opponent claimed that the 'revealers of ambiguity' (al-kashifun lil-tashbih) have disappeared, implying that only a few are left. Ibn Asakir does not directly refute this but moves to the second claim. The opponent also said that the seekers of the Sunnah are rare except those protected by Allah. Ibn Asakir points out the inconsistency: the opponent claims that the vast majority follow his own school, but then says Sunni seekers are rare. This is contradictory.
