The Truthful Scholar on Earth — Ibn Asakir's Refutation
Ibn Asakir refutes the opponent's claim about a truthful scholar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Asakir's refutation of the opponent's claim that the earth is never devoid of a truthful scholar, and his argument that the true scholars are those condemned by the opponent.

Core Idea
Ibn Asakir agrees with the principle but denies the opponent qualifies as that scholar.

The opponent asserts that Allah always leaves a truthful, wise scholar on earth to speak truth and repel falsehood. Ibn Asakir acknowledges this principle as true but turns it against the opponent: the opponent himself is not such a scholar, and the real scholars are those he has defamed. Let's examine the passage to see how this refutation works.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice the opponent's claim and Ibn Asakir's initial agreement.
2
Identify the opponent's description
The opponent describes a truthful speaker and wise scholar who repels falsehood. Ibn Asakir agrees that such a person exists, but not the opponent.
3
Understand the turn
Ibn Asakir argues that the opponent is not among those scholars; rather, the scholars he has condemned are the true ones. This refutation uses the opponent's own statement against him.
The condemned scholars are likely the Ash'ari scholars.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قوله ( أن الله عز وجل لا يخلى الأرض من قائل عليم وعالم حكيم يقول الحق ويدفع الباطل ، ولا يدع لذى بدعة قولا يعلو ولا أمراً . فقد صدق ولكن ليس هو ممن وصفه بهذه الصفة إذ لم يتحقق يسمو كونه من أهل العلم ولا من ذوى المعرفة ، ولكن هم العلماء الذين بالغ في ذمهم وأغرق لفرط جهله وسوء عقده في شتمهم وأما قوله ( لا معروف أفضل من السنة ولا منكر أشد من البدعة. فانظروا بعين التحقيق إلى مقالة هذا القرعة لتعلموا أهو أشد تسننا وأقوى في العلم تمكنا أم من أشتهرت ردوده على جميع المبتدعة من أصناف الخوارج وطوائف المتشيعة وانتشرت تصانيفه فى الابطال لمذاهب المعتزلة القدرية والإنكار على من يقول بأقوال المفوضة الجبرية والاصطلام لحجج المعطلة الجهمية والمحق لتعلقات المشبهة الجسمية من الكرامية والسالمية بالحجج السمعية والبراهين العقلية فإن اعتقد أن الرد على أصحاب البدع بدعة فقد تحقق كل ذي لب تسميتي إياه قرعة وإن اعتقد أن البدعة اعتقاد التنزيه والتوحيد والسنة القول بالتشبيه والميل إلى التقليد فبئس ما اعتقد وويل له مما تقلد ، وإن كان يبدع الأشعري رحمه الله في بعض المسائل الأدلة الاقلة فليذكر ما ابتدع فيه حتى نسمع ما عنده عليه من
As for his saying: 'Allah does not leave the earth devoid of a truthful speaker and a wise scholar who speaks the truth and repels falsehood, and does not leave for any innovator a statement that rises nor an affair.' He has spoken truthfully, but he is not among those described with this attribute, for he has not attained to be of the people of knowledge nor of the possessors of understanding. Rather, they are the scholars whom he has excessively condemned and drowned due to his extreme ignorance and bad belief in reviling them. And as for his saying: 'There is no known good better than the Sunnah, and no known evil more severe than bid'ah.' Look with the eye of verification at the statement of this bald man to know whether he is more strongly adhering to Sunnah and more firmly established in knowledge, or the one whose refutations against all innovators from the various branches of Khawarij and groups of Shia are famous, and whose books have spread in invalidating the schools of the Mu'tazila Qadariyya and in denouncing those who say the sayings of the Mufawwida Jabariyya, and in seizing the arguments of the Jahmiyya Mu'attila, and in proving the attachments of the Mujassima Mushabbiha from the Karamiyya and Salimiyya with scriptural evidences and rational proofs. If he believes that refuting the people of bid'ah is itself bid'ah, then every possessor of intellect has verified my calling him 'bald.' And if he believes that bid'ah is believing in transcendence and tawhid, and that Sunnah is saying anthropomorphism and inclining to blind imitation, then evil is what he believes, and woe to him from what he imitates. And if he declares al-Ash'ari an innovator in some issues of the few evidences, let him mention what he innovated so that we may hear what he has against him.
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage refutes two more claims. First, the opponent says Allah never leaves the earth without a truthful wise scholar who repels falsehood. Ibn Asakir agrees with the principle but denies that the opponent qualifies as such a scholar. In fact, the opponent has condemned the real scholars. Second, the opponent asserts that no good is better than Sunnah and no evil worse than bid'ah. Ibn Asakir challenges the opponent's consistency: the opponent claims to uphold Sunnah, but al-Ash'ari and his followers are the ones who have actually refuted all innovating sects (Khawarij, Shia, Mu'tazila, Jabariyya, Jahmiyya, Mujassima, etc.). If the opponent thinks refuting innovators is bid'ah, then he is foolish. If he thinks that believing in tawhid (transcendence) is bid'ah and anthropomorphism is Sunnah, then he is ruined. If he accuses al-Ash'ari of innovating in some issues, let him specify.

بدعة
عالم
قائل عليم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does Ibn Asakir say the opponent does not qualify as the truthful scholar?
A) Because the opponent does not speak the truth
B) Because the opponent has condemned the true scholars
C) Because the opponent is an innovator
D) Because the opponent lacks knowledge

Key Takeaway
The existence of a truthful scholar on earth is a divine promise, but the opponent's condemnation of true scholars disqualifies him from that description.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever spoken ill of a scholar or dismissed their teachings because of a personal disagreement? How does Ibn Asakir's description of the opponent's 'extreme ignorance and bad belief in reviling them' challenge you to examine the state of your own heart towards those who carry sacred knowledge?
?
The truthful scholar is defined by knowledge and understanding, not by attacks on others. Think of a recent conversation you had about religious matters: did you defend truth while respecting others, or did you condemn people? What would it take for you to embody the attribute of a truthful speaker without falling into the opponent's error?
?
Ibn Asakir says the opponent does not attain to be 'of the people of knowledge nor of the possessors of understanding.' Reflect on your own learning: are you truly seeking knowledge and understanding, or are you content with surface-level arguments that lead you to belittle those who differ? What one step can you take today to deepen your understanding before you speak?

Sunnah's Virtue and Bid'ah's Evil — Ibn Asakir's Challenge
Ibn Asakir defends al-Ash'ari by listing his refutations against various sects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Asakir's argument that the opponent's claim about the superiority of Sunnah and evil of bid'ah is used to expose his inconsistency, and that al-Ash'ari's followers are the true defenders of Sunnah.

Core Idea
If refuting innovators is bid'ah, then the opponent stands condemned.

The opponent declares that no good surpasses the Sunnah and no evil surpasses bid'ah. Ibn Asakir immediately challenges him: if this is true, then the one who refutes all innovating sects (like al-Ash'ari) is the true adherent of Sunnah, while the opponent's condemnation of these refuters reveals his hypocrisy. The passage lists the various groups refuted by al-Ash'ari and ends with a conditional challenge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage starting from 'وأما قوله (لا معروف أفضل من السنة...' and its translation. Notice the list of sects and the conditional statements.
2
List the sects refuted by al-Ash'ari
Identify the various groups mentioned: Khawarij, Shia, Mu'tazila, Qadariyya, Mufawwida, Jabariyya, Jahmiyya, Mu'attila, Mushabbiha, Mujassima, Karamiyya, Salimiyya. Notice they are all innovating sects.
This list shows al-Ash'ari's comprehensive refutations.
3
Understand Ibn Asakir's challenge
Ibn Asakir presents three conditional scenarios: if the opponent thinks refuting innovators is bid'ah, then he is foolish; if he thinks tawhid is bid'ah and anthropomorphism is Sunnah, then he is ruined; if he accuses al-Ash'ari of innovation in some issues, let him specify.
4
Reflect on the rhetorical force
Ibn Asakir uses sarcasm ('bald man') and logical dilemmas to show the opponent's position is self-contradictory. The true defender of Sunnah is not the opponent but al-Ash'ari.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قوله ( أن الله عز وجل لا يخلى الأرض من قائل عليم وعالم حكيم يقول الحق ويدفع الباطل ، ولا يدع لذى بدعة قولا يعلو ولا أمراً . فقد صدق ولكن ليس هو ممن وصفه بهذه الصفة إذ لم يتحقق يسمو كونه من أهل العلم ولا من ذوى المعرفة ، ولكن هم العلماء الذين بالغ في ذمهم وأغرق لفرط جهله وسوء عقده في شتمهم وأما قوله ( لا معروف أفضل من السنة ولا منكر أشد من البدعة. فانظروا بعين التحقيق إلى مقالة هذا القرعة لتعلموا أهو أشد تسننا وأقوى في العلم تمكنا أم من أشتهرت ردوده على جميع المبتدعة من أصناف الخوارج وطوائف المتشيعة وانتشرت تصانيفه فى الابطال لمذاهب المعتزلة القدرية والإنكار على من يقول بأقوال المفوضة الجبرية والاصطلام لحجج المعطلة الجهمية والمحق لتعلقات المشبهة الجسمية من الكرامية والسالمية بالحجج السمعية والبراهين العقلية فإن اعتقد أن الرد على أصحاب البدع بدعة فقد تحقق كل ذي لب تسميتي إياه قرعة وإن اعتقد أن البدعة اعتقاد التنزيه والتوحيد والسنة القول بالتشبيه والميل إلى التقليد فبئس ما اعتقد وويل له مما تقلد ، وإن كان يبدع الأشعري رحمه الله في بعض المسائل الأدلة الاقلة فليذكر ما ابتدع فيه حتى نسمع ما عنده عليه من
As for his saying: 'Allah does not leave the earth devoid of a truthful speaker and a wise scholar who speaks the truth and repels falsehood, and does not leave for any innovator a statement that rises nor an affair.' He has spoken truthfully, but he is not among those described with this attribute, for he has not attained to be of the people of knowledge nor of the possessors of understanding. Rather, they are the scholars whom he has excessively condemned and drowned due to his extreme ignorance and bad belief in reviling them. And as for his saying: 'There is no known good better than the Sunnah, and no known evil more severe than bid'ah.' Look with the eye of verification at the statement of this bald man to know whether he is more strongly adhering to Sunnah and more firmly established in knowledge, or the one whose refutations against all innovators from the various branches of Khawarij and groups of Shia are famous, and whose books have spread in invalidating the schools of the Mu'tazila Qadariyya and in denouncing those who say the sayings of the Mufawwida Jabariyya, and in seizing the arguments of the Jahmiyya Mu'attila, and in proving the attachments of the Mujassima Mushabbiha from the Karamiyya and Salimiyya with scriptural evidences and rational proofs. If he believes that refuting the people of bid'ah is itself bid'ah, then every possessor of intellect has verified my calling him 'bald.' And if he believes that bid'ah is believing in transcendence and tawhid, and that Sunnah is saying anthropomorphism and inclining to blind imitation, then evil is what he believes, and woe to him from what he imitates. And if he declares al-Ash'ari an innovator in some issues of the few evidences, let him mention what he innovated so that we may hear what he has against him.
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage refutes two more claims. First, the opponent says Allah never leaves the earth without a truthful wise scholar who repels falsehood. Ibn Asakir agrees with the principle but denies that the opponent qualifies as such a scholar. In fact, the opponent has condemned the real scholars. Second, the opponent asserts that no good is better than Sunnah and no evil worse than bid'ah. Ibn Asakir challenges the opponent's consistency: the opponent claims to uphold Sunnah, but al-Ash'ari and his followers are the ones who have actually refuted all innovating sects (Khawarij, Shia, Mu'tazila, Jabariyya, Jahmiyya, Mujassima, etc.). If the opponent thinks refuting innovators is bid'ah, then he is foolish. If he thinks that believing in tawhid (transcendence) is bid'ah and anthropomorphism is Sunnah, then he is ruined. If he accuses al-Ash'ari of innovating in some issues, let him specify.

Key Terms
السنة
البدعة
المبتدعة
الخوارج
المتشيعة
المعتزلة
القدرية
المفوضة
الجبرية
المعطلة
الجهمية
المشبهة
الجسمية
الكرامية
السالمية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the opponent is a defender of Sunnah because he praises it.
Correction: Ibn Asakir shows that the opponent's condemnation of scholars like al-Ash'ari, who actually refute innovations, reveals that the opponent is not a true defender of Sunnah; rather, al-Ash'ari and his followers are.

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Asakir, what does al-Ash'ari and his followers actually do regarding innovation?
A) They ignore innovations
B) They write refutations against all innovating sects
C) They support innovation
D) They are themselves innovators

Key Takeaway
True Sunnah is defended not by those who attack scholars but by those who refute innovations with evidence, as al-Ash'ari did.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever dismissed someone's critique of your beliefs as bid'ah without examining the evidence? How does Ibn Asakir's challenge expose your own potential for blind partisanship, and will you commit to listening fairly next time?
?
Is there a scholar you admire that you would defend without considering whether their actions might actually contradict the Sunnah? How does this passage push you to weigh evidence over loyalty, and what will you do to change?
?
When you hear the word 'Sunnah', does it evoke a feeling of pride or a call to action? This lesson shows that true Sunnah involves active refutation of falsehood—what falsehood in your circles are you avoiding to confront, and what step will you take today?

The Salimiyya Sect and Its Anthropomorphic Beliefs
Definition of the Salimiyya sect and its anthropomorphic beliefs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the core beliefs of the Salimiyya sect and why they are classified as anthropomorphists.

Core Idea
The Salimiyya claim Allah is seen in human form — pure anthropomorphism.

The Salimiyya are an extreme sect that emerged in Basra, named after al-Hasan al-Salimi. Their beliefs about Allah and the Quran are deeply problematic. This passage from Ibn 'Asakir outlines their key doctrines and situates them within the broader category of the Mushabbiha (anthropomorphists).

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the first paragraph carefully. Note the key terms: السالمية, المشبهة, صورة آدمى.
2
Identify the three main beliefs
The passage lists three specific beliefs of the Salimiyya: seeing Allah in human form, hearing Allah in recitation, and dead experiencing physical activities. Find each one.
3
Understand the sect's origin and affiliation
Note the founder and the fact that many hadith scholars and jurists belonged to this sect, including al-Ahwazi.
This sets up the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(1) السالمية قرقة من المشبهة يقولون أن الله تعالى يرى في صورة آدمى وأنه تعالي يقرأ على لسان كل قارئ وأنهم إذا سمعوا القرآن من قارئ يرون أنهم إنما يسمعونه من الله تعالى ويعتقدون أن الميت يأكل في القبر ويشرب وينكح إلى غير ذلك ، وهذه النحلة معروفة بالبصرة وسوادها بالسالمية نسبة إلى مقالة الحسن بن محمد بن أحمد بن سالم السالمي البصري وابنه أبي عبد الله المتصوف وإلى هذه النحلة الشنيعة ينتسب كثيرون من أهل الحديث والمتفقهة والأهوازى هذا من جملة هولاء
( ۲ ) يريد بهما ما أخرجه الأهوازى فى كتابه المذكور ) أن الله تعالى لما أراد أن يخلق نفسه خلق الخيل فأجراها حتى عرقت ثم خلق نفسه من ذلك العرق ) و ( رأيت ربي يوم عرفة بعرفات على جمل أحمر عليه إزاران وهو يقول قد سمحت قد غفرت الا المظالم ) وهما مما لا يرويه عاقل ولا مسلم وكم له فيه من الفاضحات . قال الحاكم أنبأنا إسماعيل بن محمد الشعراني أخبرت عن محمد بن شجاع الثلجي أخبرني حبان بن هلال عن حماد بن سلمه عن أبى المهزم عن أبي هريرة .. ( ... خلق خيلا فأجراها فعرقت الخلق نفسه من ذلك العرق ( أهـ . وهو موضوع أتهم بعضهم بوضعه ابن شجاع ولا مخل لاتهام مثله بقول إسماعيل ( أخبرت عنه ، فمن ذا الذي أخبره عنه أسمع منه سماعا أو أخبر هو كذلك وما مبلغ ثقة هذا وذاك فليتق الله الخصوم أن ينسبوا هذه القرية إلى فقي، مثله في علمه ودينه ووجاهته وخاتمته وأن هو كان شجى في حلوق المشبهة )
(1) The Salimiyya is a sect of the anthropomorphists. They say that Allah is seen in the form of a human, and that He recites on the tongue of every reciter, and that when they hear the Quran from a reciter, they think they are hearing it from Allah. They also believe that the dead eat, drink, and have intercourse in the grave, etc. This school of thought is known in Basra and its environs as the Salimiyya, attributed to the doctrine of al-Hasan ibn Muhammad ibn Ahmad ibn Salim al-Salimi al-Basri and his son Abu Abd Allah al-Mutasawwif. Many of the people of hadith and the fuqaha belong to this ugly school, and al-Ahwazi is one of them.
(2) By them he refers to what al-Ahwazi narrated in his aforementioned book: 'That Allah, when He wanted to create His self, created horses and made them run until they sweated, then He created His self from that sweat,' and 'I saw my Lord on the Day of Arafah at Arafat on a red camel, wearing two garments, saying: "I have forgiven, I have forgiven, except for injustices."' These are things that no sane person or Muslim would narrate. How many disgraceful things does he have in it! Al-Hakim said: Isma'il ibn Muhammad al-Sha'rani informed us: it was narrated to me from Muhammad ibn Shuja' al-Thalji: Hiban ibn Hilal narrated to me from Hammad ibn Salama from Abu al-Muhazzam from Abu Hurayra... '...He created horses and made them run so they sweated, then He created His self from that sweat.' End of quote. It is fabricated; some accused Ibn Shuja' of fabricating it. There is no room to accuse the likes of him (Ibn Shuja'?) with the statement of Isma'il 'I was informed about him.' So who is it that informed him about him? Did he hear from him directly or was he informed likewise? And what is the level of trustworthiness of this one and that? So let the opponents fear Allah that they attribute this village (?) to a faqih like him in his knowledge, religion, prestige, and end, and that it was a thorn in the throats of the anthropomorphists.
Source pages p. 274

Commentary
This passage consists of two explanatory notes (footnotes) that the author added. The first defines the Salimiyya sect, a subgroup of the anthropomorphists (المشبهة). They believe Allah is seen in human form, that He recites on the tongue of every Quran reciter, and that the dead experience physical activities in the grave. This sect is named after al-Hasan al-Salimi and his son, and many hadith scholars and jurists belong to it, including al-Ahwazi. The second note exposes two fabricated hadiths from al-Ahwazi's book: the creation of God's self from horse sweat, and seeing God riding a camel at Arafah. The author declares these narrations to be unacceptable by any sane or Muslim. He then provides a chain for one of them and notes that it is fabricated (موضوع), with some accusing Muhammad ibn Shuja' of fabricating it. He questions the reliability of the transmission and warns opponents not to attribute such absurdities to a respected faqih (likely al-Ash'ari or someone else).

Key Terms
السالمية
المشبهة
صورة آدمى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Salimiyya are considered a mainstream Sunni sect.
Correction: Ibn 'Asakir labels them as anthropomorphists (المشبهة) and their beliefs are deviant.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what do the Salimiyya believe about hearing the Quran?
A) They believe the Quran is created.
B) They think they hear Allah when hearing a reciter.
C) They believe the Quran is eternal.
D) They deny hearing anything from Allah.

Key Takeaway
The Salimiyya sect holds anthropomorphic beliefs about Allah's form and speech, and was widespread in Basra.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite the Quran, do you ever imagine Allah's voice coming through your tongue? How does the Salimiyya's extreme view challenge you to uphold Allah's transcendence while still believing the Quran is His speech?
?
The Salimiyya believed the dead experience physical pleasures like eating and drinking in the grave. How does your belief about the nature of barzakh life affect the way you prepare for death today?
?
This sect explicitly pictures Allah in human form. Are there any subtle mental images you hold of Allah that might border on anthropomorphism? What concrete steps will you take to purify your belief about Allah's attributes?

Exposing Fabricated Hadiths: The Horse Sweat and Camel Ride Narrations
Exposure of two fabricated hadiths and criticism of their chain.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the content and status of two fabricated hadiths about Allah's creation and appearance, and the importance of refuting such forgeries.

Core Idea
Fabricated hadiths about Allah's self and form are dangerous lies.

In this passage, Ibn 'Asakir presents two shocking narrations from al-Ahwazi's book: that Allah created His self from horse sweat, and that He appeared on a camel at Arafah. These are clearly fabricated, and the author questions their transmission chain and integrity.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Carefully read the second paragraph. Note the two fabricated hadiths and the chain criticism.
2
Identify the two narrations
The passage presents two narrations: (1) Allah created horses, they sweated, and He created His self from that sweat; (2) Seeing Allah on a red camel at Arafah. Understand why these are absurd.
3
Examine the chain and authenticity
The author provides a chain for the first narration and notes it is fabricated (موضوع). He questions the reliability of the transmitters and warns opponents not to attribute such lies to a respected scholar.

?

?

?
These fabricated hadiths were used to support a distorted view of Allah. Are there popular teachings or anecdotes you cherish that might have weak foundations? Will you examine them honestly, even if it means letting go of something you once held dear?

Ibn Asakir on al-Ahwazi's Anthropomorphism and Fabricated Hadiths
Ibn Asakir describes al-Ahwazi's anthropomorphic beliefs and his fabricated hadith book.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ibn Asakir condemned al-Ahwazi as an anthropomorphist and how he linked that to his fabricated hadith book.

Core Idea
Anthropomorphism leads to fabricating hadiths and opposing orthodox scholars.

This passage shows Ibn Asakir responding to a critic of Imam al-Ash'ari. He not only defends al-Ash'ari but exposes the theological corruption of his opponent, al-Ahwazi, who held anthropomorphic beliefs and compiled a book of fabricated hadiths. Understanding this helps us see the stakes of the anti-Ash'ari polemic and the methods used to discredit opponents.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without overanalyzing, then read the translation to grasp the overall flow of Ibn Asakir's critique.
2
Identify the accusations against al-Ahwazi
Notice the sects Ibn Asakir labels him with: Salimi, anthropomorphist, corporealist, Hashwi. Also note the specific criticism of his book as full of fabricated hadiths.
3
Understand the significance of the examples
The hadiths of 'riding the camel' and 'sweat of the horse' are examples of narrations that imply Allah has a body. Reflect on why these are considered anthropomorphic and why Ibn Asakir highlights them.
4
Consider Ibn Asakir's response
Ibn Asakir vows to refute al-Ahwazi's claims. Note that he does not engage his arguments directly here but instead discredits his source and motive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأما أبو الحسن رحمه الله فقد تقدم وصف العلماء له بالعلم وثناؤهم عليه وشهادتهم له بالمعرفة والفهم وذكر عدد تصانيفه . وتفصيل أسماء تواليفه ولو لم يصنف كتاباً غير التفسير لكفاه فأغص الله الأهوازي بريقه وفض فاه فإنه كان في اعتقاده سالميا مشبهاً مجسماً حشويا ومن وقف على كتابه الذي سماه كتاب البيان في شرح عقود أهل الإيمان ، الذي صنفه في أحاديث الصفات وأطلع على ما فيه من الآفات ورأى ما فيه من الأحاديث الموضوعة والروايات المستنكرة المدفوعة ، والأخبار الواهية الضعيفة والمعانى المتنافية السخيفة كحديث ركوب الجمل وعرق الخيل قضى عليه في اعتقاده بالويل وبعض هذا الكتاب موجود بدمشق بخط يده فمن أراد الوقوف عليه فليقف ليتحقق سوء معتقده وما كان منطوياً عليه من سوء الاعتقاد هو الذي حمله على ما ذكره في الأشعرى للعناد فمن تأمل ما ذكره بعين الانتقاد تبين له وجه الكذب فيه والفساد وأنا بمشيئة الله وحسن معونته أنقض ما ذكره وأوضح كذبه فيه لمن تأمله بعين الانصاف وتدبره .
As for Abu al-Hasan (may Allah have mercy on him), the description of the scholars for him regarding knowledge and their praise of him and their testimony for him with knowledge and understanding has preceded, and the number of his works and the detailed list of his books. If he had not authored a book other than the Tafsir, it would have sufficed him. So Allah choked al-Ahwazi with his saliva and silenced him, for he was in his belief a Salimi, an anthropomorphist, a corporealist, a Hashwi. And whoever examines his book which he called "al-Bayan fi Sharh 'Uqud Ahl al-Iman", which he authored on the hadiths of the attributes, and looks at what is in it of defects, and sees what is in it of fabricated hadiths, rejected narrations, weak and da'if reports, and contradictory and absurd meanings like the hadith of riding the camel and the sweat of the horse, would judge him in his belief with woe. And some of this book exists in Damascus in his own handwriting. So whoever wants to examine it, let him examine it to realize the evil of his belief. And what was hidden in him of evil belief is what drove him to what he mentioned about al-Ash'ari out of obstinacy. Whoever considers what he mentioned with a critical eye, the face of falsehood and corruption becomes clear to him. And I, by Allah's will and good assistance, will refute what he mentioned and clarify his falsehood in it for those who consider it with a fair eye and reflect.
Source pages p. 274

Commentary
This passage is a description and condemnation of al-Ahwazi's beliefs and his book. Ibn Asakir begins by reminding that Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari has already been praised by scholars. Then he invokes a curse on al-Ahwazi, stating that his belief was that of the Salimiyya, a sect of anthropomorphists. He describes al-Ahwazi's book "al-Bayan" as containing fabricated hadiths and weak reports, specifically mentioning the hadith of riding the camel and the sweat of the horse (which imply anthropomorphism). He notes that some of the book exists in al-Ahwazi's own handwriting in Damascus, and that his evil belief motivated his attack on al-Ash'ari. Ibn Asakir declares his intention to refute al-Ahwazi's claims.

Key Terms
السالمية
التشبيه
التجسيم
الحشوية
البيان في شرح عقود أهل الإيمان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Ibn Asakir is attacking a fellow Sunni scholar unfairly.
Correction: Ibn Asakir is condemning al-Ahwazi because his beliefs were anthropomorphic (tashbih) and his book contained fabricated hadiths, making him outside the orthodox Sunni consensus.

Checkpoint
What sect does Ibn Asakir accuse al-Ahwazi of belonging to?
A) Ash'ariyya
B) Salimiyya
C) Mu'tazila
D) Hanbali

Key Takeaway
Ibn Asakir discredits al-Ahwazi by labeling him an anthropomorphist and exposing the fabricated hadiths in his book, thereby defending al-Ash'ari and orthodox theological methodology.

The Benefit of Lineage and Personal Virtue
Ibn Asakir refutes al-Ahwazi's argument that al-Ash'ari's lineage does not benefit him.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ibn Asakir refutes the claim that al-Ash'ari's lineage is useless by balancing the role of righteous ancestry with personal virtue.

Core Idea
Lineage benefits when paired with personal virtue; both matter.

Ibn Asakir responds to an objection that al-Ash'ari's lineage to Abu Musa al-Ash'ari is irrelevant. He acknowledges that mere affiliation does not help without good deeds, but argues that a good origin combined with personal righteousness does bring benefit, citing prophetic hadith and a Quranic story to support his case.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the opening line where Ibn Asakir presents al-Ahwazi's argument: that lineage to a righteous Companion is useless because prophets and the truthful had disbelieving children.
Notice the objection attempts to sever any connection between ancestry and spiritual benefit.
2
See Ibn Asakir's nuanced response
Ibn Asakir concedes that mere lineage without good deeds is useless, but then adds that when the origin is good and the descendant is virtuous, the lineage does benefit. Pay attention to his conditional reasoning.
He uses the phrase 'لا سيما' (especially) to highlight al-Ash'ari's personal merit.
3
Recognize the scriptural proofs
Ibn Asakir supports his argument with the hadith 'People are mines' and the Quranic story of the two servants and the orphan boys' wall. Identify how each proof reinforces the point that righteous ancestry combined with personal piety brings blessing.
The hadith shows that noble lineage can be a sign of potential, while the story shows that righteousness of fathers benefits children.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأما قوله أن انتماء أبى الحسن إلى أبي موسى الأشعري ليس بنافعه في دينه لأن الأنبياء والصديقين ولدوا الكفار والمنافقين . فلعمري أن مجرد الانتساب لا ينفع إذا عرى المنتسب عن فعل الخير والاكتساب ، وهذا مما لا يدفع إلا أن الأصل إذا طاب وسما زكي الفرع المنسوب إليه ونما لا سيما إذا كان الفرع طيبا في نفسه مميزا بالصفات الحميدة عن أبناء جنسه مشهودا له بالزكاء في نيته وغرسه ، مشهورا بحسن فهمه وصحة حسه وقد سبق ذكر ما عرف من علم أبي الحسن ودينه وسلف وصفه بقوة إيمانه . وشدة يقينه ، وقد صح عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم قوله : الناس معادن خيارهم في الجاهلية خيارهم في الإسلام إذا فقهوا وليس ينكر فضل أبي الحسن رحمه الله إلا الذين تعاموا عن الحق وسفهوا ، أولا شك أن بركة صلاح الآباء مبشرة بفلاح من نسلوه من الأبناء ولو لم يعتبر إلا بقضة العبدين الكريمين حين اختلفا في إقامة جدار الغلامين اليتيمين
As for his statement that the affiliation of Abu al-Hasan to Abu Musa al-Ash'ari does not benefit him in his religion, because prophets and the truthful gave birth to disbelievers and hypocrites. By my life, mere affiliation does not benefit if the affiliate is devoid of doing good and earning. This is not deniable except that if the origin is good and lofty, the branch attributed to it becomes pure and grows, especially if the branch is itself good, distinguished by praiseworthy qualities from its peers, witnessed for purity in its intention and planting, famous for its good understanding and correctness of perception. And the description of Abu al-Hasan's knowledge and religion has preceded, and his description of strength of faith and intensity of certainty. And it is authentically reported from the Prophet (peace be upon him) his saying: "People are mines; the best of them in Jahiliyya are the best of them in Islam if they understand." And none denies the virtue of Abu al-Hasan (may Allah have mercy on him) except those who turned blind to the truth and were foolish. Undoubtedly, the blessing of the righteousness of fathers gives glad tidings of the success of those who carry them from sons. If one were not to consider except the story of the two noble servants when they disagreed about erecting the wall of the two orphan boys.
Source pages p. 275

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of al-Ahwazi's objection that Abu al-Hasan's lineage to Abu Musa al-Ash'ari does not benefit him. Ibn Asakir argues that while mere lineage does not benefit without personal good deeds, if the origin is good and the descendant is virtuous, then the lineage does benefit. He supports this with the hadith "People are mines" and the Quranic story of the two servants (Moses and al-Khidr) who built the wall for the two orphan boys, which shows that the righteousness of fathers brings blessing to children. Therefore, al-Ash'ari's ancestry is praiseworthy.

Key Terms
الانتساب
الناس معادن
العبدين الكريمين
الغلامين اليتيمين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Ibn Asakir believes lineage alone saves and guarantees salvation.
Correction: Ibn Asakir explicitly states that mere lineage without personal virtue and good deeds is useless. He argues that lineage benefits only when the descendant is also righteous and acts accordingly.

Checkpoint
What hadith does Ibn Asakir cite to support that lineage can benefit?
A) "People are mines; the best of them in Jahiliyya are the best in Islam if they understand."
B) "The best of you are those with the best character."
C) "Actions are by intentions."
D) "The lineage of a believer does not benefit him."

Key Takeaway
Ibn Asakir affirms that lineage benefits a person when they combine it with personal virtue and good deeds, as exemplified by al-Ash'ari and supported by prophetic and Quranic proofs.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You may pride yourself on being from a good family or following a respected teacher, but Ibn Asakir says mere affiliation is useless without personal virtue. What specific good deed did you do today that you cannot attribute to your lineage or company?
?
The hadith says 'the best of you in Jahiliyya are the best in Islam if they understand.' Is your Islam a matter of understanding you have personally pursued, or is it an inherited label? What will you do this week to deepen your understanding so that your Islam is truly yours?
?
Think of someone you have dismissed because of their background. What if they possess more personal virtue than you? Would your attitude change if you saw them as a 'branch' that could be pure despite their 'origin'? Will you ask Allah to purify your heart from looking down on others?

The Weak Chain of al-Ahwazi's Hadith
Ibn Asakir critiques the weakness of al-Ahwazi's hadith chain.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ibn Asakir considers the hadith chain of al-Ahwazi to be weak and disconnected.

Core Idea
Weak narrators and a broken link make al-Ahwazi's hadith unreliable.

Ibn Asakir begins by stating how his refutation stung the anthropomorphists. He then exposes the weaknesses in the chain of the hadith they narrated, pointing to known unreliable narrators like Hammad bin Salama (after his confusion) and Abu al-Muhazzim (matruk). This lesson examines his critique of the chain.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the translation. Focus on the narrators mentioned.
2
Identify the weak narrators
Find the names of the narrators that Ibn Asakir criticizes and note the specific reasons for their weakness.
Hammad bin Salama after confusion, his stepson a forger, Abu al-Muhazzim matruk.
3
Understand the disconnected chain
Ask yourself why Ibn Asakir says there is no connection between Ibn Shuja' and this hadith. The chain is munqati' (disconnected).
This undermines the reliability of the entire narration.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ذلك وجذعا في أعينهم بما ألفه في الرد عليهم وباقامته النكير عليهم لروايتهم أمثال هذه من السخافات ، ومعلوم عند النقاد ما يدخل في روايات حماد بن سلمة بعد اختلاطه ربيبه الوضاع المشهور وأبو المهزم شيخه متروك عند أهل الجرح ، والاهوازي من جملة رواته، وأين صلة ابن شجاع بهذا الحديث بمثل هذا السند المنقطع ، ومن غريب التعدى ما يقوله ابن عدى أنه كان يضع هذه الأحاديث ويدسها في كتب أهل الحديث ليفضحهم فيروونها بسلامة باطن هـ . لأن ابن شجاع ما كان خادماً ولا ربيباً عند راو من الرواة حتى يتصور أن يدس بين كتب أحدهم شيئاً فكان هذا الجارح العامى اللاحن لم يكن يعرف مبلغ علم ابن شجاع وديانته ووقاره وتصونه ووجاهته في عصره حتى تكلم بكلام ما يبطله معه فياترى هل يبقى الراوى مقبول الرواية بعد أن دس في كتبه شئ وتلقن ذلك ورواه فإذا لم يبرهن الجارح على كتب من دس ابن شجاع وما ذادس وكيف دس ؟ لا ينجيه من هذه الوقعية إذا وقعت الواقعة كونه يرويها عن عامي مثله كأسراب طير يطير بعضها خلف بعض فلعنة الله على الكاذبين ، نعم ابن شجاع له شذوذ في مسألة القرآن كابن المديني الذي يقول فيه البخاری : ما استصغرت نفسي عند أحد استصغارى لها عند ابن المديني ، ركابن علية الذي يقول فيه أحمد : فاتنى حماد بن زيد فأخلف الله على ابن علية . وليس هو بمنفرد في هذا الشذوذ وللنظر متسع فى المسألة بالمعنى الذى أرادوه : ومن راجع كتاب الرد على الجهمية لعثمان الدارمي يتبين له ما ذا كانوا ينقمون منه مما يعد المخطئ في تغاليه ابناء اخوات خالاتهم سامحهم الله تعالى وإيانا بمنه وكرمه قال الذهبي في سیر النبلاء عند ترجمة ابن شجاع : أحد الاعلام سمع من ابن علية ووكيع وأبي أسامة وطبقتهم وأخذ الحروف عن يحيى بن آدم والفقه عن الحسن بن زياد وكان من بحور العلم وكان صاحب تعبد وتهجد وتلاوة وله كتاب المناسك في نيف وستين جزءا وكتاب تصحيح الآثار وغير ذلك وعاش ٨٥ سنة ومات سنة ٢٦٦هـ
That [refutation] was a thorn in their eyes through what he authored in refutation of them and his establishment of disapproval against them for narrating such absurdities. And it is known among critics what enters into the narrations of Hammad bin Salama after his confusion; his stepson the notorious forger, and Abu al-Muhazzim his teacher is abandoned (matruk) among the people of jarh. And al-Ahwazi is among his narrators. And where is the connection of Ibn Shuja' to this hadith with such a disconnected chain? And from the strange nature of transgression is what Ibn 'Adi says, that he used to fabricate these hadiths and insert them into the books of the hadith people to expose them, so they would narrate them with a sound interior (?). Because Ibn Shuja' was neither a servant nor a stepson of any narrator such that it could be imagined that he inserted something among the books of any of them. This critical layman who makes mistakes does not know the extent of Ibn Shuja's knowledge, piety, dignity, abstention, and prestige in his time, until he spoke words that are invalidated. So does the narrator remain acceptable in narrations after something has been inserted in his books and he learned it and narrated it? If the critic does not prove in whose books Ibn Shuja' inserted, what he inserted, and how he inserted, he is not saved from this pitfall when it occurs, because he narrates it from an ignorant like him like flocks of birds flying one after another. May Allah curse the liars. Yes, Ibn Shuja' has some deviation in the issue of the Quran like Ibn al-Madini, about whom al-Bukhari said: "I never belittled myself before anyone as I belittled myself before Ibn al-Madini," and like Ibn 'Ulayya, about whom Ahmad said: "I missed Hammad bin Zaid, so Allah compensated me with Ibn 'Ulayya." And he is not alone in this deviation, and there is scope for consideration in the matter according to the meaning they intended. And whoever reviews the book "al-Radd 'ala al-Jahmiyya" by 'Uthman al-Darimi will understand what they were objecting to from him, which those who err in exaggeration—the children of their sisters' aunts—considered, may Allah forgive them and us with His bounty and generosity. Al-Dhahabi said in "Siyar al-Nubala'" in the biography of Ibn Shuja': "One of the leading scholars; he heard from Ibn 'Ulayya, Waki', Abu Usama, and their generation; he took the modes of recitation from Yahya ibn Adam and fiqh from al-Hasan ibn Ziyad; he was among the oceans of knowledge; he was a man of worship, night prayer, and recitation; he authored the book of Manasik in sixty-some parts, and the book of Tashih al-Aathar, etc. He lived 85 years and died in 266 AH."
Source pages p. 275

Commentary
This passage is a critique of the chain of transmission for the fabricated hadiths cited by al-Ahwazi, and a defense of Ibn Shuja' (al-Thalji) against accusations of hadith forgery. Ibn Asakir first states that his refutation stung the eyes of the anthropomorphists. He notes that Hammad bin Salama is suspect due to confusion (ikhtilat) and his stepson being a forger, and Abu al-Muhazzim is abandoned by critics. Then he questions the connection of Ibn Shuja' to these hadiths with a disconnected chain. He rejects Ibn 'Adi's claim that Ibn Shuja' would plant hadiths, explaining that Ibn Shuja' was a respected scholar. He concludes with a quote from al-Dhahabi praising Ibn Shuja' as a leading scholar, worshipful, and author of many works, and notes his death year.

Key Terms
حماد بن سلمة
أبو المهزم
السند المنقطع
الوضاع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Hammad bin Salama is always a reliable narrator.
Correction: Ibn Asakir notes that after his confusion, his narrations are suspect.

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Asakir, what is the problem with the chain of the hadith cited by al-Ahwazi?
A) It is fabricated
B) It includes unreliable narrators and is disconnected
C) It is authentic
D) It has a missing narrator

Key Takeaway
The hadith chain of al-Ahwazi is unreliable due to weak narrators and disconnection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear a hadith that confirms what you already believe, do you ever check its chain, or do you accept it because it 'feels' true? This lesson shows how a single weak link can destroy the entire message. What hadith about Allah's attributes have you quoted without verifying its source?
?
Imagine you are seated with one of the anthropomorphists He refutes. You cite a weak hadith to support a point about Allah's hand. Would you be willing to admit your chain is broken, even if the meaning seems beautiful? This lesson forces you to ask: do you love the truth more than your own opinion?
?
The chain here is not just weak—it is disconnected. How many of your religious beliefs are based on hearsay or 'someone said' rather than a verified source? Will you commit today to tracing one belief back to its original evidence, even if it challenges you?

Refuting the Accusation Against Ibn Shuja'
Ibn Asakir refutes Ibn 'Adi's accusation against Ibn Shuja'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ibn Asakir refutes the accusation that Ibn Shuja' fabricated hadiths.

Core Idea
Ibn Shuja' was a respected scholar, not a forger — the accusation is baseless.

Ibn Asakir addresses a peculiar claim by Ibn 'Adi that Ibn Shuja' used to fabricate hadiths and plant them in the books of hadith scholars. He argues that Ibn Shuja' had no opportunity to do so, and the critic lacks evidence. This lesson examines his refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note the accusation and the counterarguments.
2
Identify the accusation and refutation
What does Ibn 'Adi claim? How does Ibn Asakir respond? List the arguments Ibn Asakir uses.
Ibn Shuja' was not a servant or stepson; the critic is ignorant of his status; no proof is provided.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
Ibn Asakir ends with a strong condemnation of liars. Consider why he uses such language.
It underscores the severity of falsely accusing a scholar.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ذلك وجذعا في أعينهم بما ألفه في الرد عليهم وباقامته النكير عليهم لروايتهم أمثال هذه من السخافات ، ومعلوم عند النقاد ما يدخل في روايات حماد بن سلمة بعد اختلاطه ربيبه الوضاع المشهور وأبو المهزم شيخه متروك عند أهل الجرح ، والاهوازي من جملة رواته، وأين صلة ابن شجاع بهذا الحديث بمثل هذا السند المنقطع ، ومن غريب التعدى ما يقوله ابن عدى أنه كان يضع هذه الأحاديث ويدسها في كتب أهل الحديث ليفضحهم فيروونها بسلامة باطن هـ . لأن ابن شجاع ما كان خادماً ولا ربيباً عند راو من الرواة حتى يتصور أن يدس بين كتب أحدهم شيئاً فكان هذا الجارح العامى اللاحن لم يكن يعرف مبلغ علم ابن شجاع وديانته ووقاره وتصونه ووجاهته في عصره حتى تكلم بكلام ما يبطله معه فياترى هل يبقى الراوى مقبول الرواية بعد أن دس في كتبه شئ وتلقن ذلك ورواه فإذا لم يبرهن الجارح على كتب من دس ابن شجاع وما ذادس وكيف دس ؟ لا ينجيه من هذه الوقعية إذا وقعت الواقعة كونه يرويها عن عامي مثله كأسراب طير يطير بعضها خلف بعض فلعنة الله على الكاذبين ، نعم ابن شجاع له شذوذ في مسألة القرآن كابن المديني الذي يقول فيه البخاری : ما استصغرت نفسي عند أحد استصغارى لها عند ابن المديني ، ركابن علية الذي يقول فيه أحمد : فاتنى حماد بن زيد فأخلف الله على ابن علية . وليس هو بمنفرد في هذا الشذوذ وللنظر متسع فى المسألة بالمعنى الذى أرادوه : ومن راجع كتاب الرد على الجهمية لعثمان الدارمي يتبين له ما ذا كانوا ينقمون منه مما يعد المخطئ في تغاليه ابناء اخوات خالاتهم سامحهم الله تعالى وإيانا بمنه وكرمه قال الذهبي في سیر النبلاء عند ترجمة ابن شجاع : أحد الاعلام سمع من ابن علية ووكيع وأبي أسامة وطبقتهم وأخذ الحروف عن يحيى بن آدم والفقه عن الحسن بن زياد وكان من بحور العلم وكان صاحب تعبد وتهجد وتلاوة وله كتاب المناسك في نيف وستين جزءا وكتاب تصحيح الآثار وغير ذلك وعاش ٨٥ سنة ومات سنة ٢٦٦هـ
That [refutation] was a thorn in their eyes through what he authored in refutation of them and his establishment of disapproval against them for narrating such absurdities. And it is known among critics what enters into the narrations of Hammad bin Salama after his confusion; his stepson the notorious forger, and Abu al-Muhazzim his teacher is abandoned (matruk) among the people of jarh. And al-Ahwazi is among his narrators. And where is the connection of Ibn Shuja' to this hadith with such a disconnected chain? And from the strange nature of transgression is what Ibn 'Adi says, that he used to fabricate these hadiths and insert them into the books of the hadith people to expose them, so they would narrate them with a sound interior (?). Because Ibn Shuja' was neither a servant nor a stepson of any narrator such that it could be imagined that he inserted something among the books of any of them. This critical layman who makes mistakes does not know the extent of Ibn Shuja's knowledge, piety, dignity, abstention, and prestige in his time, until he spoke words that are invalidated. So does the narrator remain acceptable in narrations after something has been inserted in his books and he learned it and narrated it? If the critic does not prove in whose books Ibn Shuja' inserted, what he inserted, and how he inserted, he is not saved from this pitfall when it occurs, because he narrates it from an ignorant like him like flocks of birds flying one after another. May Allah curse the liars. Yes, Ibn Shuja' has some deviation in the issue of the Quran like Ibn al-Madini, about whom al-Bukhari said: "I never belittled myself before anyone as I belittled myself before Ibn al-Madini," and like Ibn 'Ulayya, about whom Ahmad said: "I missed Hammad bin Zaid, so Allah compensated me with Ibn 'Ulayya." And he is not alone in this deviation, and there is scope for consideration in the matter according to the meaning they intended. And whoever reviews the book "al-Radd 'ala al-Jahmiyya" by 'Uthman al-Darimi will understand what they were objecting to from him, which those who err in exaggeration—the children of their sisters' aunts—considered, may Allah forgive them and us with His bounty and generosity. Al-Dhahabi said in "Siyar al-Nubala'" in the biography of Ibn Shuja': "One of the leading scholars; he heard from Ibn 'Ulayya, Waki', Abu Usama, and their generation; he took the modes of recitation from Yahya ibn Adam and fiqh from al-Hasan ibn Ziyad; he was among the oceans of knowledge; he was a man of worship, night prayer, and recitation; he authored the book of Manasik in sixty-some parts, and the book of Tashih al-Aathar, etc. He lived 85 years and died in 266 AH."
Source pages p. 275

Commentary
This passage is a critique of the chain of transmission for the fabricated hadiths cited by al-Ahwazi, and a defense of Ibn Shuja' (al-Thalji) against accusations of hadith forgery. Ibn Asakir first states that his refutation stung the eyes of the anthropomorphists. He notes that Hammad bin Salama is suspect due to confusion (ikhtilat) and his stepson being a forger, and Abu al-Muhazzim is abandoned by critics. Then he questions the connection of Ibn Shuja' to these hadiths with a disconnected chain. He rejects Ibn 'Adi's claim that Ibn Shuja' would plant hadiths, explaining that Ibn Shuja' was a respected scholar. He concludes with a quote from al-Dhahabi praising Ibn Shuja' as a leading scholar, worshipful, and author of many works, and notes his death year.

Key Terms
التعدى
ابن عدى
ابن شجاع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Ibn Shuja' was a forger of hadiths.
Correction: Ibn Asakir shows the accusation is baseless and impossible given Ibn Shuja's position.

Checkpoint
What is Ibn Asakir's main argument against the accusation that Ibn Shuja' planted hadiths?
A) Ibn Shuja' was a servant
B) Ibn Shuja' had no opportunity and the accuser lacks proof
C) Ibn Shuja' admitted it
D) The hadiths are authentic

Key Takeaway
Ibn Asakir decisively refutes the accusation of hadith forgery against Ibn Shuja' by showing it is unsubstantiated.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever believed an accusation against someone without verifying the facts? This lesson shows how a baseless claim can harm a righteous person. What one step can you take today to avoid repeating that mistake?
?
Ibn Shuja' was slandered despite his piety and knowledge. Is there someone in your community who is misunderstood or wrongly accused? What can you do to defend them, even if you don't know all the details?
?
Ibn 'Adi's ignorance led him to fabricate a story. In what area of your life are you quick to judge without full knowledge? Will you commit to seeking the truth before speaking, starting now?

Ibn Shuja's Creed and Scholarly Legacy
Ibn Asakir defends Ibn Shuja' by contextualizing his deviation and citing his scholarly virtues.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Asakir's balanced defense of Ibn Shuja', acknowledging his deviation on the Quran while affirming his scholarly achievements.

Core Idea
Ibn Shuja' was a great scholar despite a deviation shared by others.

Ibn Asakir acknowledges that Ibn Shuja' had some deviation in the issue of the Quran, but he notes this was not unique to him — other notable scholars like Ibn al-Madini and Ibn 'Ulayya had similar views. He then quotes al-Dhahabi's high praise of Ibn Shuja's knowledge and devotion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the two parts: admission of deviation and praise from al-Dhahabi.
2
Understand the deviation
Ibn Asakir admits Ibn Shuja' had deviation on the Quran but compares him to Ibn al-Madini and Ibn 'Ulayya. Why is this comparison significant?
It shows that deviation on this issue did not make someone a lesser scholar.
3
Reflect on al-Dhahabi's praise
Read the qualities al-Dhahabi lists: oceans of knowledge, worship, authorship. How does this balance the earlier admission?
Ibn Shuja' remains a leading scholar despite his deviation.

ذلك وجذعا في أعينهم بما ألفه في الرد عليهم وباقامته النكير عليهم لروايتهم أمثال هذه من السخافات ، ومعلوم عند النقاد ما يدخل في روايات حماد بن سلمة بعد اختلاطه ربيبه الوضاع المشهور وأبو المهزم شيخه متروك عند أهل الجرح ، والاهوازي من جملة رواته، وأين صلة ابن شجاع بهذا الحديث بمثل هذا السند المنقطع ، ومن غريب التعدى ما يقوله ابن عدى أنه كان يضع هذه الأحاديث ويدسها في كتب أهل الحديث ليفضحهم فيروونها بسلامة باطن هـ . لأن ابن شجاع ما كان خادماً ولا ربيباً عند راو من الرواة حتى يتصور أن يدس بين كتب أحدهم شيئاً فكان هذا الجارح العامى اللاحن لم يكن يعرف مبلغ علم ابن شجاع وديانته ووقاره وتصونه ووجاهته في عصره حتى تكلم بكلام ما يبطله معه فياترى هل يبقى الراوى مقبول الرواية بعد أن دس في كتبه شئ وتلقن ذلك ورواه فإذا لم يبرهن الجارح على كتب من دس ابن شجاع وما ذادس وكيف دس ؟ لا ينجيه من هذه الوقعية إذا وقعت الواقعة كونه يرويها عن عامي مثله كأسراب طير يطير بعضها خلف بعض فلعنة الله على الكاذبين ، نعم ابن شجاع له شذوذ في مسألة القرآن كابن المديني الذي يقول فيه البخاری : ما استصغرت نفسي عند أحد استصغارى لها عند ابن المديني ، ركابن علية الذي يقول فيه أحمد : فاتنى حماد بن زيد فأخلف الله على ابن علية . وليس هو بمنفرد في هذا الشذوذ وللنظر متسع فى المسألة بالمعنى الذى أرادوه : ومن راجع كتاب الرد على الجهمية لعثمان الدارمي يتبين له ما ذا كانوا ينقمون منه مما يعد المخطئ في تغاليه ابناء اخوات خالاتهم سامحهم الله تعالى وإيانا بمنه وكرمه قال الذهبي في سیر النبلاء عند ترجمة ابن شجاع : أحد الاعلام سمع من ابن علية ووكيع وأبي أسامة وطبقتهم وأخذ الحروف عن يحيى بن آدم والفقه عن الحسن بن زياد وكان من بحور العلم وكان صاحب تعبد وتهجد وتلاوة وله كتاب المناسك في نيف وستين جزءا وكتاب تصحيح الآثار وغير ذلك وعاش ٨٥ سنة ومات سنة ٢٦٦هـ

?

Defending Ibn Shuja': Refuting Unproven Accusations of Hadith Forgery
Ibn Asakir refutes Ibn 'Adi's accusation that Ibn Shuja' forged hadith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ibn Asakir refutes an accusation of hadith forgery by demonstrating the accuser's lack of evidence and highlighting the accused's scholarly credentials.

Core Idea
An accusation of hadith forgery requires proof; mere claims do not taint a narrator's reliability.

In this passage, Ibn Asakir defends the narrator Ibn Shuja' against a serious accusation by Ibn 'Adi that he forged hadith and inserted them into scholars' books. Ibn Asakir systematically dismantles the charge by showing its logical flaws and pointing to Ibn Shuja's stellar reputation among hadith critics. This lesson examines the principles of jarh wa ta'dil and the importance of evidence in evaluating narrators.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation side by side. Notice the tone and structure of Ibn Asakir's refutation.
Pay attention to the rhetorical questions he uses.
2
Identify Ibn Asakir's key arguments
Identify the main points Ibn Asakir makes: the lack of connection between Ibn Shuja' and the alleged forgery, the ignorance of the accuser, and the need for concrete evidence.
Notice how he compares Ibn Shuja' to other respected scholars with some irregularity.
3
Reflect on the principle of proof in jarh wa ta'dil
Consider the broader principle that an accusation must be proven. How does this passage illustrate that principle?
Think about the difference between a valid critique and an unsubstantiated claim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَمَعْلُومٌ عِنْدَ النُّقَّادِ مَا يَدْخُلُ فِي رِوَايَاتِ حَمَّادِ بْنِ سَلَمَةَ بَعْدَ اخْتِلَاطِهِ رَبِيبُهُ الْوَضَّاعُ الْمَشْهُورُ وَأَبُو الْمِهْزَمِ شَيْخُهُ مَتْرُوكٌ عِنْدَ أَهْلِ الْجَرْحِ، وَالْأَهْوَازِيُّ مِنْ جُمْلَةِ رُوَاتِهِ، وَأَيْنَ صِلَةُ ابْنِ شُجَاعٍ بِهَذَا الْحَدِيثِ بِمِثْلِ هَذَا السَّنَدِ الْمُنْقَطِعِ، وَمِنْ غَرِيبِ التَّعَدِّي مَا يَقُولُهُ ابْنُ عَدِيٍّ أَنَّهُ كَانَ يَضَعُ هَذِهِ الْأَحَادِيثَ وَيَدُسُّهَا فِي كُتُبِ أَهْلِ الْحَدِيثِ لِيُفْضِحَهُمْ فَيَرْوُونَهَا بِسَلَامَةِ بَاطِنِهِ. لِأَنَّ ابْنَ شُجَاعٍ مَا كَانَ خَادِمًا وَلَا رَبِيبًا عِنْدَ رَاوٍ مِنَ الرُّوَاةِ حَتَّى يُتَصَوَّرَ أَنْ يَدُسَّ بَيْنَ كُتُبِ أَحَدِهِمْ شَيْئًا فَكَانَ هَذَا الْجَارِحُ الْعَامِيُّ اللَّاحِنُ لَمْ يَكُنْ يَعْرِفُ مَبْلَغَ عِلْمِ ابْنِ شُجَاعٍ وَدِيَانَتِهِ وَوَقَارِهِ وَتَصَوُّنِهِ وَوَجَاهَتِهِ فِي عَصْرِهِ حَتَّى تَكَلَّمَ بِكَلَامٍ مَا يُبْطِلُهُ مَعَهُ فَيَا تَرَى هَلْ يَبْقَى الرَّاوِي مَقْبُولَ الرِّوَايَةِ بَعْدَ أَنْ دُسَّ فِي كُتُبِهِ شَيْءٌ وَتَلَقَّنَ ذَلِكَ وَرَوَاهُ فَإِذَا لَمْ يُبَرْهِنِ الْجَارِحُ عَلَى كُتُبِ مَنْ دُسَّ ابْنُ شُجَاعٍ وَمَا ذَادَسْ وَكَيْفَ دَسَّ؟ لَا يُنْجِيهِ مِنْ هَذِهِ الْوَقْعِيَّةِ إِذَا وَقَعَتِ الْوَاقِعَةُ كَوْنُهُ يَرْوِيهَا عَنْ عَامِّيٍّ مِثْلِهِ كَأَسْرَابِ طَيْرٍ يَطِيرُ بَعْضُهَا خَلْفَ بَعْضٍ فَلَعْنَةُ اللَّهِ عَلَى الْكَاذِبِينَ. نَعَمِ ابْنُ شُجَاعٍ لَهُ شُذُوذٌ فِي مَسْأَلَةِ الْقُرْآنِ كَابْنِ الْمَدِينِيِّ الَّذِي يَقُولُ فِيهِ الْبُخَارِيُّ: مَا اسْتَصْغَرْتُ نَفْسِي عِنْدَ أَحَدٍ اسْتِصْغَارِي لَهَا عِنْدَ ابْنِ الْمَدِينِيِّ، وَكَابْنِ عُلَيَّةَ الَّذِي يَقُولُ فِيهِ أَحْمَدُ: فَاتَنِي حَمَّادُ بْنُ زَيْدٍ فَأَخْلَفَ اللَّهُ عَلَيَّ ابْنَ عُلَيَّةَ. وَلَيْسَ هُوَ بِمُنْفَرِدٍ فِي هَذَا الشُّذُوذِ وَلِلنَّظَرِ مُتَّسَعٌ فِي الْمَسْأَلَةِ بِالْمَعْنَى الَّذِي أَرَادُوهُ. وَمَنْ رَاجَعَ كِتَابَ الرَّدِّ عَلَى الْجَهْمِيَّةِ لِعُثْمَانَ الدَّارِمِيِّ يَتَبَيَّنُ لَهُ مَا ذَا كَانُوا يَنْقُمُونَ مِنْهُ مِمَّا يُعَدُّ الْمُخْطِئُ فِي تَغَالِيهِ أَبْنَاءُ أَخَوَاتِ خَالَاتِهِمْ سَامَحَهُمُ اللَّهُ تَعَالَى وَإِيَّانَا بِمَنِّهِ وَكَرَمِهِ. قَالَ الذَّهَبِيُّ فِي (سِيَرِ النُّبَلَاءِ) عِنْدَ تَرْجَمَةِ ابْنِ شُجَاعٍ: أَحَدُ الْأَعْلَامِ سَمِعَ مِنِ ابْنِ عُلَيَّةَ وَوَكِيعٍ وَأَبِي أُسَامَةَ وَطَبَقَتِهِمْ وَأَخَذَ الْحُرُوفَ عَنْ يَحْيَى بْنِ آدَمَ وَالْفِقْهَ عَنِ الْحَسَنِ بْنِ زِيَادٍ وَكَانَ مِنْ بُحُورِ الْعِلْمِ وَكَانَ صَاحِبَ تَعَبُّدٍ وَتَهَجُّدٍ وَتِلَاوَةٍ وَلَهُ كِتَابُ الْمَنَاسِكِ فِي نَيِّفٍ وَسِتِّينَ جُزْءًا (وَكِتَابُ تَصْحِيحِ الْآثَارِ وَغَيْرُ ذَلِكَ) وَعَاشَ ٨٥ سَنَةً وَمَاتَ سَنَةَ ٢٦٦ هـ.
And it is known to the critics what enters into the narrations of Hammad ibn Salamah after his confusion—his stepson, the notorious forger, and Abu al-Mihzam, his shaykh, who is abandoned (matruk) according to the scholars of jarh, and al-Ahwazi is among his narrators. And what connection does Ibn Shuja' have to this hadith with such a disconnected chain? And from the strange transgression is what Ibn 'Adi says: that he used to forge these hadith and insert them into the books of the hadith scholars to expose them, so they would narrate them with sound inner selves. Because Ibn Shuja' was neither a servant nor a stepson to any narrator such that it could be imagined that he inserted something among the books of any of them. So this critic, the commoner, the ungrammatical, did not know the extent of Ibn Shuja's knowledge, his religiosity, his dignity, his modesty, and his prestige in his time, until he spoke words that cannot be invalidated along with him. So, do you think the narrator remains acceptable in narration after something has been inserted into his books and he learned it and narrated it? If the critic does not prove into whose books Ibn Shuja' inserted, what he inserted, and how he inserted, nothing saves him from this fall when the fall occurs—the fact that he narrates them from a commoner like him like flocks of birds flying one after another. May the curse of Allah be upon the liars. Yes, Ibn Shuja' has some irregularity in the issue of the Qur'an, like Ibn al-Madini, about whom al-Bukhari said: 'I never considered myself small before anyone as I considered myself small before Ibn al-Madini,' and like Ibn 'Ulayyah, about whom Ahmad said: 'I missed Hammad ibn Zaid, so Allah compensated me with Ibn 'Ulayyah.' And he is not unique in this irregularity, and there is scope for examination in the issue according to the meaning they intended. And whoever reviews the book 'Al-Radd 'ala al-Jahmiyyah' by 'Uthman al-Darimi will see what they were criticizing him for, which those who err in exaggeration—sons of their aunts' sisters—consider, may Allah forgive them and us by His favor and generosity. Al-Dhahabi said in 'Siyar al-Nubala'' under the biography of Ibn Shuja': 'One of the luminaries, he heard from Ibn 'Ulayyah, Waki', Abu Usamah and their peers, and he took the variant readings (al-huruf) from Yahya ibn Adam and fiqh from al-Hasan ibn Ziyad. He was one of the seas of knowledge, and he was devoted to worship, night prayer, and recitation. He wrote the book 'Al-Manask' in more than sixty parts, and 'Kitab Tashih al-Athar' and others. He lived 85 years and died in the year 266 AH.'
Source pages p. 275

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the accusation made by Ibn 'Adi against the narrator Ibn Shuja' that he forged hadith and inserted them into the books of hadith scholars. Ibn Asakir begins by noting that critics are aware of the problems in the narrations of Hammad ibn Salamah after his confusion, and that his stepson was a known forger, and his shaykh Abu al-Mihzam is abandoned, but he questions what connection Ibn Shuja' has to this hadith with a disconnected chain. He then attacks Ibn 'Adi's claim as a strange transgression, arguing that Ibn Shuja' was never a servant or stepson to any narrator, so it is impossible for him to have inserted forgeries into their books. The critic is described as ignorant and ungrammatical, not knowing Ibn Shuja's true status. Ibn Asakir then poses a rhetorical question: if a narrator is accused of having had forgeries inserted into his books, does he still remain acceptable? He answers that unless the accuser proves exactly whose books were tampered with and how, the accusation is baseless. He then acknowledges that Ibn Shuja' had some irregularity (shudhudh) in the issue of the Qur'an, comparing him to other great scholars like Ibn al-Madini and Ibn 'Ulayyah, who were also respected despite some differences. He recommends reading 'Uthman al-Darimi's refutation of the Jahmiyyah to understand what was actually criticized. Finally, he quotes al-Dhahabi's high praise of Ibn Shuja' as a scholar and pious worshipper, establishing his reliability.

Key Terms
جرح وتعديل
وضع الحديث
شذوذ
ابن شجاع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: An accusation of hadith forgery is automatically accepted and taints the narrator's reliability, even without evidence.
Correction: Ibn Asakir shows that accusations must be proven with specific details; otherwise, they are baseless and do not affect the narrator's acceptance.

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Asakir, what is the main problem with Ibn 'Adi's accusation that Ibn Shuja' inserted forged hadith into scholars' books?
A) Ibn Shuja' was too young to have forged hadith
B) Ibn Shuja' had no access to the scholars' books because he was not a servant or stepson
C) Ibn 'Adi was a known liar
D) The hadith in question are all authentic

Key Takeaway
Ibn Asakir's defense illustrates that in hadith criticism, accusations require concrete evidence, and the character and knowledge of the accused must be considered.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear a rumor about someone's character, do you immediately accept it, or do you demand evidence like Ibn Asakir demanded? Think of a time you believed an accusation without proof. What would it take for you to change that pattern?
?
Ibn Asakir defended Ibn Shuja' despite his irregularity on the Qur'an. Can you respect and defend someone's overall character even if you disagree with them on a particular issue? When have you failed to do so, and what can you learn from this example?
?
The accuser was described as ignorant and ungrammatical. Do you ever speak about others beyond your depth, acting as if your knowledge gives you the right to judge? Repent today for any unjust speech you have made.

Refuting the Jewish Convert Story
Ibn Asakir refutes a fabricated story and provides counter-evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ibn Asakir uses evidence from a family endowment to disprove a fabricated claim about al-Ash'ari's lineage.

Core Idea
A fabricated story collapses when tested against established facts.

In this passage, Ibn Asakir confronts a slanderous tale that Abu Bishr, an ancestor of al-Ash'ari, was a Jew who converted. He dismisses it as baseless and then provides a concrete counterexample: the story of Bishr ibn al-Husayn, who benefited from a family endowment. This shows that the lineage was recognized and sound.

Guided Path
1
Read the claim and dismissal
Read the opening where Ibn Asakir introduces the story and immediately calls it a fabrication from unknown sources.
Notice his strong language: 'fabricated story from unknown fabricators'.
2
Identify the counter-evidence
Find the reference to Bishr ibn al-Husayn and the endowment (waqf) set up by his grandfather Bilal.
This is the key proof that the family lineage was accepted.
3
Follow the logical reasoning
Read the conditional statement: 'if his lineage had this defect, the produce would not have been given to him.' Understand the logical implication.
This is a classic argument from consequences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما حكايته النكرة عن بعض شيوخ البصرة من أن أبا بشر كان يهودياً فأسلم على يدي بعض الأشعريين، فحكاية مفتر عن مجاهيل مفترين ما حكى أن أحدا نفاه عن أبي موسى الأشعرى غير هذا الجاهل المتحامل المفترى، وكيف تجاسر لارعاه الله على هذه الكذبة وهو لا يعرف في الشرق والغرب إلا بهذه التشبة وقد تقدمت حكاية بندار بن الحسين في أنه كان يا كل فمن غلة ضيعة وقفها جده بلال أفتبين بتلك الحكاية وغيرها أن دعواه في نفی نسبه زور أو أن قوله محال إذلو كان في نسبه هذه العلة لم يرفع إليه من وقف بلال الغلة ولو لم يكن أبو الحسن صحيح النسب لا نتزعت منه الضيعة بذلك السبب. واستشهاده على ذلك بالبيت الشعر الذي قيل في سالف الدهر : وما كنـــــــــى عن أبيه إلا وثـ استشهاد يدل على جهله بالمعاني وكيف سكت عن البيت الأول وآتى بالثاني وإنما قيل : سألته عن أبيــــــــــه فقال جدى شعيب وما كني عن أبيه إلا وثـ وما كنى من نسب الاشعرى إلى إسماعيل أو إسحق عن أبي بشر ولا عنى فيه ما أراده الأهوازي في سر ولا جهر ولكن اقتصر مرة على ذكر الاسم لما من الغنية وأتى مرة أخرى في تعريفه بذكر الكنية، وما هذا إلا بمنزلة قولنا أبو بكر بن أبي قحافة تارةً وتارةً عبد الله بن عثمان فقد اتضح جهل الأهوازي في هذا من كل وجه بحمد الله، وبان وأنه كان غير بالاسماء والاصطلاحات حين لم يفرق بين الكنى وبين الكنايات.
As for his reprehensible tale from some shaykhs of Basra that Abu Bishr was a Jew and then converted to Islam at the hands of some Ash'aris, it is a fabricated story from unknown fabricators. No one has ever narrated that anyone denied his lineage to Abu Musa al-Ash'ari except this ignorant, biased, and slanderous man. How dare he — may Allah not protect him — tell this lie, while he is not known in the East or West except by this attribution? The story of Bishr ibn al-Husayn has already been mentioned, that he used to eat from the produce of an estate endowed by his grandfather Bilal. That story and others make it clear that his claim to deny his lineage is false, or his statement is impossible. For if his lineage had this defect, the produce of Bilal's endowment would not have been given to him, and if Abu al-Hasan had not been of sound lineage, the estate would have been taken from him for that reason. And his citing as evidence the line of poetry that was said in ancient times: 'He did not use a kunyah from his father except...' — this citation shows his ignorance of meanings. How could he ignore the first line and bring the second? Rather, it was said: 'I asked him about his father, and he said: my grandfather Shu'ayb, and he did not use a kunyah from his father except...' And he did not use a kunyah from the lineage of al-Ash'ari to Isma'il or Ishaq from Abu Bishr, nor did he mean in it what al-Ahwazi intended, in secret or openly. Rather, he sometimes confined himself to mentioning the name [؟], and other times he defined him by mentioning the kunyah. This is no different from our saying 'Abu Bakr ibn Abi Quhafah' one time and 'Abdullah ibn 'Uthman' another time. So the ignorance of al-Ahwazi in this matter has become clear from every angle, praise be to Allah, and it is evident that he was not [؟] with names and terminologies, as he did not distinguish between kunyahs and allusions.
Source pages p. 278

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of two specific claims. First, Ibn Asakir dismisses the story that Abu Bishr (the ancestor) was a Jew who converted at the hands of an Ash'ari. He calls it a fabricated story from unknown fabricators, noting that no one else has ever made this claim. He then brings a counter-argument: the story of Bishr ibn al-Husayn, who used to eat from the produce of an estate endowed by his grandfather Bilal. This shows that the family had a sound lineage; otherwise, the endowment would not have been given to them. So the claim that the lineage is defective is false. Second, Ibn Asakir critiques the misuse of a poetic line as evidence. The critic al-Ahwazi quoted a line that says 'He did not use a kunyah from his father except...' but ignored the first line which gives the context: 'I asked him about his father, and he said: my grandfather Shu'ayb, and he did not use a kunyah from his father except...' The point is that the poet did not use a kunyah from his actual father but from his grandfather. The critic misapplied this to al-Ash'ari, claiming that since he is known as 'Abu al-Hasan' and not by a father's name from his lineage, his lineage is suspect. But Ibn Asakir explains that al-Ash'ari was sometimes called by his name and sometimes by his kunyah, just as Abu Bakr is called by both his name (Abdullah ibn 'Uthman) and his kunyah (Abu Bakr ibn Abi Quhafah). The critic's failure to distinguish between 'kunyah' (agnomen) and 'kinayah' (allusion) shows his ignorance of terminology.

Key Terms
يهودي
حكاية
وقف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Abu Bishr was a Jew who converted to Islam.
Correction: The story is fabricated; the family lineage is sound as evidenced by the waqf story.

Checkpoint
What evidence does Ibn Asakir use to refute the claim that Abu al-Hasan's lineage is defective?
A) A line of poetry that proves his nobility
B) The story of Bishr ibn al-Husayn and the endowment of his grandfather Bilal
C) A consensus of the scholars of Basra
D) The fact that al-Ash'ari was known by his name only

Key Takeaway
Ibn Asakir uses the survival of a family endowment as concrete evidence that the lineage was sound, disproving the fabricated Jewish convert story.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear a scandalous story about someone's past, do you instinctively accept it or do you seek evidence? Where in your life do you need to be more cautious about believing unverified claims?
?
Ibn Asakir used a family endowment as concrete proof. What tangible evidence exists in your life that confirms your identity or the truth of your beliefs? How does that affect your confidence in them?
?
If a fabricated story about you were spread, would you have clear evidence to defend your reputation? What kind of legacy are you building now that could serve as proof of your character?

Refuting the Poetic Misinterpretation
Ibn Asakir critiques the misuse of a poetic line and explains the correct meaning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ibn Asakir exposes al-Ahwazi's ignorance by showing the correct context of a poetic line and the distinction between kunyah and kinayah.

Core Idea
A verse taken out of context can mislead; but full context reveals the truth.

Here, Ibn Asakir attacks al-Ahwazi's misuse of a poetic line as evidence. Al-Ahwazi quoted only part of the poem, claiming it showed al-Ash'ari's lineage was suspect. Ibn Asakir provides the missing first line and explains the poet's actual meaning, then draws an analogy with the names of Abu Bakr to show the critic's confusion between 'kunyah' and 'kinayah'.

Guided Path
1
Read the poetic citation and critique
Read the passage where Ibn Asakir presents the poetic line and immediately states it shows ignorance.
Notice the partial line with ellipsis—OCR issue, but the meaning is clear.
2
Find the correct context
Look for the full two lines: 'I asked him about his father, and he said: my grandfather Shu'ayb, and he did not use a kunyah from his father except...'
This shows the poet used a kunyah from the grandfather, not the father.
3
Understand the analogy
Read the analogy with Abu Bakr, who is called both by his name and his kunyah.
This illustrates that using a kunyah from a grandfather does not imply a defect.
4
Grasp the terminological distinction
Note the final line: al-Ahwazi failed to distinguish between 'kunyah' (agnomen) and 'kinayah' (allusion).
This is the core of the refutation: ignorance of terminology.

?

?

Refuting Claims Against al-Ash'ari's Orthodoxy and Fame
Ibn Asakir refutes the claim that al-Ash'ari only attracted ignorant followers and was obscure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ibn Asakir defends al-Ash'ari's Sunni orthodoxy and refutes the claim that he was followed only by ignoramuses and was obscure.

Core Idea
Al-Ash'ari's Sunni identity is a reality, his followers were scholars, and his fame contradicts claims of obscurity.

In this passage, Ibn Asakir responds to a critic who dismissed al-Ash'ari as a claimant to Sunnism who attracted only the ignorant and remained obscure. Ibn Asakir dismantles each accusation with clear reasoning and evidence, turning the critic's own words against him. As you read, notice how the rebuttal progresses from affirming truth, to listing the elite followers, to exposing logical inconsistency.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the rhythm, then the English translation. Note the three distinct points Ibn Asakir addresses.
The critic's statements are quoted and then refuted.
2
Identify the three refutations
Locate where Ibn Asakir counters: (1) al-Ash'ari's Sunni identity, (2) the quality of his followers, (3) the contradiction about fame.
Each refutation begins with 'As for his statement...'
3
Analyze the evidence for each refutation
For (1) Ibn Asakir says it's reality witnessed by the knowledgeable. For (2) he says only scholars followed him. For (3) he shows the critic contradicts himself.
The phrase 'stars of the cities' and 'imams of the times' emphasizes scholarly status.
4
Understand the rhetorical technique
Ibn Asakir uses the critic's own statement about fame spreading to refute the later claim of obscurity, highlighting the contradiction.
This is a powerful argument from inconsistency.
5
Reflect on the lesson's significance
Consider how this defense establishes al-Ash'ari's credentials within Sunni orthodoxy and silences detractors.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قوله ( وادعى أنه من أهل السنة . فليس ذلك دعوى بل حقيقة يشهد بصحتها كل ذى علم وتقوى . وقوله ( فمال إليه طائفة جهال . فذلك أيضاً منه كما سبق محال ما مال إلى قوله إلا العلماء ولا أتبعه إلا الفقهاء فإن أصحابه نجوم الأمصار وأتباعه أئمة الاعصار وقد تقدم ذكر جماعة من مشاهير أتباعه وتسمية أئمة من أصحابه وأشياعه ممن لا يسابق في فضل ولا يجارى ولا يشك في علمه ولا يتمارى . وقوله ( فشاع أمره وذاع في الآفاق ذكره . ينقض قوله فيما بعد أنه لم يزل مخمولاً غير مقبول في بلاد الإسلام وتناقض القول غير مستبدع من مثله من الجهال الطعام
As for his statement, 'And he claimed to be from Ahl al-Sunnah' — this is not a claim, but a reality, the truth of which every person of knowledge and piety attests. And his statement, 'So a group of ignoramuses inclined towards him' — that too from him is, as previously, impossible. None inclined to his statement except the scholars, and none followed him except the jurists. Indeed, his companions are the stars of the cities, and his followers are the imams of the times. Mention has already been made of a group of his famous followers and the naming of imams among his companions and partisans, who are not surpassed in virtue, nor rivaled, nor is there any doubt in their knowledge, nor any dispute. And his statement, 'His matter spread and his mention spread throughout the horizons' — contradicts his subsequent statement that he never ceased to be obscure and unaccepted in the lands of Islam. Contradiction in speech is not surprising from someone like him among the ignorant commoners [؟].
Source pages p. 279

Commentary
This passage is a defense of Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari's Sunni orthodoxy and refutation of claims that he was followed only by the ignorant and was obscure. Ibn Asakir responds point by point to the critic's statements. First, regarding the claim that al-Ash'ari 'claimed' to be from Ahl al-Sunnah, Ibn Asakir asserts that this is not a mere claim but a reality witnessed by every knowledgeable and pious person. Second, to the claim that only a group of ignorant people followed him, Ibn Asakir says the opposite is true: only scholars and jurists followed him. He describes his followers as 'stars of the cities' and 'imams of the times', and notes that a list of famous followers was already provided earlier, who are unmatched in virtue and knowledge. Third, the critic's own statement that al-Ash'ari's fame spread everywhere contradicts his later claim that al-Ash'ari remained obscure and unaccepted in Islamic lands. Ibn Asakir points out this contradiction, saying it is not surprising from an ignorant person like the critic.

Key Terms
أهل السنة
جهال
أئمة
شهرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-Ash'ari was a marginal figure followed only by the uneducated and remained obscure.
Correction: His Sunni identity was widely acknowledged, his followers were leading scholars and jurists, and his fame was widespread.

Checkpoint
How does Ibn Asakir refute the claim that only ignoramuses followed al-Ash'ari?
A) By saying that the critic himself was an ignoramus
B) By stating that only scholars and jurists followed him
C) By listing the names of ignorant followers
D) By denying that anyone followed him

Key Takeaway
Ibn Asakir defends al-Ash'ari by affirming his Sunni reality, the scholarly elite of his followers, and exposing the critic's self-contradiction on fame versus obscurity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Ibn Asakir declares that al-Ash'ari's being from Ahl al-Sunnah is 'a reality, the truth of which every person of knowledge and piety attests.' Do you live your faith in such a way that knowledgeable and pious people would attest to your belonging to the truth? If not, what one change can you make today to align yourself more closely with the scholars and their way?
?
The critic belittled al-Ash'ari by saying only ignoramuses followed him. Ibn Asakir countered that only scholars and jurists followed him. Who are you following? Are your teachers and influences people of solid knowledge and piety, or are they popular but shallow? Take a moment to evaluate your primary sources of religious guidance.
?
The critic contradicted himself: acknowledging al-Ash'ari's fame while claiming he was obscure. Are there contradictions in your own life—professing one thing with your mouth but living another? Identify one area where your actions contradict your beliefs and commit to resolving it today.
