Refuting al-Ahwazi's Attack on Genealogies
Ibn Asakir refutes al-Ahwazi's attack on Arab genealogies and his ignorance of kunyas.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ibn Asakir refutes al-Ahwazi's attack on Arab genealogies by exposing his misinterpretation of poetry and his ignorance of kunyas.

Core Idea
Misunderstanding a satirical poem led al-Ahwazi to falsely attack Arab genealogies.

Ibn Asakir presents a fascinating example of how misunderstanding a single piece of poetry can lead to a major theological error. Al-Ahwazi, a critic of Ash'ari theology, attacked Arab genealogies based on a poem he thought praised a Nabati lineage expert, but the poem was actually satirical. Ibn Asakir also exposes al-Ahwazi's ignorance of kunyas and his association with an unreliable scribe.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to understand the flow of Ibn Asakir's refutation.
Pay attention to the two-line poem and how Ibn Asakir interprets it.
2
Identify the two refutation strategies
Notice that Ibn Asakir first uses the poem to show al-Ahwazi's misunderstanding, then points to his ignorance of kunyas through the example of Naja and Abu Bishr.
These two examples together expose al-Ahwazi's general incompetence.
3
Reflect on the lesson's significance
Consider how this refutation defends Sunni tradition against sectarian attacks that are based on ignorance.
Think about the importance of correctly understanding sources before criticizing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما طعن الخوزى فى أنساب العرب إلا من الأمر النادر العجب وكأنه فيما أتاه من نفيه من المين عنى بهذين البيتين : وماذا بمصر من المضحكات ولكنه ضحك كالبكا بصير بها نبطى من أهل السواد يخلص أنساب أهل الفلا ولعل الأهوازي سمع هذين البيتين قديما ولم يكن بمراد قائلهما ومقصوده عليما فظن أنهما قيلا على وجه المدح فشرع في الطعن في الأنساب والقدح ولم يعرف المراد بهذا الشعر ، كما لم يفكر في معنى ما سبق منه من الهذر وهذا الفضل فى كنية أبي بشر وجد في نسخة نجا وما أدراك من نجا ، هو الذى لا يمتد إلى إضافته إلى ذوى الفهم بالعلم رجا و نسخته التي بخطه لا يصح ما فيها إذ لا خط له ولا هجا وكان له إلى صحبة الاهوازي لما بينهما من المناسبة في الجهل التجا
And al-Ahwazi's attack on the genealogies of the Arabs is only among the rare and astonishing matters, as if he, in what he brought of his denial from falsehood, intended these two lines: 'And what in Egypt among laughable things, but it is laughter like weeping; a Nabati from the people of the Sawad sees with insight, he extracts the genealogies of the desert people.' Perhaps al-Ahwazi had heard these two lines long ago and was not knowledgeable about the intent and purpose of their speaker, so he thought they were said as praise, so he began attacking genealogies and reviling, and he did not know the meaning of this poetry, just as he did not reflect on the meaning of the nonsense that preceded from him. And this merit regarding the kunya of Abu Bishr was found in a copy of Naja—and what will make you know what Naja is?—he who cannot extend his addition to people of understanding with hope of knowledge, and his copy in his own handwriting is not sound in what it contains, for he has no handwriting nor poetry, and he had [resorted] to the companionship of al-Ahwazi because of the suitability between them in ignorance, he transgressed.
Source pages p. 279

Commentary
This passage refutes al-Ahwazi's attack on Arab genealogies by exposing his ignorance. Ibn Asakir states that al-Ahwazi's attack is a rare and astonishing matter, because he misunderstood a satirical poem that actually mocks a Nabati (non-Arab) who pretends to trace Arab lineages. Al-Ahwazi thought the poem was praise and used it as a basis to criticize Arab genealogies. Ibn Asakir then comments on al-Ahwazi's ignorance of the meanings of kunyas (teknonymics), citing a manuscript issue involving the kunya 'Abu Bishr' and a person named Naja. The argument highlights al-Ahwazi's general incompetence in names and technical terms, his manuscript being unreliable, and his natural affinity for ignorance that led him to associate with al-Ahwazi.

Key Terms
الخوزى (al-Ahwazi)
الأنساب (genealogies)
الكنى (kunyas)
النبطى (Nabati)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-Ahwazi believed the poem was genuine praise, justifying his attack on Arab genealogies.
Correction: The poem was satirical, mocking the Nabati who presumes to trace Arab lineages. The phrase 'ضحك كالبكا' (laughter like weeping) indicates sarcasm, not praise.

Checkpoint
Why did al-Ahwazi mistakenly attack Arab genealogies?
A) He had reliable sources from Egypt
B) He misinterpreted a satirical poem as praise
C) He followed the opinion of the Mu'tazila
D) He wanted to defend the Nabati

Key Takeaway
Ibn Asakir refutes al-Ahwazi by demonstrating his fundamental ignorance: he misread a satirical poem as praise and lacked basic knowledge of kunyas.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear a criticism of a belief you hold, do you immediately defend it, or do you first examine whether the critic actually understands what they are attacking? How would your response change if you realized they were operating from a complete misinterpretation, like al-Ahwazi?
?
Al-Ahwazi relied on a copyist named Naja who was known for incompetence. Who are the 'Najas' in your own life—people or sources you trust without verifying their reliability? What would it cost you to check them?
?
The poem's phrase 'laughter like weeping' is a warning about misplaced certainty. Where in your own heart are you laughing confidently at something that should move you to tears? What would it take to see the truth?

Refutation of Three Slanders Against Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari
Ibn Asakir refutes three specific slanders about al-Ash'ari's orthodoxy, followers, and fame.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ibn Asakir refutes three false claims about Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari, demonstrating his true Sunni status, the caliber of his followers, and the inconsistency of his detractor's accusations.

Core Idea
Ibn Asakir exposes the contradictions of al-Ahwazi's slander.

In this passage, Ibn Asakir continues his defense of Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari by directly refuting three specific allegations made by his critic al-Ahwazi. Each claim is met with evidence from the scholarly consensus and the stature of al-Ash'ari's followers, culminating in a pointed observation about the very inconsistency of the critic's own statements.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the full Arabic passage once without pausing. Then read the English translation. Notice the three distinct refutations as they appear.
The passage is short but dense; each refutation builds on the previous.
2
Identify the three claims and their refutations
List each claim al-Ahwazi made and how Ibn Asakir responds. Pay attention to the evidence he provides for each point.
The third refutation uses a logical contradiction technique.
3
Reflect on the method of refutation
Consider how Ibn Asakir uses testimony of scholars, description of followers, and self-contradiction to defend al-Ash'ari. Why is this method effective?
This shows the confidence of the Sunni position against baseless attacks.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قوله ( وادعى أنه من أهل السنة . فليس ذلك دعوى بل حقيقة يشهد بصحتها كل ذى علم وتقوى . وقوله ( فمال إليه طائفة جهال . فذلك أيضاً منه كما سبق محال ما مال إلى قوله إلا العلماء ولا أتبعه إلا الفقهاء فإن أصحابه نجوم الأمصار وأتباعه أئمة الاعصار وقد تقدم ذكر جماعة من مشاهير أتباعه وتسمية أئمة من أصحابه وأشياعه ممن لا يسابق في فضل ولا يجارى ولا يشك في علمه ولا يتمارى . وقوله ( فشاع أمره وذاع في الآفاق ذكره . ينقض قوله فيما بعد أنه لم يزل مخمولاً غير مقبول في بلاد الإسلام وتناقض القول غير مستبدع مثله من الجهال الطعام
And as for his statement: 'And he claimed to be from Ahl al-Sunnah' — that is not a claim but a truth, the correctness of which every possessor of knowledge and piety testifies. And his statement: 'So a group of ignorant people inclined towards him' — that too is from him, as previously, impossible; none inclined to his statement except the scholars, and none followed him except the jurists, for his companions are the stars of the cities and his followers are the imams of the ages, and a group of his famous followers have been previously mentioned, and the naming of imams among his companions and partisans who cannot be surpassed in virtue nor rivaled, nor doubted in their knowledge nor disputed. And his statement: 'So his affair became widespread and his mention spread in the horizons' — that contradicts his subsequent statement that he remained obscure and unacceptable in the lands of Islam, and contradiction in speech is not something novel from the likes of him among the ignorant of the people.
Source pages p. 279

Commentary
This passage refutes three false statements made by al-Ahwazi. First, al-Ahwazi claimed that Abu al-Hasan claimed to be Sunni. Ibn Asakir responds that this is not a claim but an attested truth witnessed by every knowledgeable and pious person. Second, al-Ahwazi claimed that only ignorant people followed him. Ibn Asakir refutes this by stating that only scholars and jurists followed him, describing his companions as 'stars of the cities' and 'imams of the ages', and mentions that he has already listed many of them. Third, al-Ahwazi claimed that Abu al-Hasan's fame spread, which contradicts his other claim that Abu al-Hasan remained obscure and unacceptable. Ibn Asakir notes that such contradiction is expected from ignorant people like al-Ahwazi.

Key Terms
أهل السنة (Ahl al-Sunnah)
علماء (scholars)
فقهاء (jurists)
مخمول (obscure)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari was not sincerely Sunni, his followers were ignorant, and he was obscure.
Correction: Ibn Asakir shows that al-Ash'ari's Sunnism is affirmed by all scholars, his followers were the elite jurists and scholars, and his fame is attested by the very critic's own admission.

Checkpoint
How does Ibn Asakir refute al-Ahwazi's claim that Abu al-Hasan's followers were ignorant?
A) He says they were all merchants
B) He states that only scholars and jurists followed him
C) He admits some were ignorant but not all
D) He ignores the claim

Key Takeaway
Ibn Asakir decisively refutes three slanders by citing the testimony of scholars, the eminence of al-Ash'ari's followers, and the internal contradiction in his accuser's own words.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear criticism of a scholar or teacher, do you immediately accept it, or do you check for contradictions in the critic's own words? How often do you fall into the trap of believing inconsistent accusations without verifying?
?
This lesson shows that the quality of a teacher's followers is evidence of his truthfulness. Who are your scholars? Are they people of knowledge and piety, or are they those who follow without understanding? What does your choice of teachers say about your own commitment to truth?
?
Ibn Asakir refutes slanders by appealing to the testimony of scholars. In your own life, when your faith or character is attacked, do you rely on the witness of righteous people to defend you? Have you built relationships with such witnesses?

Ibn Asakir's Refutation: The Evidence from Abu al-Hasan's Writings
Ibn Asakir refutes al-Ahwazi by citing Abu al-Hasan's writings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ibn Asakir uses Abu al-Hasan's own writings to refute the claim that he supported innovation and Mu'tazilite thought.

Core Idea
Abu al-Hasan's books reveal his enmity towards Mu'tazila, disproving the claim.

Ibn Asakir responds to al-Ahwazi's accusation by pointing to the clear evidence in Abu al-Hasan's works. He argues that anyone who reads his writings will see the intense hostility of the Mu'tazila towards him, thus proving the accusation false. This lesson focuses on that key argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Focus on the part where Ibn Asakir argues from Abu al-Hasan's writings.
Notice that Ibn Asakir first quotes the accusation, then immediately dismisses it as similar to previous lies.
2
Identify the evidence
Find the specific evidence Ibn Asakir uses: the intense hatred of the Mu'tazila and zanadiqa for Abu al-Hasan and his companions.
This is the core of his refutation.
3
Reflect on the method
Consider why Ibn Asakir selects this evidence. How does hatred from opponents prove that Abu al-Hasan was not supporting them?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقوله ( أنه كان ينصر البدعة ويدخل على الناس قول المعتزلة والزنادقة. فمن جنس ما تقدم ذكرنا له من أقواله السخيفة وتقولاته غير الصادقة فإن من وقف على ما ذكره أبو الحسن في تواليفه وكتبه وعرف شدة بغض المعتزلة والزنادقة له ولصحبه تيقن كذب الاهوازي فيما قاله وتبين له تحامله وتحقق أبطاله ، وما زعم أنه حكاه عن أهل البصرة فالذي صدق في حكايته فعن معتزلة أو سالمية أمثاله ، وما لم يكذب هو فيه فإنمارواه فإنما رواه عن مجهولين أو كذابين أشكاله
And his statement: 'That he used to support innovation and introduce to the people the speech of the Mu'tazila and the zanadiqa.' That is of the same kind as what we have previously mentioned of his foolish statements and false sayings. For whoever examines what Abu al-Hasan mentioned in his works and books and knows the intense hatred of the Mu'tazila and the zanadiqa for him and his companions will be certain of the lie of al-Ahwazi in what he said, and his bias will become clear to him, and his falsehood will be confirmed. And what he claimed that he narrated from the people of Basra, what is true in his narration is from the Mu'tazila or the Salimiyya like him, and what he did not lie about in it, his narration is only from unknown people or liars of his ilk.
Source pages p. 280

Commentary
This passage refutes al-Ahwazi's claim that Abu al-Hasan supported innovation and introduced Mu'tazilite and zandaqa (heresy) speech. Ibn Asakir argues that this claim is of the same kind as al-Ahwazi's previous foolish statements. He states that anyone who examines Abu al-Hasan's writings will see the intense hatred that the Mu'tazila and zanadiqa had for him and his companions, thus proving al-Ahwazi's lie and bias. Furthermore, Ibn Asakir remarks that if al-Ahwazi's report about the people of Basra is true, it only comes from Mu'tazila or Salimiyya like him; otherwise, his narrations are from unknown people or liars similar to himself.

Key Terms
المعتزلة (Mu'tazila)
الزنادقة (zanadiqa)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari was influenced by Mu'tazilite thought or supported their innovations.
Correction: Ibn Asakir argues that the intense hatred of the Mu'tazila towards Abu al-Hasan and his companions, as evident from his writings, proves he did not support them but rather opposed them.

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Asakir, what proves that Abu al-Hasan did not support Mu'tazilite ideas?
A) He wrote books criticizing them
B) The Mu'tazila hated him and his companions
C) He was a Sunni imam
D) Al-Ahwazi was a known liar

Key Takeaway
The enmity of the Mu'tazila towards Abu al-Hasan demonstrates his opposition to their beliefs, refuting the claim that he supported innovation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear an accusation against a scholar or a fellow Muslim, do you rush to judge based on the accusation alone, or do you first seek out their own words and the testimony of their opponents? This lesson shows that the enemies' hatred can be evidence of truth. How will you change your habit of judgment starting today?
?
Think of a time when someone falsely accused you. Did you have 'writings' or evidence that could clear you? Now, consider that Abu al-Hasan's own books proved his innocence. Are you documenting your own stance and principles clearly so that if you are slandered, the truth can be known?
?
If the hatred of the Mu'tazila was proof that Abu al-Hasan was not one of them, then who are the people who oppose you because of your faith? Could their opposition actually be a sign that you are on the right path? Are you willing to accept that opposition as confirmation, or do you try to appease them?

Ibn Asakir's Refutation: Discrediting al-Ahwazi's Sources
Ibn Asakir criticizes al-Ahwazi's sources, revealing them to be unreliable.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ibn Asakir discredits al-Ahwazi's narrations by identifying his unreliable sources.

Core Idea
Al-Ahwazi's only reliable reports come from Mu'tazila like himself, otherwise from unnamed liars.

After dismissing the main accusation, Ibn Asakir further dismantles al-Ahwazi's credibility by examining the sources of his claims. He argues that any truth in his narration comes from Mu'tazila or Salimiyya associates, and the rest from unknown or lying individuals. This lesson explores that source criticism.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage

2
Identify the categories
Ibn Asakir divides al-Ahwazi's narrations into two categories: what is true (from Mu'tazila/Salimiyya) and what is not lied about (from unknown/liars). Understand each category.
The first category still comes from biased sources; the second from unreliable ones.
3
Analyze the rhetorical strategy
See how Ibn Asakir undermines al-Ahwazi's authority by showing his sources are either partisan or unknown. This is a powerful method of refutation.

Refuting Slander with Quranic Ethics
Ibn Asakir refutes al-Ahwazi's lies and cites Quran on not insulting idols.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ibn Asakir uses Quranic principles to refute false accusations and establish the prohibition of insulting scholars.

Core Idea
If God forbids insulting idols, how can we insult righteous scholars?

In this passage, Ibn Asakir dismantles al-Ahwazi's claim that insulting al-Ash'ari is a righteous act. He exposes the weakness of al-Ahwazi's sources and then turns to the Quran, showing that Islam prohibits insult and backbiting, even against false gods. This lesson will guide you through his refutation and the powerful Quranic argument at its heart.

Guided Path
1
Identify the accusation and response
Read the first part where Ibn Asakir dismisses al-Ahwazi's claim as foolish. Notice how he refers to previous refutations and the hatred of Mu'tazila for al-Ash'ari.
Ibn Asakir is building credibility by pointing to al-Ash'ari's known enemies.
2
Examine the criticism of sources
Ibn Asakir attacks al-Ahwazi's sources, saying he relies on Mu'tazila, Salimiyya, unknown persons, or liars. Understand that this is a common scholarly method to discredit a report.
This step shows the importance of chain of transmission (isnad) in Islamic scholarship.
3
Reflect on the rhetorical questions
Read the series of questions asking where God commanded insult, cursing, backbiting. These force the reader to think: is there any Quranic basis for such behavior?
The questions are meant to be answered 'Nowhere'.
4
Analyze the Quranic verse and analogy
Ibn Asakir cites Quran 6:108 which forbids insulting idols. Then he draws an analogy: if wood and stones are protected, how can living scholars be insulted? This is the climax.
This is a powerful argument from the greater to the lesser (qiyas al-awla).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقوله ( أنه كان ينصر البدعة ويدخل على الناس قول المعتزلة والزنادقة. فمن جنس ما تقدم ذكرنا له من أقواله السخيفة وتقولاته غير الصادقة فإن من وقف على ما ذكره أبو الحسن في تواليفه وكتبه وعرف شدة بغض المعتزلة والزنادقة له ولصحبه تيقن كذب الاهوازي فيما قاله وتبين له تحامله وتحقق أبطاله ، وما زعم أنه حكاه عن أهل البصرة فالذي صدق في حكايته فعن معتزلة أو سالمية أمثاله ، وما لم يكذب هو فيه فإنمارواه فإنما رواه عن مجهولين أو كذابين أشكاله ومن العجائب أنه اعتقد الاتيان بذمه قربة وزعم أنه ذكر ما ذكر من شتمه حسبة ورغب إلى الله عز وجل أن يجعله لوجهه خالصا وإلى مرضاته واصلا فتبينوا ما قال تجدوا عقله ناقصا ، وقوله باطلا متى تعبدنا الله بالسب والشتم وأين أمرنا بالتفرغ للثلب والذم وهل سوغ لنا الاشتغال باللعن أو ندبنا إلى استعمال الغيبة والطعن أو أثنى في كتابه على المستعملين للهمز أو مدح العيابين المشتغلين باللمز فتأملوا رحمكم الله القرآن العظيم وتفهموا الآيات والذكر الحكيم تجدوا فيه النهى ذلك كله ، والامر بالاعراض عن أكـثـره وأقله وقد نهى ذو الجلال والاكرام عن سب ما يعبد من دونه من الاصنام فقال : ولا تسبوا الذين يدعون من دون الله فيسبوا الله عدواً بغير علم ) فمن تفرغ لسب عباد الله سهواً بغير فهم ، وإذا كان الله قد نهى عن سب الأخشاب والأحجار فكيف يبيح لكم سب العلماء الاخيار .
And his saying that he used to support innovation and introduce to people the speech of the Mu'tazila and the heretics. This is of the same kind as what we previously mentioned of his foolish statements and false claims. For whoever examines what Abu al-Hasan mentioned in his compilations and books and knows the intensity of the Mu'tazila's and heretics' hatred for him and his companions will be certain of al-Ahwazi's lie in what he said, and it becomes clear to him his bias and the invalidity of his claim. And what he claimed that he reported from the people of Basra - what is true in his report is from Mu'tazila or Salimiyya like him, and what he did not fabricate therein, he only narrated it from unknown persons or liars like him. Among the wonders is that he thought that bringing censure upon him was an act of drawing near to God, and he claimed that he mentioned what he mentioned of insulting him as a service, and he desired from God Almighty that He make it purely for His face and reaching His pleasure. So investigate what he said, you will find his intellect deficient and his statement false. When did God command us to insult and revile? Where were we ordered to devote ourselves to slander and blame? Did He permit us to occupy ourselves with cursing, or did He encourage us to use backbiting and fault-finding? Did He praise in His Book those who use slander, or commend the fault-finders occupied with reproach? So ponder, may God have mercy on you, the Great Quran and comprehend the verses and the wise Reminder; you will find therein prohibition of all that, and the command to turn away from most of it and little of it. Indeed, the Lord of Majesty and Honor prohibited insulting what is worshipped besides Him, the idols, saying: "And do not insult those they invoke besides God, lest they insult God in enmity without knowledge." So whoever devotes himself to insulting the servants of God heedlessly without understanding — and if God has prohibited insulting wood and stones, how can He permit you to insult the righteous scholars?
Source pages p. 280

Commentary
This passage is a refutation and argumentative attack on al-Ahwazi. Ibn Asakir begins by categorizing al-Ahwazi's accusation that al-Ash'ari supported innovation as belonging to the same kind of foolish statements previously mentioned. He then provides a proof: anyone who examines al-Ash'ari's works and knows the hatred of the Mu'tazila and heretics for him will recognize al-Ahwazi's lie. The author further argues that al-Ahwazi's reports are either from his own ilk (Mu'tazila or Salimiyya) or from unknown or lying narrators. He then criticizes al-Ahwazi's claim that insulting al-Ash'ari is a righteous act. Ibn Asakir proceeds to ask a series of rhetorical questions: where did God command insult? Where did He permit cursing, backbiting, or fault-finding? He invites the reader to consult the Quran, which prohibits all that and commands turning away from it. He then cites the verse "And do not insult those they invoke besides God..." as evidence. The argument culminates in a logical conclusion: if God forbade insulting lifeless idols, how then can it be permissible to insult righteous scholars?

Key Terms
البدعة
المعتزلة
الزنادقة
السب
الشتم
الغيبة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some assume that insulting or slandering a scholar can be a religious duty or a meritorious act if one believes the scholar is wrong.
Correction: Ibn Asakir refutes this by showing that the Quran prohibits insult and backbiting absolutely, and even forbids insulting false gods. Thus, insulting a believer—especially a scholar—is far more prohibited.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of the Quranic verse cited in this passage?
A) It permits insulting scholars if they are wrong.
B) It prohibits insulting idols to prevent retaliation against God.
C) It commands insulting polytheists.
D) It encourages backbiting for religious reasons.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Asakir refutes slander by exposing unreliable sources and invoking the Quranic principle that even insulting false gods is forbidden, making insulting scholars an even graver sin.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have I convinced myself that harsh words against someone are justified because I am defending the truth? How does the Quranic prohibition against insulting idols challenge that assumption? What would it mean to actually follow this verse in my disagreements?
?
Do I ever examine the source of my anger or criticism, or do I assume it is righteous like al-Ahwazi did? Could it be that my 'service to God' is actually a cover for personal animosity? What would it take to honestly face that possibility?
?
If God forbids insulting a lifeless stone, what excuse do I have for insulting a living soul—even one I consider misguided? How can I reconcile my words with the command to turn away from slander? What practical step will I take today to guard my tongue?

Refuting the Objection on Insulting Scholars
Ibn Asakir refutes the objection that insulting prevents retaliation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Asakir's refutation of the claim that insulting scholars is prohibited only to prevent retaliation against God.

Core Idea
Insulting scholars is inherently prohibited, not just to prevent backlash.

Ibn Asakir presents an objection that the prohibition of insulting scholars is merely to prevent a chain of retaliation that could lead to insulting God. He then dismisses this reasoning as intellectually unacceptable and a sign of great ignorance. This lesson examines his argument and its refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
First, read the Arabic text and its literal translation to grasp the objection and response in their original form.
Notice the phrase 'فإن قيل' which introduces a hypothetical objection.
2
Identify the objector's argument
The objector claims the prohibition is so that insulting al-Ash'ari does not cause retaliation against God. Trace the chain: insult to al-Ash'ari -> listener retaliates by insulting the objector's own Imam -> possibly leads to insulting God.
The objector mentions ignorant pseudo-Sunnis who perpetuate this cycle with a saying about insulting Ali and Abu Bakr.
3
Understand Ibn Asakir's refutation
Ibn Asakir does not engage in a lengthy rebuttal; he simply states that this reasoning is 'a plan no person of intellect would approve' and 'a lapse indicating great ignorance.' Realize that the refutation is strong by dismissing the premise as foolish.
The brevity highlights the absurdity of the objection.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this refutation matters: it affirms that insulting scholars is forbidden in itself, regardless of potential consequences.
This guards against reducing ethical rules to mere utilitarian calculations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل ( إن المعنى في النهي عن هذا السب لئلا يكون سبباً لسب الرب فربما سمع سب الأهوازى لهذا الإمام بعض من يراه بعين الاعظام فيقابل سبه بسب إمامه ويتكلم فيه عند الغضب بمثل كلامه ويحمله على ذلك السب فرط حمية أو إظهار صلابة في معتقده وعصبية ويجتنب مقابلة السيئة بالجسنة اقتداء بقول بعض جهال المتسننة سبوا علياً كما سبوا عتيقكم كفر بكفر وإيمان بإيمان فيكون حينئذ سبباً لسب صاحب مذهبه لأن ذلك أنما جرى من قائله خطأ بتسببه وهذه خطة لا يرتضيها ذو عقل وسقطة تنبى عن عظيم جهل
For if it is said: The meaning in the prohibition of this insult is so that it does not become a cause for insulting the Lord. For perhaps someone who hears al-Ahwazi's insult of this Imam, while he sees him with veneration, would counter his insult with an insult of his own Imam, and speak about him in anger with similar speech, and that insult drives him due to excessive zeal or showing firmness in his creed and partisanship, and he avoids repaying evil with good, following the saying of some ignorant pseudo-Sunnis: 'Insult Ali as they insulted your freed one (Abu Bakr), disbelief for disbelief, faith for faith.' Then it becomes a cause for insulting the follower of his school, because that action was committed by its speaker mistakenly due to his provocation. And this is a plan that no person of intellect would approve, and a lapse that indicates great ignorance.
Source pages p. 280

Commentary
This passage contains an objection and its answer. The hypothetical objector claims that the prohibition of insulting scholars is meant to prevent causing retaliation against God Himself: someone who venerates al-Ash'ari might respond to al-Ahwazi's insult by insulting al-Ahwazi's own Imam (likely Abu Bakr or another figure). The objector suggests that this exchange could escalate to insulting God. The objector then notes that some ignorant pseudo-Sunnis say: 'Insult Ali as they insulted your freed one (Abu Bakr), disbelief for disbelief, faith for faith' — implying a cycle of retaliation. Ibn Asakir responds that this reasoning is unacceptable: no intelligent person would approve of this plan, and it is a lapse indicating great ignorance. The author does not elaborate further here; the refutation is implicit in the dismissal of the objector's reasoning as foolish.

Key Terms
السب
اللعن
الحمية
العصبية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that insulting scholars is only prohibited because it could lead to worse consequences, such as blasphemy, and that if such consequences are avoided, the insult might be permissible.
Correction: Ibn Asakir refutes this by dismissing the utilitarian reasoning as foolish; the prohibition stands regardless of consequences, implying the act is inherently wrong.

Checkpoint
According to the objector's argument, why might insulting al-Ash'ari be prohibited?
A) Because al-Ash'ari is a saint.
B) Because it could lead to retaliation against God.
C) Because it is always a sin.
D) Because al-Ahwazi is lying.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Asakir rejects the reduction of the prohibition of insulting scholars to mere prevention of retaliation, calling such reasoning foolish and ignorant.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever justified a sin by telling yourself it prevents a greater harm? How does this lesson expose the danger of such consequentialist reasoning in matters of Islamic law? What will you do the next time you feel tempted to trade a small evil for a supposed good?
?
When you hear someone insulting a scholar you respect, does anger rise in you that tempts you to retaliate in kind? After reading this passage, what is the real source of that anger, and how will you discipline your heart to respond with wisdom instead of zeal?
?
Is there a part of you that secretly agrees with the 'ignorant pseudo-Sunnis' who think evil can be repaid with evil? How will you uproot this mindset and instead follow the command to repel evil with that which is better?

Condemning the Cursing of Scholars: The Prophetic Example
Ibn Asakir condemns cursing scholars via prophetic example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ibn Asakir uses the Prophet's refusal to curse polytheists as a powerful argument against cursing Muslim scholars.

Core Idea
The Prophet was sent as mercy, not a curser—even for polytheists.

This passage from Ibn Asakir's Al-Tabyin is a sharp rebuke of those who curse Muslim scholars, specifically targeting al-Ahwazi for his attacks on al-Ash'ari. Ibn Asakir anchors his argument in a hadith where the Prophet refused to curse polytheists, establishing a principle that makes cursing believers even more reprehensible.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith and its chain
Begin by reading the hadith where the Prophet (peace be upon him) refuses to curse the polytheists, stating he was sent as a mercy. Note the chain of narrators that Ibn Asakir provides for authenticity.
The chain shows the reliability of the hadith.
2
Understand the core argument
Ibn Asakir argues: if the Prophet did not curse polytheists, how can anyone dare to curse Muslim scholars who are devoted to worship? This is the central analogy.
The argument is a fortiori (qiyas in aqwa).
3
Consider the Quranic verse
Read the citation of Quran 5:91 about Satan sowing enmity. Ibn Asakir links cursing to Satanic influence.
This adds a spiritual dimension to the condemnation.
4
Reflect on the consequences
Notice how Ibn Asakir describes the culprit as misguided and ignorant. He emphasizes that cursing scholars leads to perdition.
The lesson ends with a warning about following one's whim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أجمعين من لعن من سئل في لعنه من المشركين بالله مع كونهم بالشرك بالله متمسكين ، وذلك فيما أخبرنا أبو عبد الله محمد بن الفضل الفراوي وأبو المظفر عبد المنعم بن عبد الكريم القشيرى قالا أنا أبو سعد محمد بن عبد الرحمن بن محمد الجنزروذى قال أنا أبو عمرو محمد بن أحمد بن الحيرى وأخبرنا الشيخ أبو عبد الله الحسين بن عبد الملك بن الحسين الخلال حمدان باصبهان قال أنا أبو القسم إبراهيم بن منصور بن إبراهيم السلمي قال أنا أبو بكر محمد بن إبراهيم بن على بن المقرى قالا أنا أحمد بن على بن المثنى الموصلى قال ثنا محمد بن عباد المكي قال ثنا مروان زاد ابن المقرى ابن معاوية عن يريد زاد ابن المقرى ابن كيسان عن أبي حازم عن أبي هريرة قال قيل وفي حديث ابن حمدان قلت يا رسول الله ادع على المشركين قال ( إني لم أبعث لعانا إنما بعثت رحمة ) رواه مسلم في صحيحه عن محمد بن عباد فإذا كان رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم لم ير لعن المشركين فكيف استجاز الاهوازى فى دينه لعن العلماء المتنسكين فلا بهدى الله عز وجل اهتدى ، ولا بنبيه صلى الله عليه وسلم اقتدی بل عمى عن سلوك طريق الهدى وألقى نفسه فيما يفضى به إلى الردى ، افتراه حسب أن يترك سدى حين أخطأ فيما قاله في الأشعرى واعتدى وأتبع مراد الشيطان الرجيم في لعن المسلمين حين تجنب الكف عنهم والاغضاء ، قال الله عز وجل في كتابه الكريم : إنما يريد الشيطان أن يوقع بينكم العداوة والبغضاء ) فمن أضل سبيلا ممن أتبع هواه واستفرغ في ذم العلماء بالباطل قواه، ولم يرقب فيهم إلا ولا ذمة ولم يرع له محلاً ولا حرمة ومن أعظم جهلاً ممن فرغ نفسه للطعن والوقيعة في الأكابر والأعيان من علماء الشريعة ولو أنعم فيما قاله تفكرا لعلم أنه أتى أمراً مستنكرا ولو كان بأحكام الشريعة خبيرا لتيقن أنه ارتكب حوباً كبيرا ، و كفاه تركاً للحق واجتنابا عده ما ذكره من البهتان في حقه احتسابا فما أسعده أن سلم مما ذكره رأساً برأس وانفلت منه كفافاً بغير بأس ، وأنى له بالسلامة وقد خرج من حد الاستقامة ولو قال بدل وأصل موصلا لكان قد ذكر لفظاً مستعملا لكن عجمته تحمله على تجنب الصواب وجهالته تقتضى له تعسفه في الخطاب.
All of them, from one who was asked about cursing the polytheists despite their adherence to shirk with Allah, and that in what informed us Abu Abdullah Muhammad ibn al-Fadl al-Farawi and Abu al-Muzaffar Abd al-Mun'im ibn Abd al-Karim al-Qushayri said: Abu Sa'd Muhammad ibn Abd al-Rahman ibn Muhammad al-Janzarudhi informed us, he said: Abu Amr Muhammad ibn Ahmad ibn al-Hayri informed us, and informed us the Shaykh Abu Abdullah al-Husayn ibn Abd al-Malik ibn al-Husayn al-Khallal Hamdan in Isfahan, he said: Abu al-Qasim Ibrahim ibn Mansur ibn Ibrahim al-Sulami informed us, he said: Abu Bakr Muhammad ibn Ibrahim ibn Ali ibn al-Muqri' informed us, they said: Ahmad ibn Ali ibn al-Muthanna al-Mawsili informed us, he said: Muhammad ibn Abbad al-Makki narrated to us, he said: Marwan (Ibn al-Muqri' added: Ibn Mu'awiya) narrated to us from Yazid (Ibn al-Muqri' added: Ibn Kaysan) from Abu Hazim from Abu Hurayra, he said: it was said—and in the hadith of Ibn Hamdan: I said, O Messenger of Allah, invoke against the polytheists. He said, 'I was not sent as a curser; I was only sent as a mercy.' Muslim narrated it in his Sahih from Muhammad ibn Abbad. So if the Messenger of Allah, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, did not see fit to curse the polytheists, how then did al-Ahwazi permit himself in his religion to curse the ascetic scholars? He has not been guided by the guidance of Allah Almighty, nor has he followed his Prophet, may Allah bless him and grant him peace; rather he became blind to the path of guidance and cast himself into what leads to perdition. Did he think that he would be left neglected when he erred in what he said about al-Ash'ari and transgressed, and followed the desire of the accursed Satan in cursing the Muslims while avoiding refraining from them and overlooking? Allah Almighty said in His Noble Book: 'Satan only desires to cause enmity and hatred between you.' So who is more astray than one who follows his whim and exhausts his powers in slandering the scholars with falsehood, and does not regard any covenant or protection toward them, nor observes any standing or sanctity? And who is more ignorant than one who devotes himself to disparaging and attacking the great and eminent figures among the scholars of the Sharia? If he had reflected on what he said, he would know that he has committed a monstrous thing; and if he were knowledgeable in the rulings of the Sharia, he would be certain that he has committed a great sin. It suffices him as abandonment of truth and avoidance that he counts the slander he mentioned against him as a meritorious act. How fortunate would he have been if he had been safe from what he mentioned head for head and escaped from it unscathed without harm. But how can he have safety when he has left the bounds of uprightness? And if he had said instead 'a connected origin' he would have mentioned a usable expression, but his non-Arabness leads him to avoid correctness and his ignorance demands his harshness in speech.
Source pages p. 281

Commentary
This passage is a rebuke and condemnation of a person (likely al-Ahwazi) who cursed the scholar Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari. Ibn Asakir begins by citing a hadith in which the Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him) refused to curse the polytheists, stating that he was sent as a mercy, not a curser. The chain of narration is provided for authenticity. The author then argues: if the Prophet himself did not curse the polytheists, how can this person dare to curse Muslim scholars who are devout and ascetic? He accuses the culprit of being misguided, disobedient to the Prophet, and following Satan. He quotes Quran 5:91 about Satan sowing enmity. He then denounces the culprit's ignorance in slandering the great scholars, emphasizing that if he reflected, he would realize his grave error. The passage ends with a criticism of the culprit's language, saying that even his speech is corrupt due to his non-Arab background and ignorance.

Key Terms
لعن
المشركين
رحمة
الاهوازى
الأشعرى
الشيطان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think it is permissible to curse misguided Muslims as a form of religious zeal.
Correction: The Prophet did not even curse polytheists; cursing a Muslim scholar is a greater sin and contradicts the prophetic mission of mercy.

Checkpoint
What argument does Ibn Asakir use to condemn the one who cursed al-Ash'ari?
A) That al-Ash'ari was a companion of the Prophet.
B) That the Prophet himself did not curse polytheists, so cursing a Muslim scholar is worse.
C) That the one who cursed is a polytheist himself.
D) That cursing is forbidden in all circumstances.

Key Takeaway
Cursing Muslims contradicts the prophetic mission of mercy and reflects ignorance of the Sharia.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever spoken ill of a scholar or religious figure, even in your heart? What does this hadith teach you about your own tendency to judge and condemn? How will you guard your tongue from now on?
?
The Prophet was sent as mercy, not a curser. Are you more like the Prophet or like al-Ahwazi when you encounter someone you disagree with? What is one specific step you will take to embody mercy in your speech today?
?
If you were to examine your own speech, where do you find yourself cursing or slandering others—even those you consider misguided? How does this passage expose your own need for repentance and change?

Refuting Doubts about al-Ash'ari's Repentance
Ibn Asakir refutes claims that al-Ash'ari's repentance from Mu'tazilism was insincere.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ibn Asakir defends the sincerity of al-Ash'ari's repentance from Mu'tazilism by refuting the claims of al-Wazzan and establishing criteria for accepting repentance.

Core Idea
True repentance is proven by regret, piety, and public witness—not by miracles.

In this passage, Ibn Asakir addresses a slanderous claim that al-Ash'ari's return to Sunnism was insincere. He dismantles the arguments of a critic named al-Wazzan and provides a clear standard for judging the authenticity of repentance. By the end, you will see how al-Ash'ari's life and works testify to his genuine transformation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the full translation to grasp the argument's flow.
Notice that Ibn Asakir addresses two distinct statements from al-Wazzan.
2
Identify al-Wazzan's objections
Find al-Wazzan's first objection (doubt about which statement to believe) and his second (no change in intellect, only miracles cause change). How does Ibn Asakir respond to each?
Ibn Asakir calls al-Wazzan ignorant and implies he is a Mu'tazilite.
3
Note the criteria for repentance
Ibn Asakir explains when repentance is doubted and when it is accepted. List the conditions for accepting a claim of repentance.
Regret, piety, truthfulness, and trustworthiness are key.
4
Consider the evidence for al-Ash'ari
Ibn Asakir lists proofs of al-Ash'ari's sincerity: his debates with al-Jubba'i, his writings, his refutations. How do these support the criteria?
5
Reflect on the dismissal of other reports
Why does Ibn Asakir dismiss the reports from al-Askari and al-Hamrani? What does this tell about source criticism?
He shows the first is actually a merit, and the second is from an unreliable narrator praising an unknown man.

وقول الوزان الذي حكى عنه أنه ادعى أنه رجع عن الاعتزال فلا أدرى أصدقه في القول الأول أو الثاني فقول جاهل أو متجاهل لا يصح معناه عند أهل الفهم بالمعاني لأن أحداً من الطوائف لم يكذب أنه كان معتزليا وإنما ينكر من لا يعتد بانكاره رجوعه بعد الاعتزال سنيا ، وقول الوزان لم يتغير على شيء من عقله ولم يبعث الله نبياً تظهر على يديه المعجزات فيدع الخلق ما هم عليه ضرورة ، فقول جاهل لم يؤته الله في دينه بصيرة لأنه زعم أن تغير العقل سبب الرجوع عن الاعتزال وهذا يشعر أن هذا الوزان كان من المعتزلة الضلال ، ودعواء أن أحداً لا يترك ما كان عليه إلا عند ظهور المعجز من المحال فكم من منتقل من مذهب إلى غيره لقوة النظر والاستدلال أو لارشاد من الحق سبحانه والهام أورؤيا وعظ بها رائيها فى منام أو شدة بحث عن الحق على عمر الأيام وهذه المعاني كلها موجودة في حق هذا الإمام وإنما يشك في توبة التائب إذا لم يوجد منه غير مجرد الدعوى ولم يكن عند اختبار حاله من أهل الدين ولا من ذوى التقوى ، فأما إذا اقترن منه بدعوى التوبة ظهور الأسف على ما أسلف من الحوبة وكان المظهر للتوبة ذا ديانة موصوفا عند الخلق بصدق وأمانة لم يكن للشك في صحة توبته مجال فمن قال غير هذا فقوله محال ، ولا شك أن دين أبي الحسن رحمه الله متين وتبرأه من مذهب الاعتزال ظاهر مبين ومناظراته لشيخهم الجبائي مشهورة، واستظهاراته عليه في الجدل مذكورة وقمعه لغيره من شيوخهم معروف شائع وقطعه لهم في المناظرة منتشر ذائع ، وتواليفه في الرد على أهل التعطيل كثيرة وفضيحة أهل الأهواء بما أظهر من عوار مذهبهم كبيرة فكيف يزعم أنه أظهر غير ما أبطن أو أضمر ضد ما أعلن وما حكاه عن أبي محمد الحسن بن محمد العسكرى، فقد بينت أن ذلك من مناقبه ضد ما تصوره المفترى وما حكاه عن أبي عبد الله الحمراني الذي يثني عليه فمما لا يصغى ذو لب إليه ثناؤه على الحمراني غير مقبول، وكيف يقبل ثناء مثله على رجل مجهول أنه قال إن الناس اختلفوا في سبب رجوعه فقال أصحابه بأن له وهو
And the statement of al-Wazzan which is narrated from him that he claimed that he (al-Ash'ari) returned from Mu'tazilism—I do not know whether to believe him in the first statement or the second—that is the statement of an ignorant person or one pretending ignorance, its meaning is not valid with people of understanding in meanings, because none of the sects denied that he was a Mu'tazilite; only one whose denial is not countenanced denies his return after Mu'tazilism to Sunnism. And the statement of al-Wazzan: 'His intellect did not change in anything, and Allah did not send a prophet upon whose hands miracles appear to compel creatures to leave what they are upon'—that is the statement of an ignorant person whom Allah has not given insight in his religion, because he claimed that change of intellect is the cause of return from Mu'tazilism, and this indicates that this al-Wazzan was from the misguided Mu'tazila. And the claim that no one leaves what he was upon except at the appearance of a miracle is impossible; how many have transferred from one school to another due to strength of reflection and inference, or guidance from the Truth Almighty and inspiration, or a vision by which its seer was admonished in a dream, or intense search for truth over the course of days? And all these meanings exist in the case of this Imam. And one only doubts the repentance of the repentant if nothing other than mere claim is found from him, and when tested his state is not from the people of religion nor from those possessing piety. But if, along with his claim of repentance, there appears regret for what he previously committed of sin, and the one manifesting repentance is a person of religiosity, described among people as having truthfulness and trustworthiness, then there is no room for doubt in the validity of his repentance. Whoever says otherwise, his statement is impossible. And there is no doubt that the religion of Abu al-Hasan, may Allah have mercy on him, is firm, and his disavowal of the Mu'tazilite school is clear and manifest, and his debates with their master al-Jubba'i are famous, and his refutations of him in disputation are recorded, and his suppression of others among their shaykhs is well-known and widespread, and his rout of them in debate is disseminated and common, and his writings in refutation of the people of ta'til (denial of attributes) are many, and his exposure of the people of desires by revealing the defects of their school is great. So how can one claim that he manifested other than what he concealed, or harbored the opposite of what he declared? And what he narrated from Abu Muhammad al-Hasan ibn Muhammad al-Askari—I have shown that that is among his merits, contrary to what the slanderer imagined. And what he narrated from Abu Abdullah al-Hamrani, whom he praises—no intelligent person pays attention to that; his praise of al-Hamrani is unacceptable. How can the praise of someone like him be accepted for an unknown man? He said: 'Indeed people differed concerning the cause of his return, and his companions said that he had...' and it (the text) ends.
Source pages p. 282

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of doubts about the sincerity of Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari's repentance from Mu'tazilism. Ibn Asakir begins by addressing the claim of a person called al-Wazzan, who allegedly said he did not know whether to believe al-Ash'ari's earlier or later statement. The author dismisses this as ignorance, since no one denies that al-Ash'ari was a Mu'tazilite, but some reject his return to Sunnism. He then refutes al-Wazzan's argument that al-Ash'ari's intellect did not change and that only miracles cause people to abandon their beliefs. Ibn Asakir says this is false, as many people change schools through reasoning, divine guidance, inspiration, dreams, or persistent search for truth—all of which apply to al-Ash'ari. He then explains the criteria for accepting a claim of repentance: it must be accompanied by regret and the person must be known for honesty and piety. Al-Ash'ari meets these criteria, as evidenced by his firm faith, his debates with the Mu'tazilite leader al-Jubba'i, his writings against those who deny divine attributes, and his exposure of the errors of heretical sects. The author concludes by dismissing the reports from al-Askari and al-Hamrani as either irrelevant or from unreliable sources, and the text ends mid-sentence with a statement about differing opinions on the cause of al-Ash'ari's return.

Key Terms
الاعتزال (Mu'tazilism)
التوبة (repentance)
الوزان (al-Wazzan)
الجبائي (al-Jubba'i)
المعجز (miracle)
النظر والاستدلال (reflection and inference)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that conversion from one theological school requires a miracle or that repentance is only valid if accompanied by a supernatural sign.
Correction: Ibn Asakir shows that change can occur through reason, guidance, inspiration, dreams, or search for truth, and repentance is validated by regret and continued good character.

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Asakir, what is a valid reason for someone to leave a theological school like Mu'tazilism?
A) Only a miracle that compels the person.
B) A change in intellect or mental state.
C) Strength of reflection, inference, or divine guidance.
D) The influence of political authorities.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Asakir defends al-Ash'ari's repentance by refuting ignorant objections, establishing rational criteria for accepting repentance, and demonstrating that al-Ash'ari's life and works provide overwhelming evidence of his sincerity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you were to examine your own heart, do you truly regret your past sins with a regret that changes your actions, or are you merely claiming repentance while continuing in the same path? What would those who know you best say about your truthfulness and piety?
?
When you see someone change their beliefs or lifestyle for the better, do you immediately suspect insincerity, demanding some miraculous sign before accepting their transformation? Or do you, like Ibn Asakir, look for evidence of genuine regret and virtue? Which pattern dominates your reactions?
?
Al-Ash'ari's repentance was proven by his debates, writings, and public disavowal of his former errors. What concrete evidence exists in your life that you have truly turned away from what displeases Allah? Can you point to a specific change in your words, actions, or priorities that would convince a skeptic?

Refuting allegations of worldly motive in al-Ash'ari's repentance
Ibn Asakir refutes two allegations against al-Ash'ari's sincerity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Asakir's refutation of the claims that al-Ash'ari's repentance was motivated by inheritance or status, and why sincerity is essential for credible guidance in creed.

Core Idea
True repentance in creed cannot be feigned; it is proven by asceticism and lasting impact.

Ibn Asakir presents a robust defense of Imam al-Ash'ari's sincerity in returning to Sunni orthodoxy. He addresses two specific allegations—that al-Ash'ari changed his doctrine for inheritance or status—and dismantles them using logical, ethical, and historical arguments. This passage also defends the integrity of al-Ash'ari's writings as guides for later generations.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the gist, then read the literal translation. Notice the structure: Ibn Asakir first states the two allegations, then refutes them, then concludes with the correct view.
The passage is a continuous refutation; pay attention to how he transitions between arguments.
2
Identify the two allegations
The passage mentions two reasons people gave for al-Ash'ari's repentance: inheritance and desire for status. Find these in the text and note how Ibn Asakir introduces them.
The inheritance claim is refuted with reference to al-Ash'ari's asceticism; the status claim is refuted by appealing to fear of God.
3
Analyze the refutations
Ibn Asakir refutes the inheritance claim by noting al-Ash'ari's detachment from worldly goods and by asking how a feigned repentance could produce sincere, guiding books. He refutes the status claim by arguing that a believer would not pretend in matters of religion. Understand these points and how they reinforce each other.
The refutation relies on both logical consistency and ethical principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحق فكان سبب نزوعه ، وقال آخرون مات له قريب من الذكور أو الاناث فتاب لئلا يمنعه الحاكم من الميراث ، وقال آخرون إنما فارق مذاهب المعتزلة لما لم يظفر عند العامة بسمو المنزلة فقد تقدم ذكر تقلل أبى الحسن وزهده وتبليغه باليسير من غلة وقف جده ، فقول من زعم أنه رجع لأخذ الميراث باطل من الجهات الثلاث وهب أنه أبدى ذلك في حق نفسه لغرض من الاغراض أو لنيل ما ناله من حطام الدنيا من الأعراض ، فكيف تسخو نفسه برجوع من يرجع عن بدعته التي هو يسرها ويعتقدها بالنظر في كتبه التي ألفها على مذهب أهل السنة ممن ينظر فيها بعده ويعتمدها ، ولا شك أنه قد استبصر بما ذكر فيها عالم من الناس وزالت عنهم بها ظلم الشكوك والالتباس. وقول من زعم أنه أظهر التوبة ليؤخذ عنه ويسمع ما يلقى إلى المتعلمين منه وتعلو منزلته عند العامة فذلك ما لا يصنعه من يؤمن بالبعث يوم القيامة كيف يستجيز مسلم أن يظهر ضد ما يبطن أو يضمر خلاف ما يبدى ويعلن ، لاسيما فيما يتعلق بالاعتقادات ويرجع إلى أصول الديانات فتعين حينئذ مما ذكر الحمراني القول الأول وبان أنه الصحيح الذي عليه المعول وهو أنه لما بان له الحق أتبعه وترك ما عداه وهو القول الذى نقول بـ في هذا المعنى ولا نتعداه.
The truth was the cause of his renunciation. Others said: a relative of his died, male or female, so he repented lest the ruler prevent him from inheritance. Others said: he only left the doctrines of the Mu'tazila because he did not attain high status among the common people. The mention of Abu al-Hasan's insignificance, his asceticism, and his contentment with little from the yield of his grandfather's endowment has preceded. So the statement of one who claims that he returned for inheritance is false from three aspects. Suppose he showed that regarding himself for some purpose or to obtain some worldly debris of ephemeral things, how could his soul consent to the return of one who recants from his innovation which he delights in and believes in, by looking at his books which he authored on the doctrine of Ahl al-Sunna, among those who look into them after him and rely on them? There is no doubt that many people have gained insight through what is mentioned in them, and the darkness of doubts and confusion has been removed from them. And the statement of one who claims that he showed repentance so that people would take from him and hear what he teaches to students, and his status would rise among the common people — that is not done by one who believes in the Resurrection on the Day of Judgment. How can a Muslim dare to show the opposite of what he conceals, or hide contrary to what he reveals and declares, especially regarding matters of belief and the foundations of religions? Thus it is determined from what al-Hamrani mentioned that the first opinion is the correct one upon which reliance is placed: that when the truth became clear to him, he followed it and abandoned what was other than it. That is the opinion we hold in this meaning and do not go beyond it.
Source pages p. 283

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of two allegations against Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari: that he repented from Mu'tazilism to secure an inheritance, or to gain status among the common people. The author first notes that earlier he had mentioned al-Ash'ari's asceticism and contentment with little, implying he was not worldly. Then he argues that the inheritance claim is false from three unspecified aspects (likely logical, textual, and historical). He then posits that even if al-Ash'ari had worldly motives, his books — which guide others to Sunni doctrine — would not have been written insincerely, as many have benefited from them. Next, he dismisses the status-seeking claim by appealing to the fear of God: a Muslim who believes in the afterlife would not publicly profess a belief contrary to his private conviction, especially in matters of religion. Therefore, the correct view is that al-Ash'ari sincerely recognized the truth and followed it.

Key Terms
توبة
بدعة
معتزلة
أهل السنة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think al-Ash'ari's repentance was insincere due to worldly motives like inheritance or status.
Correction: Ibn Asakir shows that his asceticism, the lasting guidance of his books, and the principle that a believer would not pretend in religion prove his sincerity.

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Asakir, why is the claim that al-Ash'ari repented for inheritance or status false?
A) Because al-Ash'ari never had any relatives die.
B) Because al-Ash'ari was already wealthy and did not need inheritance.
C) Because a sincere Muslim would not feign repentance in creed, especially given his asceticism and the guidance in his books.
D) Because the Mu'tazila were not powerful enough to deny inheritance.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Asakir establishes that al-Ash'ari's repentance was sincere because worldly motivations are incompatible with his asceticism and the lasting guidance of his books.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
What worldly attachments or desires might you be holding on to that, if discovered, could make others doubt the sincerity of your faith? Are you willing to abandon them as al-Ash'ari abandoned his former beliefs? Reflect and commit to one step you will take to detach from that attachment.
?
If your life's contributions were to be examined after your death, would they guide others to the truth as al-Ash'ari's books did, or would they lead people away from it? What specific change can you make today to ensure your legacy points others toward correct belief and practice?
?
The passage says a believer in the Resurrection would never show the opposite of what he conceals, especially in creed. Where in your own life do you see a gap between your outward actions and inward convictions? Will you take the difficult step to align them, starting with one area, no matter the cost?

Refuting a Fabricated Story about al-Ash'ari's Burial Statement
Ibn Asakir dismisses a fabricated story of al-Ash'ari declaring himself an unbeliever at a burial.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ibn Asakir deconstructs a fabricated story about al-Ash'ari based on legal, rational, and doctrinal inconsistencies.

Core Idea
Fabrications collapse under reason and doctrine.

Ibn Asakir continues exposing false narratives by examining a second story attributed to al-Ash'ari. This one claims that at a burial, al-Ash'ari said something indicating atheism. The author shows how the story violates Islamic law, common sense, and even the doctrines of the accusers themselves. Let's see his method.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the flow of the argument.
Pay attention to the emotional tone—Ibn Asakir uses strong dismissive language.
2
Identify the three layers of critique
Find where Ibn Asakir argues that the story is: (1) legally impermissible, (2) rationally absurd, (3) theologically self-contradictory.
He even uses the Mu'tazila's own doctrine against them.
3
Examine the narrator critique
Notice how Ibn Asakir discredits the narrator as unreliable and biased against the imams.
This shows the importance of transmitter criticism (jarh wa ta'dil).
4
Reflect on the broader lesson
Ask yourself: How does this refutation protect the integrity of Sunni scholarship against sectarian fabrications?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والحكاية الثانية التي حكاها عن الحمراني أيضاً فحكاية مثلها مما لا يستجاز في الشرع ولا يرضى مما عزاه إليه من القول عند تلقين الذي أدخل القبر لأنها حكاية جمع فيها حاكيها عنه الكذب والهجر ، وكيف يستحسن عاقل أن يقول مثل هذا القول عند دفن آدمی وهي حالة شديدة الهول أم كيف لم يشغله ما يراه من ظلمة القبر وضيق اللحد عن الاعتراف بفساد الدين وسوء العقد وهب أن الملحد لا يؤمن بالبعث أليس يوقن بالبلاء وطول المكث وكيف يعترف أنه ولد ملحدا والمعتزلة تقول أن كل مولود يخلق موحدا فهذه الحكاية لعمرى من الكذب البارد وإيراد مثلها يدل على العقل الفاسد ، ولأبي الحسن رحمه الله من الرد على أصناف الملاحدة والنقض لمقالات أصحاب العقائد الفاسدة والكشف عن تمويهات الفرق الجاحدة مما تقدم ذكره ، ما يدل على بطلان مثل هذه الكذبة الباردة ولو أراد الله به خيراً لم يحك مثل هذه الحكاية لأن فسادها ظاهر عند أهل الفهم والدراية ، وحاكيها مجهول العدالة عند أهل الرواية ومزكيه لا يكتفى بتزكيته لأنه ليس أهلاً للكفاية لتناهيه في العداوة للأئمة فوق النهاية وتجاوزه فيما يظهره من البغض لهم للحد والغاية.
And the second story that he narrated from al-Hamrani as well — a story like it is not permissible in the Law nor acceptable. It is what he attributed to him of speech at the prompting of the one who entered the grave, because it is a story in which its narrator combined lies and nonsense. How can a rational person deem it good to say such a statement at the burial of a human, a state of severe terror? Or how is he not distracted by what he sees of the darkness of the grave and the narrowness of the tomb from admitting the corruption of religion and evil of belief? Suppose the atheist does not believe in Resurrection, does he not feel certain about affliction and long stay? And how can he admit that he was born an atheist while the Mu'tazila say that every newborn is created a monotheist? This story, by my life, is cold lying, and citing such indicates a corrupt mind. And Abu al-Hasan, may God have mercy on him, has refutations of various kinds of atheists, criticisms of the statements of those with corrupt beliefs, and exposure of the obfuscations of denying sects, as previously mentioned, which indicate the invalidity of such a cold lie. If God had intended good for him, he would not have narrated such a story because its corruption is apparent to people of understanding and knowledge. Its narrator is unknown for integrity among people of transmission, and his vouching for it is not sufficient because he is not qualified, due to his extreme enmity towards the imams beyond limit and his exceeding in the hatred he shows towards them to the utmost degree.
Source pages p. 283

Commentary
This passage refutes a second story attributed to al-Ash'ari via al-Hamrani, claiming that at a burial he said something indicating atheism. The author argues that such a statement is legally impermissible and rationally absurd. He points out that the terror of the grave would distract anyone from making false confessions. Even if the person were an atheist, they would not deny the reality of the grave's affliction. Furthermore, the Mu'tazila themselves believe every child is born a monotheist, so claiming to be born an atheist contradicts their own doctrine. Therefore, the story is a cold lie. The author adds that al-Ash'ari's own works refute atheism, and the narrator is unreliable due to known enmity towards the imams.

Key Terms
حكاية
ملحد
تلقين
قبر
معتزلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might accept any historical story about a scholar without examining its internal consistency or source reliability.
Correction: Ibn Asakir shows that even without external verification, a story can be refuted by demonstrating its contradictions with established law, reason, and known doctrines.

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Asakir, what is one reason the story about al-Ash'ari at the burial is false?
A) It contradicts the Qur'an.
B) The story claims al-Ash'ari said something at a burial that is both legally impermissible and rationally absurd, and it contradicts Mu'tazilite teaching that every child is born a monotheist.
C) The narrator is unknown, but the story might still be true.
D) The story was reported by a reliable source.

Key Takeaway
A fabricated historical report can be refuted by examining its consistency with law, reason, and known doctrines.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever accepted a negative report about someone's faith based on a single incident, ignoring their lifelong devotion? How does Ibn Asakir's method of refuting this fabricated story challenge you to verify what you hear about others, especially scholars?
?
The narrator of this story is described as having 'extreme enmity towards the imams.' Reflect: Is there anyone you oppose so strongly that you might be tempted to believe negative reports about them without scrutiny? How can you guard against that bias?
?
Ibn Asakir argues that even an atheist would be terrified at the grave and not deny religion. Does your own awareness of death and the grave affect your certainty in faith? What would you say if you were asked about your beliefs at the moment of burial?

Refuting the Denial of Repentance for Innovators
Ibn Asakir refutes al-Ahwazi's denial of accepting repentance from innovators using Qur'anic verses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ibn Asakir argues that the repentance of innovators is accepted, based on Qur'anic verses and the refutation of al-Ahwazi's misinterpretation.

Core Idea
Even innovators can repent; the Qur'an's doors of mercy are open until death.

Ibn Asakir continues his refutation of al-Ahwazi's extreme positions. Here he tackles the claim that innovators' repentance is not accepted, showing that the Qur'anic verses cited actually support the acceptance of repentance for those who turn back before death.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the point of contention
Understand that al-Ahwazi denies the acceptance of repentance from innovators (mubtadi'a). Ibn Asakir calls this denial far-fetched and impossible.
This sets up the problem: can innovators repent and be forgiven?
2
Examine Ibn Asakir's critique
Ibn Asakir argues that the verse al-Ahwazi uses actually refers to apostates who die in disbelief, not to those who repent. He criticizes al-Ahwazi for not considering the context of the verses.
Notice how he says 'if he had contemplated what is before and after the verses, he would have known'.
3
Reflect on the Qur'anic exception
The cited verses include an exception: 'Except for those who repent after that and correct themselves. Indeed, Allah is Forgiving and Merciful.' This proves that repentance is accepted from those who return to faith.
The exception shows that the threat of rejected repentance is not absolute.

وأما إنكار (الأهوازى قبول توبة المبتدعة فمن الانكارات البعيدة الممتنعة وقد سبق الكلام فى ذلك فى أول هذا الكتاب بما فيه غنية لذوى الفهم وأولى الألباب ، واحتجاجه بالآية غير صحيح في الاعتبار لأنها إنما عنى بها من ارتد ولحق بالكفار ولم يختم عمله بعمل المؤمنين الابرار ، بل مات على كفره وصار إلى النار ولو تأمل ما قبلها وبعدها من الآيات لعرف ذلك ولكنه ممن يكتم ما أنزل من الهدى والبينات ، قال الله عز وجل : ومن يبتغ غير الإسلام دينا فلن يقبل منه وهو في الآخرة من الخاسرين ، كيف يهدى الله قوماً كفروا بعد إيمانهم وشهدوا أن الرسول حق وجاءهم البينات والله لا يهدى القوم الظالمين ، أولئك جزاؤهم أن عليهم لعنة الله والملائكة والناس أجمعين ، خالدين فيها لا يخفف عنهم العذاب ولا هم ينظرون ، إلا الذين تابوا من بعد ذلك وأصلحوا فإن الله غفور رحيم إن الذين كفروا بعد إيمانهم ثم أزدادوا كفرا لن تقبل توبتهم وأولئك هم الضالون إن الذين كفروا وماتوا وهم كفار فلن يقبل من أحدهم ملء الأرض ذهبا ولو افتدى به أولئك لهم عذاب أليم وما لهم من ناصرين وقيل أنها نزلت في اليهود والنصارى فلا يحتج بها في حق موحد إلا الجهال بالتفسير الحيارى .
As for al-Ahwazi's denial of accepting the repentance of innovators, it is among the far-fetched and impossible denials. Speech on this has already preceded at the beginning of this book, sufficient for people of understanding and intellect. His argument by the verse is not correct in consideration because it only means one who apostatized and joined the disbelievers, and did not seal his deed with the deed of the righteous believers, but died in his disbelief and went to the Fire. If he had contemplated what is before and after the verses, he would have known that. But he is among those who conceal what was revealed of guidance and clear proofs. Allah Almighty said: 'Whoever seeks a religion other than Islam, it will never be accepted from him, and in the Hereafter he will be among the losers.' 'How shall Allah guide a people who disbelieved after their belief and bore witness that the Messenger is true and clear proofs came to them? And Allah does not guide the wrongdoing people.' 'Those — their recompense is that upon them is the curse of Allah, the angels, and all mankind.' 'Abiding eternally therein, the punishment will not be lightened for them, nor will they be reprieved.' 'Except for those who repent after that and correct themselves. Indeed, Allah is Forgiving and Merciful.' 'Indeed, those who disbelieved after their belief and then increased in disbelief — their repentance will never be accepted, and those are the astray.' 'Indeed, those who disbelieve and die while they are disbelievers — never would the full earth of gold be accepted from one of them, even if he offered it as ransom. Those will have a painful punishment, and they will have no helpers.' And it is said that it was revealed concerning the Jews and Christians, so it cannot be used as evidence against a monotheist except by the ignorant of interpretation, the bewildered.
Source pages p. 284

Commentary
This passage refutes al-Ahwazi's denial that the repentance of innovators (mubtadi'a) is accepted. The author states that this denial is far-fetched and that he has already addressed it earlier. He criticizes al-Ahwazi's use of Qur'anic verses, arguing that they apply to apostates who die in disbelief, not to those who repent. He then cites verses from Surah Al-Imran (3:85-91), showing that while rejection of Islam is not accepted, repentance is granted to those who return to faith after apostasy. The exception clause 'except those who repent' demonstrates that repentance is accepted. The verse about increased disbelief and death in unbelief refers to those who persist until death, not to those who repent earlier. Finally, he notes that some exegetes say these verses were revealed about Jews and Christians, so they do not apply to monotheists (Muslims) except by misinterpretation. Thus, repentance of Muslim innovators is accepted.

Key Terms
توبة
مبتدعة
ردة
آية
تفسير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the Qur'anic verses about rejected repentance apply to all who commit major sins or innovations, meaning their repentance is never accepted.
Correction: Ibn Asakir clarifies that the verses specifically refer to those who die in unbelief after apostasy, not to those who repent. The exception 'except those who repent' shows that repentance is accepted for those who turn back before death.

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Asakir, what does the Qur'anic exception 'except those who repent' in Surah Al-Imran indicate?
A) That repentance is never accepted from apostates.
B) That repentance is accepted from any innovator who repents before death.
C) That the verses are only about Jews and Christians, not Muslims.
D) That repentance is conditionally accepted only if the person was not an innovator.

Key Takeaway
Repentance is accepted from any innovator who repents before death, as per the Qur'anic exception.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever secretly harbored the belief that someone who commits a great sin or holds a serious innovation is beyond the reach of Allah's mercy? What does this passage say about that, and what will you now actively pray for them—or check your own heart first?
?
Ibn Asakir rebukes those who conceal the truth from clear proofs. Is there a truth in this lesson—or any other Islamic teaching—that you have avoided applying to your own life because it would require you to change an assumption or behavior? What is that truth, and what step will you take today to live by it?
?
If you were to die tonight, would you be certain that your repentance is accepted? This lesson shows that even apostates can repent before death—so what sin or doubt holds you back from full, immediate turning to Allah? Commit to a specific act of repentance before you sleep.
